As Inflation Signs Grow, Divergent Reactions --- Dow Gains More Than 110 Points Despite Worries of Rate Increase; Short Sellers, Boeing...
Wednesday's Markets
As Inflation Signs Grow, Divergent Reactions --- Dow Gains More Than 110 Points Despite Worries of
Rate Increase; Short Sellers, Boeing Likely Factors
By E.S. Browning
814 words
15 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS REBOUNDED strongly despite more signs of inflation, leaving investors debating whether it was a
one-day snapback or something more lasting.
After falling in seven of the eight previous sessions, the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 110.78 points,
or 1.03%, to 10816.92. It swung back to a gain of 0.9% for 2006, after sinking into a decline for the year on
Tuesday. It still is down 7.1% from the six-year high hit May 10.
It is common for stocks to stage brief rallies in the middle of longer-term declines, often fueled by bearish
investors known as short sellers when they pause to take profits. To make their bets, short sellers borrow
stock and sell it. They hope to profit by buying it back and returning it after a decline. So, to take profits, short
sellers must buy stocks, pushing them temporarily higher. The question is how much of the rally was caused
by short sellers, and how much by longer-term buying.
More than a third of the Dow's gain, or 40.24 points, came from a single stock, Boeing Co., which rose $5.03,
or 6.5%, to $82.01. Boeing won a big order, at the same time that rival Airbus announced delayed production
for its giant A380 jet.
Across the market, things that had been down were up, and vice versa. Several hard-hit foreign markets
rebounded. Japan recovered 0.6%, Russia 3%, Turkey almost 2%, Austria 1% and Colombia more than 6%.
India, China and Britain continued to fall, however.
Oil futures, which had been falling, rose 58 cents to $69.14 a barrel, although gold futures fell 20 cents to
$562.30 a troy ounce.
Treasury bonds, which had risen as money flowed to their relative safety, fell heavily. That was partly
because money flowed back to stocks and partly because of the inflation jitters. The dollar, which had been
surging, also pulled back.
Inflation worries worsened after a government report showed May consumer prices, excluding volatile food
and energy prices, rising more than expected. The gain was above the 2% annual level that Federal Reserve
officials have said marks the limit of their comfort zone. That reinforced expectations that the Fed will push
benchmark short-term interest rates up another quarter point at its policy meeting June 28 and 29. The
expectation of higher rates makes investors less eager to own existing bonds, with their lower rates.
Reinforcing the expectation of higher rates, Dallas Federal Reserve Bank President Richard Fisher called
current inflation expectations "unacceptable."
The inflation news also was bad for stocks because it reinforced fears that the Fed could raise interest
rates into the summer, further weighing on corporate profits, consumer spending and economic growth.
Ordinarily, that concern would have pushed stocks down. Its failure to do so suggested that the stock gains
were more a rebound from the recent declines than a reaction to the day's news.
Overall, almost as many stocks fell as rose on the New York Stock Exchange. But traders said they were
pleased to see stocks rally strongly at day's end, instead of fading as they so often have lately.
"This market has endured significant damage in the last two weeks, and the selling isn't disappearing," said
Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore money-management group T. Rowe Price. While there is no
guarantee that the declines won't resume, he said, "my own sense is that we are closer to having washed
through this."

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.52%, or 6.35 points, to 1230.04, still down 1.5% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.65%, or 13.53 points, to 2086.00, down 5.4% for 2006.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
0.67%, or 1.32 points, to 197.87.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.77 billion
shares; 1,757 stocks rose and 1,556 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 24/32, or $7.50 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 5.064%. The 30-year bond was down 1 7/32 to yield 5.100%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 115.06 yen, down from 115.30, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2593 from $1.2545.
(See related article: "Credit Markets: Consumer Data Sinks Bond Prices, In Break With Recent Calm As
Investors' Jitters Surface" -- WSJ June 15, 2006)

Interest-Rate Fears Drive World-Wide Slide --- U.S. Stocks Follow Global Retreat, Nudging Dow Index, Russell 2000 Into Negative Ground for...
Tuesday's Markets
Interest-Rate Fears Drive World-Wide Slide --- U.S. Stocks Follow Global Retreat, Nudging Dow Index,
Russell 2000 Into Negative Ground for the Year
By E.S. Browning
768 words
14 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INTEREST-RATE FEAR swept the stock market yet again, sending the Dow Jones Industrial Average to its
seventh decline in eight sessions and leaving it in the red for the year.
Following heavy declines in Asia and Europe, which carried over into Latin America, U.S. stocks gyrated
during the day, as some money managers advised clients to seek the relative safety of large U.S. stocks and
others decided to pull back from stocks in general.
Reflecting the newfound volatility, the Dow industrials swung almost 90 points twice in the last hour of regular
trading, going from a decline of about 84 points to a small gain for the day, and then back to a big decline.
The blue chips finished down 86.44 points, or 0.80%, at 10706.14, off 8% since hitting a six-year high on May
10. They are at a four-month low and are down 0.11% for 2006, their year-to-date decline since January.
Other U.S. indexes behaved similarly.
"It was a big-volume day and the market closed at the lows, all things that from a technical perspective or a
momentum perspective don't bode well for the market," said stock trader Michael Driscoll of Bear Stearns.
"People are saying, `Forget it, I want out of stocks. I feel more comfortable with bonds or real estate or
whatever.' "
Total trading in Big Board stocks was 3.26 billion shares, the second heaviest ever, after the 3.58 billion
shares that traded on Thursday.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index fell 1.5% to 672.72, down 14% since hitting a record high in May. Small
stocks had led the market until May, but the Russell now shows a decline of 0.1% for 2006.
The wave of selling began abroad, with benchmark indexes down 4% in Tokyo and India, 9% in Russia,
nearly 6% in Turkey, 5% in Austria, 2% in Germany and Britain, 9% in Colombia and 2% in Brazil.
Industrial commodity and precious-metals prices also plunged, with oil futures falling $1.80, or nearly 3%, to
$68.56 a barrel. Gold futures plunged $44.30, or 7% to $562.50 a troy ounce, the biggest one-day
percentage decline since 1991. Gold futures are down 22% since May 11, but still are up 9% for the year.
Some analysts pointed to the release of the May wholesale-price report, which showed core wholesale prices
-- excluding food and energy -- rising 0.3%, more than investors had hoped. In addition, May retail-sales
numbers came in sluggish.
The wholesale numbers heightened fears that May consumer-price data, due this morning, could come in
outside the Federal Reserve comfort zone for a third month. That would further increase the chances that the
Fed will continue pushing up benchmark short-term interest rates to cool the economy and hold down
inflation.
Higher rates hurt stocks by slowing economic growth, crimping corporate profits and consumer spending and
making money more expensive for investors to borrow. Higher rates also make alternative investments such
as money market funds more attractive. Especially nettlesome for investors, rates now appear to be moving
higher world-wide.
Investors also remain nervous about what Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke will say about rates in his speech to
the Economic Club of Chicago on Thursday. They are looking for some sign of hope, which would help
stanch the selling.

--- Page 10 ---
Page 10 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Reflecting the jitters, although Goldman Sachs Group reported its second-strongest quarterly profit ever, the
stock fell 4% on fears that the plunging market is already hurting financial firms' business.
The dollar continued its rebound, boosted by a flight to cash and by the fact that the currency becomes a
more attractive investment when U.S. interest rates rise.
Treasury bonds, a refuge in times of uncertainty, also advanced. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or
$1.25 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield down to 4.967%. The 30-year bond was up 5/32 to yield
5.014%.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.03%, or 12.71 points, to 1223.69, down 2% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index declined 0.90%, or 18.85 points, to 2072.47, off 6% for 2006.
(See related article: "World Stock Markets: As Some Markets Fall 20% or More, Investors Fret Continued
Selling Could Gain Momentum of Its Own" -- WSJ June 14, 2006)

Tumbling Markets May Be Reflection Of Strong Growth --- Investors Struggle to Adapt To Demise of Easy Money; Dow Gives Up 2006 Gains
Tumbling Markets May Be Reflection Of Strong Growth --- Investors Struggle to Adapt To Demise of
Easy Money; Dow Gives Up 2006 Gains
By Mark Whitehouse
1,465 words
14 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Global stock and commodity prices tumbled again, extending a rout that appears to defy -- but may simply
highlight -- the strong pace of economic growth in much of the world.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average of 30 major U.S. stocks lost 86.44 points, or 0.8%, to 10706.14,
surrendering the last of the gains it scored earlier this year. In Japan, the world's second-largest economy,
the Nikkei Stock Average shed 614.41, or 4.1%, to close at 14218.60, and other markets were down sharply.
Gold, which often moves in the opposite direction to stocks, also lost ground. The price of a troy ounce,
which had recently crossed the $700 mark, dropped $44.30, or 7.3%, to close at $562.50, and the price of
silver fell 13%.
Many economists now think the broad reversal in the prices of stocks, bonds and commodities like gold and
silver is less a warning signal of an economic downturn than a consequence of the ratcheting up of interest
rates by the world's central banks. Indeed, the central bankers' concern is the reverse of a slowdown: that
growth may get too hot and could contribute to an upward spiraling of prices -- or inflation.
By raising interest rates, they are making money more "expensive" to borrow and spend, stemming a tide of
cheap money that has buoyed the prices of everything from U.S. Treasury bonds to Japanese stocks in
recent years. That is tough on investors, because when easy money disappears, asset prices often fall
precipitously, and financial bubbles tend to burst.
In Turkey, where stock and bond prices have been battered, Tim Adams, U.S. Treasury undersecretary for
international affairs, yesterday offered that analysis, attributing the turmoil more to the end of easy money --
also known as liquidity -- than to the outlook for Turkey's economy.
"We're in a global transition from an era of benign economic conditions and tremendous amount of liquidity
sloshing around the world to one in which . . . central banks are draining some of that liquidity out of the
system," he said. "But the underlying fundamentals here are pretty good."
That isn't to say the market turmoil won't be painful. "The fact that we're still in the thick of a global expansion
does not mean that we can't have a financial crisis," says Kenneth Rogoff, an economics professor at
Harvard University. "Rising interest rates are going to expose some problems."
Scattered minicrises are already unfolding as investors shift their money away from riskier investments.
Earlier this year, Iceland and New Zealand both saw their currencies plunge as foreign investors pulled out of
high-yielding local-currency bonds. More recently, investors have dumped everything from junk bonds to
Russian, Colombian and Japanese stocks. Yesterday, Russia's RTS Index dropped 10.5% and Colombia's
stock market slid 9%.
By contrast, the global economy looks poised for a period of more-balanced growth after several years of
depending heavily on the free-spending U.S. consumer. Most economists expect the U.S. economy, which
spurted ahead at a 5.3% annual rate in the first quarter and now enjoys a low 4.6% unemployment rate, to
slow -- to a 3% pace -- as consumer spending tapers off and the Federal Reserve's steady increases in
short-term interest rates start to bite. Europe, Japan and emerging economies appear on track to pick up
some of the slack. Earlier this week, for example, Japan raised its estimate of annualized first-quarter
economic growth, adjusted for inflation, to 3.1% from 1.9%.
In its April economic outlook, the International Monetary Fund forecast world economic growth at an
inflation-adjusted 4.9% for 2006 and 4.7% in 2007. The forecast indicates that for the four years ending in
2007, growth will average 4.93%, the highest sustained rate since 1973. The IMF expects only three of the
180 economies it tracks -- Zimbabwe, Equatorial Guinea and the Seychelles -- to contract in 2006. That
would be the lowest number since at least 1980.

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gerd Hausler, director of international capital markets at the IMF, said the recent market volatility hadn't
materially changed the fund's forecast. "I think that the prospect of a global economy where the engines of
growth are more evenly balanced compared to what we've seen in the recent past should be a source of
comfort," he said.
Central banks calibrate their moves to keep the global economy in balance by curbing the inflation that can
result if economies grow too fast. Recent data have shown inflation pressures building in the U.S. and
Europe, and reappearing in Japan after many years of falling prices.
Federal Reserve officials, including Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke, have said in recent days that they will take
a hard line on inflation. Markets have taken that to mean they will raise their target for U.S. short-term
interest rates to 5.25% from the current 5% at their next policy-making meeting June 28-29. Last week, the
European Central Bank raised its short-term rate to 2.75% from 2.5%, and left open the possibility of further
increases. Economists expect Japan's central bank to raise its rate from zero later this year.
Taken together, the central banks' moves drastically change the landscape for investors. For one thing,
higher interest rates make the dollar a year or 10 years from now worth less today. So even if the economy
looks good, and assets such as stocks and bonds are expected to keep making the same dividend and
interest payments in the years to come, the present value of that future income falls.
Moreover, when interest rates rise, it becomes more expensive for investors to increase their returns by
using borrowed money -- a strategy known as leverage.
When investors are used to years of low interest rates and cheap money, as they have been until lately, it
can take them a while to adjust to the new reality. When they do, prices tend to move quickly. The
stock-market crash of 1987, the bursting of the Japanese real-estate bubble in the early 1990s, the Asian
financial crisis of 1997 and the Nasdaq meltdown of 2000 all occurred when central banks were raising
interest rates.
"The market has to learn again what it means for a central bank to tighten," says Peter Fisher, chairman of
money manager BlackRock Asia and a former Fed and Treasury official. As to how investors' changing
attitude toward risk will manifest itself this time, "I don't think we know," he said. "Clearly the first accident has
been in emerging-markets exposure."
The prevalence of hedge funds and other sophisticated investors in today's financial markets could make
those markets more sensitive to the type of risks that appear when central banks take away easy money.
That is because many hedge funds' strategies depend on the prices of different investments moving in
different directions -- if the fund incurs losses on one, it gains on the other. When financial crises occur, the
prices of all assets tend to act in highly unusual ways, confounding the funds' strategies. That, for example, is
how the Russian debt crisis in 1998 triggered the downfall of hedge fund Long Term Capital Management.
"These types of events happen every blue moon," says Carlos Asilis, a Miami-based portfolio manager for
hedge fund Vega Plus Capital Partners, which has $2 billion under management. "It's hard to model them and
it's hard to hedge against them. The best action in this type of market environment is to reduce risks and wait
for things to calm down."
To be sure, the march toward higher interest rates could also have an adverse effect on the global economy.
Economists and investors in the U.S. and Europe have expressed concerns that central bankers could take
rates too high, tipping their economies into recession. That concern is evident in the market for U.S. Treasury
bonds, where the yield on two-year notes has risen above the yield on 10-year notes -- an event called a
yield-curve inversion that has often, but not always, preceded economic downturns.
Some investors, however, see the central banks' moves as just the right medicine, both for the world
economy and for financial markets that had lost their sense of risk. "It's tough love," said Michael Litt, a
portfolio manager at Greenwich, Conn.-based asset-management firm FrontPoint Partners LLC. "It feels bad
now, but it's very good in the medium and long term."
---
Elizabeth Price contributed to this article.

Metals Extend Steep Declines; Selling Spreads to Grains, Coffee
Commodities Report
Metals Extend Steep Declines; Selling Spreads to Grains, Coffee
By Debbie Carlson and Holly Henschen
545 words
14 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Precious- and base-metals futures fell sharply, hurt largely by selling pressure as both markets continue to
tumble from multidecade highs set this spring, setting off a wave of selling that affected most commodities
markets.
Grain futures at the Chicago Board of Trade, coffee, sugar and frozen-orange-juice markets at the New York
Board of Trade and crude-oil futures at the New York Mercantile Exchange were indirectly influenced by
weakness in metals, analysts said.
Most-active August gold lost $44.50 to $566.80 per ounce on the Comex division of the Nymex. Nearby June
gold lost $44.30 to $562.50 an ounce. Most-active July silver lost $1.44 to $9.625. The most-active July
copper contract fell 21.80 cents to $3.0105 per pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
From the high set on May 12, August gold lost 23.5% of its value. From their May 11 highs, July silver fell
37% and July copper fell 25.5%. Yesterday's dollar drop was the June gold contract's biggest since Jan. 25,
1980, and ranks No. 5 in terms of biggest one-day drops for gold.
Metals prices, along with equities and other commodities, are falling as global investors downgrade their
expectations of global growth in response to interest-rate hikes in Europe and the U.S., and tough inflation
talk by the Federal Reserve. Dollar strength also has been a nemesis for commodities as a stronger
greenback makes dollar-denominated commodities pricier.
Dan Vaught, an analyst with A.G. Edwards, said it is possible that commodities have, at least for the short
term and possibly well into the intermediate term, set their highs.
"I think we're going to remain in downward pressure in metal and possibly in energies through the summer,"
Mr. Vaught said.
Losses for gold started in Asia, when spot prices fell under $600 an ounce, and continued into the London
and New York sessions.
Some analysts also cited the recent run-up in commodities -- particularly metals -- as overzealous and due for
a sharp correction. "We might have set some fairly significant highs in some of the markets. It's not to say that
we can't go back and revisit those highs," said Dave Rinehimer, director of futures research at Citigroup
Global Markets.
Selling by speculators who entered the metals markets this spring during the rally to recent highs was also a
big factor behind the break in metals, said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE: Futures fell for a second straight session after the International Energy Agency cut its 2006 global
oil-demand forecast and reported that developed nations' crude-oil stocks are at a 20-year high. The July
crude oil contract settled down $1.80 at $68.56 a barrel.
LEAN HOGS: Prices on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange settled sharply higher, as traders bought back
previously sold positions and amid strength in cash hog markets as meat packers compete for tight supplies.
Nearby June rose 1.57 cents to 75.10 cents a pound.

Interest-Rate Fears Drive World-Wide Slide --- As Some Markets Fall 20% or More, Investors Fret Continued Selling Could Gain Momentum of Its...
World Stock Markets
Interest-Rate Fears Drive World-Wide Slide --- As Some Markets Fall 20% or More, Investors Fret
Continued Selling Could Gain Momentum of Its Own
By Craig Karmin
1,142 words
14 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH STOCKS in developing countries from Russia to India suffering another day of sharp losses, concerns
are mounting that the continued selling could gain a momentum of its own as international investors head for
the exits.
"The fear is that the selloff becomes a one-way move and creates a vicious cycle," said Gary Kleiman of
Kleiman International Consultants, an emerging-markets consultancy in Washington, D.C.
Five weeks into a rout that has caused some markets to fall more than 20%, selling by hedge funds -- as well
as withdrawals by individual investors from emerging-market stock funds -- are aggravating a tough situation.
In the past three weeks, mutual funds that invest in emerging-market stocks have seen $8.5 billion in outflows
world-wide, according to EmergingPortfolio.com Fund Research. That is a dramatic reversal, representing
43% of all new money that flooded into these funds for all of last year.
The selloff comes even as many economists feel that emerging-market economies remain on firm footing with
solid growth prospects. Still, the sharp drop in share prices has prompted a retreat that has started feeding on
itself.
Yesterday, stocks in South Korea fell nearly 3% on fears that a global growth slowdown would hurt their
exports, especially technology companies. This concern contributed to the Nikkei Stock Average's 4.1%
decline in Japan. India's Sensex was down 4.4% and is 28% lower from its peak on May 10.
Russia's RTS Index was 9.4% lower, its worst day since October 2003, while Turkey's market was down 6%.
Colombia's stock market slid 9% and narrowly missed suspending trading for the second straight day, which
the market does for declines of greater than 10%, like that on Monday. Commodities resumed their recent
selloff, providing a double hit to commodity-oriented economies such as Russia and Brazil. South Africa was
down 4% as the price of gold fell again.
Last month, emerging-markets stocks started their decline amid fears that rising global interest rates would
end a long period of easy credit, which makes it easier for people to borrow money and speculate. Signs of a
slowdown in global economic growth didn't help, since many of these countries rely on exports. Together,
these factors made investors more adverse to risk and sent them fleeing from their more speculative bets,
such as emerging-markets stocks.
Today, just the possibility of investors pulling their money out of mutual funds is enough to make fund
managers hesitant to buy new stocks, says Nicholas Smithie, an emerging-market portfolio manager for MFS
Investment Management in Boston. "Even if you're not getting redemptions, you fear redemptions," he says.
"That is preventing new buying."
Mr. Smithie says his own fund's redemptions have been modest and that his response has been to stay on
the sidelines during this selloff. Other managers have unloaded some of what they perceive to be their riskier
stocks, then keep that money in cash. Vladimir Milev, an analyst for the Metzler/Payden European Emerging
Markets Fund in Los Angeles, says that about 20% of his fund is in cash, up from next to nothing in April.
At the same time, hedge funds, private investment pools for wealthy investors, have been quick to pull money
out and lock in last year's gains. Since some hedge funds borrowed money to buy these shares, they are
often selling the larger and more easily tradable markets, such as Brazil or South Korea, to raise cash
quickly. In the more thinly traded markets, even modest hedge-fund selling can have a big impact on price
movements.

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Not everyone has rushed to the exits. Big state and municipal pension funds added emerging-market stocks
over the past year or two. Many of these funds bought into emerging markets at a time when Treasury yields
were at their lowest level in decades, and these money managers needed to boost their returns.
Mr. Kleiman says he has seen no signs that these powerful institutional investors have joined the selling,
though typically their decision-making process takes much longer than that of more nimble mutual funds or
speculators. Pension funds have a lower tolerance for risk, and if they do leave, they may stay out for a while,
as they did after the 1997 to 1998 financial crisis in emerging markets.
Analysts have been quick to note that the selloff this time around shouldn't be confused with the big declines
in 1997 and 1998. In the late 1990s, the problems came primarily from the developing countries' own
behavior: Many had taken on too much foreign-currency debt and were running large trade deficits. As a
result, they were forced to devalue their currencies, which led to a meltdown in their stock and bond markets.
This time around, it is events outside the developing world -- rising interest rates, slowing global growth --
that are the primary reasons for the selloff. The emerging-market economies themselves are in better shape
than they have been in years. Trade deficits have been turned into surpluses, foreign debt has been paid
down and many countries now allow their currencies to trade more freely, offering their economies greater
flexibility.
Some investors fear that if the stock-market selloff persists too long, it may start to erode some of that
economic strength. "Major moves in the markets can cause the underlying economic fundamentals to
change," says Arjun Divecha, a fund manager with GMO Emerging Markets in Berkeley, Calif.
One reason why economists expect such strong growth in many developing countries is because they have
been attracting large amounts of capital from foreign investors, which is often used to fund economic projects.
With foreigners now pulling money out of these markets, "that great growth future is now diminished," Mr.
Divecha says.

Dow Industrials Tumble 99.34 Points As Rate Worries Continue to Take Toll
Monday's Markets
Dow Industrials Tumble 99.34 Points As Rate Worries Continue to Take Toll
By E.S. Browning
540 words
13 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CONTINUING INTEREST-RATE anxiety drove the Dow Jones Industrial Average down an additional 99.34
points, marking the sixth decline in the past seven sessions and leaving it up less than 1% for the year.
The beleaguered Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks have been under attack for
weeks, fell 2.05%, or 43.74 points, to 2091.32. That put the Nasdaq down 11.8% from the five-year high it hit
in April. Wall Street terms that a correction -- or a decline of more than 10%. The Nasdaq is at its lowest point
since October, though still well short of a bear market -- or 20% decline. It is down 5.2% since the year
began.
The Dow industrials, which fell 0.91% to 10792.58, are 7.3% off the six-year high they hit last month. They
are short of a 10% correction but up just 0.7% since the year began.
Some emerging markets tumbled again; shares in India fell 3%, and Brazil and Mexico slid more than 4%
each.
Investors fear another quarter-point interest-rate increase when the Federal Reserve holds its next policy
meeting on June 28 and 29. Many people are concerned about more rate increases after that, and worry that
the higher rates will spread pain through the economy, crimping consumer spending and corporate profits.
Adding to the anxiety, the government releases May wholesale-price data today and consumer-price data
tomorrow. On top of that, Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke was scheduled to speak three separate times this
week, most notably Thursday to the Economic Club of Chicago.
"The speeches and the inflation data are holding people at bay," keeping them from buying stocks, said Mike
Viracola, head of trading at brokerage firm Canaccord Adams, based in Boston.
Stoking the interest-rate concerns, Cleveland Federal Reserve Bank President Sandra Pianalto told a
business group that inflation at recent levels "exceeds my comfort level."
Some investors were surprised that the Dow industrials and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index last week both
posted their worst weekly declines in more than a year. They are worried that interest-rate uncertainty could
last into the summer.
The S&P 500 yesterday fell 1.27%, or 15.90 points, to 1236.40. It is down 1% for 2006, 6.7% off its recent
high and at its lowest point since last year.
But higher interest rates make the dollar more desirable, so the U.S. currency continued its rebound against
the euro and the yen. Oil and gold fell.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.25 billion
shares; 716 stocks rose and 2,591 fell.
Bond prices were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing
the yield up to 4.983%. The 30-year bond was up 1/32 to yield 5.024%.
The dollar strengthened. The currency traded at 114.30 yen, up from 113.97, while the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.2599 from $1.2642.

Politics &Economics: Energy Firms Turn to Pipelines, In Bet Gas Ports Won't Happen
Politics &Economics: Energy Firms Turn to Pipelines, In Bet Gas Ports Won't Happen
By Russell Gold
650 words
12 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Several energy companies are making big bets that new tanker terminals for importing natural gas along the
East Coast won't be built, a turn of events that could put upward pressure on consumer heating and electricity
bills there.
Several years ago the industry geared up to build several liquefied-natural-gas, or LNG, terminals along the
East Coast as the declining cost of liquefying and transporting natural gas from around the world on tankers
made the projects more economical. But stiff community opposition threatens most terminal plans, experts
say.
As a result, several companies plan to build natural-gas pipelines to bring gas to the region over land from
other parts of the country. "We don't see realistically eastern U.S. LNG facilities being built. It is just really
tough to get things constructed from an environmental and permitting standpoint," says Martha Wyrsch,
president of Duke Energy Gas Transmission, a unit of Duke Energy Corp. It announced earlier this month that
it would partner with CenterPoint Energy Inc. to build a 1,600-mile gas pipeline from Texas to Pennsylvania.
The companies haven't disclosed how much the pipelines would cost.
Earlier this year, Kinder Morgan Energy Partners LP and Sempra Energy said they were teaming up to build
a $4 billion, 1,300-mile gas pipeline from the Rockies to Ohio. Combined, the new pipelines should deliver as
much as 3.5 billion cubic feet a day into the East Coast market by 2009.
Neither pipeline project would be moving ahead unless the companies felt confident the less-expensive,
brand-new LNG terminals wouldn't be there to compete, said Bernard J. Picchi, energy research director at
Foresight Research Solutions LLC. Building a pipeline to bring gas into the Northeast is considerably more
expensive than an LNG terminal, he said.
That higher cost would be passed along to consumers. Mr. Picchi says the pipeline gas will likely cost $1 to
$1.75 per million British thermal units more than LNG. East Coast consumers will likely feel this inflation:
More than half heat their homes with gas and about one-third of their electricity is powered by gas.
Operators of the proposed LNG terminals say they aren't giving up yet, and say that pipeline construction
faces its own share of risks. "Any major energy infrastructure project is going to have a challenge to be built,
no matter whether it is an LNG terminal or a pipeline," says John Hritcko Jr., regional project manager for
Broadwater Energy, a joint venture between Royal Dutch Shell PLC and TransCanada Corp. that is trying to
build an LNG terminal in the Long Island Sound. State and federal permits for the project are pending.
Some LNG terminal construction is moving forward. The three existing LNG terminals on the East Coast are
being expanded. Several new terminals are under construction along the Gulf Coast and in Mexico. And there
still is a chance that an LNG terminal could be built on Canada's eastern coast. The proliferation of terminals
along the Gulf Coast will likely keep nearby natural-gas prices there among the lowest in the nation.
But plans for multiple new East Coast terminals, delivering gas directly to consumers, have largely fallen by
the wayside. Industry observers say the new pipelines are a death knell. "The die has been cast for the East
Coast," says Ira Joseph, LNG analyst with PIRA Energy.
"Consumers in the Northeast would benefit from siting LNG terminals in the area, but I don't see those being
sited and constructed in the near future," says Donald Mason, an Ohio Public Utility Commissioner and
chairman of the National Association of Regulatory Utility Commissioners' gas committee. "The pipeline
projects can move forward in a more costly but more timely manner," he says.

As Foreign Stocks Slide, U.S. Holds Ground --- Blue Chips Rally to Finish Higher After Hefty Drop in Early Trade; Beating `Last Push of Big...
Thursday's Markets
As Foreign Stocks Slide, U.S. Holds Ground --- Blue Chips Rally to Finish Higher After Hefty Drop in
Early Trade; Beating `Last Push of Big Fear'
By E.S. Browning
1,022 words
9 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A JARRING U.S. STOCK SLIDE followed by a rapid recovery left some market analysts hoping for an end to
the past month's stock declines -- at least for now.
Down 173.35 points by midday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average recovered all its losses, finishing up 7.92
points, or 0.07%, at 10938.82. It is up 2.1% for 2006, although down 6% since hitting a six-year high May 10.
Other indexes showed similar swings, with the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index finishing slightly ahead in
the day's trading and the Nasdaq Composite Index and Russell 2000 small-stock index slightly down.
The recovery came on record total trading of New York Stock Exchange stocks of 3.58 billion shares, and
followed deep losses in markets overseas. Such a sharp rebound on heavy volume can be a sign of better
things to come, analysts said.
"We finally got that last push of big fear" during the day, said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at
mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. "We finally reached the ultimate breaking point and
the sellers were just exhausted. I think we are looking at a rally over the next week or so." Mr. Sachs worries
that stocks could slip again after that, however.
He and other traders said they didn't see any event that triggered the late-day recovery. Instead, they said,
investors simply went into panic mode in the morning, following the sharp overseas declines, and most who
wanted to sell did their work by lunchtime. When buyers appeared, there were fewer potential sellers left to
hold stocks down. As the day progressed, some market bears took profits on bets they had made that stocks
would decline. To do that, they bought stocks, which pushed indexes higher.
"It was dramatic. It means we have had all or virtually all of the spring correction," said Phil Roth, chief
technical-market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak. "The significant thing is that you were
down a lot and erased the losses. That is a significant swing and it occurred on very big volume." The
intraday swing appeared to be the largest since 2004. Mr. Roth cautioned that he expects stocks to resume
their decline in late summer or autumn because factors weighing on stocks, such as rising interest rates and
the bull market's age, haven't changed.
The day's action was the opposite of what occurred Wednesday, when stocks rose at the start of the trading
day but then ran out of steam and plunged in the afternoon. That kind of trading signals trouble, while
yesterday's reversal is a positive sign, analysts said.
Investors began the day on bad economic news. Authorities at the European Central Bank and in India,
South Korea and South Africa raised benchmark interest rates. Turkey did so Wednesday. Those moves
intensified concerns that global efforts to prevent inflation will crimp the world's economies and hurt
corporate profits, and helped spark the overseas market slides.
Then, Federal Reserve governor Donald Kohn called recent inflation numbers "somewhat troubling," during
a Senate hearing on his nomination to be Fed vice chairman, reinforcing fears that more interest-rate
increases are coming in the U.S.
By midday, the S&P 500 had erased all of its gains for the year and was showing a decline for 2006. As the
S&P 500 reached the 1235 level at which it had bottomed out in earlier weeks, buyers tiptoed back and the
market turned. The S&P 500 finished up 0.14%, or 1.78 points, at 1257.93, up 0.8% this year.
The Nasdaq composite finished down 0.30%, or 6.48 points, at 2145.32, a decline of 2.7% this year. The
Russell 2000 fell 0.04%, or 0.25 point, to 706.53, up 4.9% this year.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite the upbeat finish, the stock market faces challenges. Many investors expect the Fed to raise its
target rate for overnight bank lending another quarter percentage point to 5.25% at its next meeting, June 28
and 29, and some fear more increases later. Many fear that continuing interest-rate increases, at a time when
key parts of the economy such as the housing market are slowing, could weigh on stocks. Those fears have
made investors pull back from stocks linked to economic expansion, including stocks in developing countries.
Reflecting those concerns as well as a flight to the safety of bonds, the yield of the benchmark 10-year
Treasury note fell to 5.006%. That put it nearly down to the level of the Fed's current target overnight rate.
When those rates get so close, some bond analysts fear it signals that the market expects slower economic
growth.
There were positive economic signs. The latest weekly jobless data showed fewer claims than expected,
suggesting economic resilience. Crude-oil futures fell 47 cents to $70.35 a barrel following the death of
terrorist leader Abu Musab al Zarqawi.
Gold fell $18.30 to $609.10 a troy ounce, and the dollar continued its rebound.
Outside the U.S., stocks plunged in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 3.51%, or 7.32 points, to 201.24.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the NYSE, 1,421 stocks rose; 1,867 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year
bond was up 9/32, pushing the yield down to 5.069%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 114.16 yen, up from 113.47, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2655 from $1.2791.

Commodities Join Broader Decline --- Some Experts See Resilience In Asset Class, Suggesting Global Growth Has Legs
Commodities Report
Commodities Join Broader Decline --- Some Experts See Resilience In Asset Class, Suggesting
Global Growth Has Legs
By Ann Davis
705 words
9 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Commodities swooned along with the broader markets yesterday, with metals tumbling. But as an asset
class, commodities have shown more resilience recently than emerging-market stocks, suggesting that
investors still see global growth as steady.
The Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index is down 8.5% since its recent May 11 high, while the more
energy-focused Goldman Sachs Commodities Index is down 6.6% since then. Yet neither of those indexes
are doing as poorly as the Morgan Stanley Capital International Emerging Market Index, which has tumbled
21% since its May 9 high.
Unlike many financial assets, the most widely watched commodities contracts represent prices for delivery in
the near term, and tend to reflect current market sentiment rather than where investors anticipate prices will
move later. Market sentiment has dampened in metals because their prices rose much more steeply earlier
this year than oil prices. Analysts and investors say metals are still in a correction. But what some analysts
find remarkable is how strong the rest of the complex continues to be in the face of considerable market jitters
over inflationary pressures and rising interest rates.
"What is interesting about the commodity space is that while base metals have sold off sharply, the rest of the
commodities have been much more resilient," said Jeffrey Currie, head of commodities research at Goldman
Sachs Group Inc. in London. While fear of interest-rate increases is weighing heavily on the market, with oil
prices still close to $70 a barrel, "the risk in these markets still continues to be to the upside given that the
current economic environment is still strong."
Nymex crude oil for July delivery settled at $70.35 a barrel, down 47 cents, or 0.7%, after sinking earlier amid
broader market moves. It held up in part because the killing of a leading al Qaeda terrorist increased
expectations of U.S. progress in the Middle East. Nymex natural gas for July delivery rose 3.6%, to $6.19 a
million British thermal units.
Since May 11, however, metals such as copper and gold have fallen nearly 15% on the New York Mercantile
Exchange after a parabolic rise. Gold for June delivery fell on the Comex division of Nymex yesterday by
$18.30 a troy ounce, or 2.9%, to $609.10. Silver finished the day at $11.042 a troy ounce, down 81.50 cents,
or 6.9%. Copper for June delivery fell 22.70 cents, or 6.2%, to $3.4520 a pound.
The closing of a hedge fund that was active in the copper market, the $250 million Ospraie Point fund, was a
sign of how tricky it has been for investors to accurately anticipate the timing of that metal's correction. The
fund was down 29% this year on a variety of bets gone wrong, people familiar with the fund say. An Ospraie
spokesman confirmed that the fund was being dissolved and investors could move their funds to the flagship
Ospraie Fund.
"There's a strike on the buyers' side and sellers are panicking" in the metals markets, said James A. Vail,
portfolio manager with ING Investment Management Co. "Maybe this is the capitulation we've all been waiting
for, but we're not going to know until it's over."
Frank Holmes, CEO and chief investment officer at US Global Investors Inc., which runs a variety of
natural-resources funds, said that metals' correction is continuing for several reasons. "It became way
overbought relative to demand," he said, and metals prices tend to go through seasonal dips in the summer.
Meanwhile, some are attributing the sharper fall in emerging markets to the fact that many commodities
markets, such as oil, are extremely liquid, whereas some emerging markets are less liquid.
Separately, gasoline futures fell by almost eight cents following a decision by the Goldman Sachs Commodity
Index to replace unleaded gasoline with a blendstock contract. Prices later recovered after a Texas refinery

reported production problems. The July contract settled 2.18 cents lower at $2.1022 a gallon on the New York
Mercantile Exchange.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Foreign Reserves Rise
To a High in Japan
On Gains in the Euro
TOKYO -- Japan's foreign reserves rose in May to their highest level, mainly because of the increased value
of the euro, the Ministry of Finance said.
Separate data from the International Monetary Fund showed that China continued to hold the world's largest
foreign-exchange reserves.
The increase in Japanese holdings by $3.87 billion to $864.11 billion in May marked the third consecutive
month of increases in the value of Japan's convertible foreign currencies, gold and IMF special drawing
rights.
The euro strengthened to $1.2809 in New York at the end of May from $1.2633 at the end of April, according
to ministry data.
The increase came despite a rise in U.S. bond yields, which decreased the value of Japan's U.S. bond
holdings, a Ministry of Finance official said. Yields move inversely to prices.
China's foreign reserves, including gold, stood at $910.05 billion in March, while Japan's reserves came in
second at $857.61 billion in April, according to data from the IMF, which uses calculation methods that are
different from Japan's.
-- James Simms
BRIEFLY:
SYDNEY, Australia -- Spending and investment helped Australia's economy gather momentum in the first
quarter. Official figures show gross domestic product increased 0.9% from the previous quarter and 3.1%
from a year earlier, the fastest pace in 18 months. Australia's economy expanded 2.9% from a year earlier in
the final three months of 2005.

EUROPE
Russian Cabinet
Approves Investments
In Power Monopoly
MOSCOW -- Russia's cabinet cleared a plan by state power monopoly RAO UES to attract up to $90 billion in
investment in the country's strained electricity industry.
The firm's head, Anatoly Chubais, said the cabinet's decision would pave the way for UES to split into a grid
company and different generating companies and attract as much as two trillion rubles, or $75 billion, of
investment, including share offerings by the individual generating companies from 2007.
The power grid will remain fully state-owned and will need state support of up to 400 billion rubles, he said.

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
UES, which inherited all Soviet electricity grid and generation assets on Russian territory, has said the
proposal was key to avoiding the kind of major blackouts that have hit Moscow and St. Petersburg in the past
year.
President Vladimir Putin threw his weight behind the plan this month by calling on UES to boost investments
in generating capacity after major industries complained that UES was not meeting their power needs.
"The approval will be a major boost for the country's economy. It means that not only new power generation
stations will be built, but that the whole of the power sector will be switched to new technologies," said Mr.
Chubais.
Energy Minister Viktor Khristenko, who presented the UES plan to the cabinet, said utilities being spun off
from UES may raise a total of $7.8 billion via stock offerings in 2007-2010.
He said UES would keep control of all companies after the IPOs, which will take place in Russia and abroad.

Turkey Unexpectedly
Raises Interest Rate
By Hefty 1.75 Points
LONDON -- The Turkish central bank delivered its own version of shock therapy to the country's ailing
financial markets with an unexpectedly aggressive 1.75 percentage-point interest-rate increase.
Yesterday's increase is the first in the five years since Turkey's financial crisis. It takes the overnight
borrowing rate to 15% and the lending rate to 18%. The move, which flouted expectations of a half-point
boost, is aimed at reining in inflation after an unexpectedly sharp surge in consumer prices reported last
week. The central bank is struggling to meet its 5% year-end target, while the inflation rate stood at 9.99%
in May.
The Turkish lira, which had fallen about 17% against the dollar recently, strengthened in after-hours trading
after the rate increase. That was partly the result of a general withdrawal from emerging markets, as investors
anticipated higher U.S. interest rates than they had previously expected.
But unexpectedly high Turkish inflation figures and concerns that officials had lost their grip on the economic
situation exacerbated the selloff. In the same period, stocks on the IMKB-100 lost 23%, and yields on
government bonds shot up four percentage points.

Swap Shop: An Arcane Corner of Finance Creates a London Billionaire --- Mr. Spencer Builds an Empire As Derivatives Broker; Big Exchanges...
Swap Shop: An Arcane Corner of Finance Creates a London Billionaire --- Mr. Spencer Builds an
Empire As Derivatives Broker; Big Exchanges Move In --- A GBP 1,000 Bet on His Shoes
By David Reilly
2,205 words
8 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- As a young broker in London in the early 1980s, Michael Spencer was fired from one job for
making a bad bet on gold and another for trying to hide losses on futures contracts.
His career in tatters, he scraped together $60,000 with three partners to play another hunch: There was
money to be made acting as middleman between big banks trading complex financial instruments. That
wager has turned Mr. Spencer into one of Britain's richest men.
As more banks, corporations, and big investors began using derivatives to hedge risk, Mr. Spencer, 51 years
old, built his company, ICAP PLC, into a derivatives-industry giant. Today, ICAP is one of the world's largest
brokers of the complex financial instruments. Mr. Spencer's stake in the publicly traded company is valued at
about $1.1 billion.
Now, big change is sweeping through this arcane corner of the financial markets. Operators of traditional
stock and commodities exchanges -- many of them newly public companies -- are searching for new sources
of revenue, and the booming derivatives market looks like fertile ground.
The planned merger of NYSE Group Inc. and European exchange operator Euronext NV would give the New
York Stock Exchange access to Euronext's derivatives-exchange business, an area Big Board Chief
Executive John Thain has identified as a priority. The Chicago Mercantile Exchange Inc. has formed a joint
venture with Reuters Group PLC to trade currency derivatives, a move that will bring it into direct competition
with ICAP. Nasdaq Stock Market Inc. is also looking toward ICAP's domain. Exchanges can often charge
higher fees for trading derivatives than for stocks, for which investors now have a host of low-cost
alternatives.
This rapid consolidation is pushing Mr. Spencer toward the point where he must eat or be eaten. "What we do
now," Mr. Spencer said in an interview late last year, will determine whether ICAP is "a shark" or "a big fat
tuna." He is beefing up ICAP's electronic-trading capabilities and aims to eventually generate more than half
of ICAP's profits through such trading. In April, ICAP beat out Nasdaq and others to purchase, for $825
million, EBS Group Ltd., which competes with Reuters and has a large electronic system for trading
currencies.
Mr. Spencer predicts that the coming years could be turbulent ones for the derivatives industry. Either
specialty brokers like ICAP will combine with mainstream exchanges to form "superpower global exchanges,"
he says, or there will be "a clash of Goliaths."
Over the years, Mr. Spencer has shown a stomach for risk. In his freewheeling workplace, he and his
employees have bet on card games and sporting events. He sometimes said to colleagues: "Don't look down.
A thousand pounds. Am I wearing lace-up or slip-on shoes?" Some took the bet. The stupidest wager he ever
made, he says, was when he lost about $65,000 betting on the value of a runway model's jewelry. He says he
eventually set up an in-house book to keep track of everyone's wins and losses, which were settled monthly
on payday. Mr. Spencer says he no longer makes such bets.
ICAP's core product is derivatives, financial instruments whose values are tied to the performance of assets
such as individual stocks and bonds, or to benchmarks such as interest rates. Investors use them to hedge
risk. An investor can buy a derivative, for example, to neutralize the effects of rising interest rates or fuel
prices, of declining bond values, of swings in the price of a commodity like corn, even of the effect of weather
on crops.
Plain-vanilla derivatives such as futures -- agreements to buy or sell a specific commodity or financial
instrument at a set price on a stipulated date -- now trade electronically on commodities exchanges such as

--- Page 30 ---
Page 30 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the Chicago Merc. Prices are available for all to see. Electronic trading is more profitable for companies that
arrange trades because commissions don't have to be paid to brokers.
Mr. Spencer has long specialized in the kind of complex products that electronic-trading systems are currently
ill-suited to handle. These more-exotic derivatives are traded over-the-counter by ICAP and other inter-dealer
brokers. Working the phones, they arrange trades between large banks and other institutional customers who
are interested, say, in hedging their interest-rate risk. Prices aren't openly displayed; they are worked out by
brokers. Buyers and sellers often don't know one another's identity. This market is unregulated, and often
opaque, making it difficult for regulators to gauge the risks buyers and sellers are taking on and the
protections they have against financial-market shocks.
The over-the-counter market is far larger than the exchange-traded one. Derivatives traded in this market had
a total face value of about $285 trillion at the end of 2005, up from about $94 trillion five years before,
according to the Bank for International Settlements, an association of central banks based in Switzerland.
(The face value represents the value of the underlying instruments on which the derivative is written.) In
comparison, exchange-traded derivatives had a total face value of about $58 trillion at year-end, according to
the bank group.
The products that firms like ICAP trade "are almost like custom-tailored suits," says Michael Pagano, a
professor at Villanova University College of Commerce and Finance. "For years, there has been a growing
demand for these types of things because institutional investors have realized they need to manage their risk
more effectively, to have some kind of protection that these derivatives can offer them." In addition, demand
has surged as hedge funds and other investors have tried to profit on fluctuations in their values.
Over the past five years, ICAP's revenue has risen 95%, profit has climbed nearly 200%, and its stock, which
trades on the London Stock Exchange, has more than quadrupled in value. The company now has more than
3,000 employees, and earned $210 million in the year ended March 31, on revenue of $1.7 billion.
ICAP and other inter-dealer brokers generate about $5 billion of revenue a year trading customized
derivatives, according to a research report by Morgan Stanley. Traditional exchanges want a piece of the
action.
Mr. Spencer, the son of a British civil servant who advised governments in Asia and Africa on economic
matters, holds a physics degree from Oxford University. In 1979, while working as a broker for a now-defunct
British firm, he placed a big bet that gold would fall in value, Mr. Spencer recalls. Six weeks later, the Soviet
Union invaded Afghanistan. Gold hit an all-time high above $800 an ounce on fears of global financial
instability. Mr. Spencer was fired.
He landed at the London office of U.S. investment bank Drexel Burnham Lambert, where he ran into trouble
once again. In 1983, without authorization to do so, Mr. Spencer put the firm's capital at risk by buying futures
contracts he believed would rise in value, he acknowledges. His plan was to sell them at a profit. Instead, on
one such trade, he racked up a loss of more than $100,000, he says. After conducting an investigation,
Drexel fired Mr. Spencer, although neither the firm nor United Kingdom regulators took any legal action. Mr.
Spencer says the episode was "a deeply sad moment."
He found another job with a small broker, where he began dabbling in the nascent market for derivatives
known as interest-rate swaps. They allow a company or a bank to swap one kind of interest-rate exposure for
another. A manufacturer holding variable-rate debt, for example, could protect itself against rising interest
rates by buying from a bank a derivative that increases in value as rates climb.
Unhappy in his post, Mr. Spencer persuaded another brokerage firm to help him and three friends start their
own firm to trade swaps. They opened Intercapital Ltd. in a basement office at the edge of London's financial
district. At the time, there were about 20 to 30 inter-dealer brokers in the U.K. But most focused on currency
trades, not swaps.
The swaps market took off as more companies turned to them for protection against interest-rate fluctuations.
Even small municipal governments in the U.S. began using swaps to manage debt. By 1997, the total face
value of outstanding interest-rate swaps had risen to $22 trillion, from $682 billion a decade earlier, according
to the International Swaps and Derivatives Association.
Mr. Spencer's firm flourished. He lured brokers away from competitors with richer pay packages. When he
moved his company, now known as ICAP, to new offices in 2004, he paid to build a wine cellar there to house
his collection, which he says numbers "north of 10,000" bottles. Last September, to celebrate his 50th
birthday, he invited about 300 friends to a mansion in the south of France. He paid British pop star Robbie
Williams $1.9 million to provide entertainment.
In the mid-1990s, change began sweeping through the financial-services industry as big banks combined into
global firms. Mr. Spencer says he figured these customers would want brokers with the same global reach. In

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
1998, he purchased London-based Exco PLC, a rival broker facing financial problems, which had a bigger
presence in U.S. markets. One year later, he bought Garban PLC, a publicly traded broker active in U.S.
bond markets.
The push into the U.S. put Mr. Spencer in direct competition with Cantor Fitzgerald LLP, a New York-based
firm that dominated trading of U.S. Treasurys and also had global ambitions. Over the years, the two firms
traded jabs by hiring brokers away from one another.
Cantor Fitzgerald, which was based at the World Trade Center, lost 658 employees in the Sept. 11 terrorist
attacks. Soon after, Mr. Spencer hired away three Cantor brokers in London, prompting Cantor to sue ICAP
in Britain's High Court. Cantor claimed ICAP had violated employment contracts the brokers had with Cantor.
During a 2002 trial, lawyers for Cantor produced emails written by Mr. Spencer in late 2001. About one month
after the terrorist attacks, Mr. Spencer had written, in apparent reference to Cantor's problems: "This is the
time I have been waiting for." In another email, he wrote that he wanted to "put one up their bottoms." Mr.
Spencer says now that the emails were "schoolboyish." But he resented being portrayed by Cantor as "a
vulture," he says, adding that after Sept. 11 he proposed a hiring truce between the two feuding firms. Cantor
rejected his offer. The two firms reached an out-of-court settlement in 2003; the terms weren't disclosed.
As the chief executive of an enormous company, Mr. Spencer had become one of the faces of the British
financial establishment. He became a major backer of the U.K.'s Conservative Party. But his behavior, at
times, still drew scrutiny. In early 2004, he bought a derivatives contract for his own account that gave him
control of two million shares of Marks &Spencer PLC, a British department-store chain. Several weeks later,
the retailer was the subject of a hostile bid, and Mr. Spencer turned a substantial profit.
It later emerged that the day before Mr. Spencer bought the derivatives, he had met with Stuart Rose, a
British retailing executive and fellow director of Image Restaurants, a private U.K. company. Mr. Rose was
later retained by Marks &Spencer as chief executive officer to help fend off the hostile bid. U.K.'s Financial
Services Authority, the market watchdog, launched an investigation into Mr. Spencer's trading. He denied
receiving nonpublic information, and was cleared by the FSA in 2004.
ICAP's share of the global market for interest-rate swaps now exceeds 40%, according to a company
spokesman. The firm's success in that business has allowed it to expand into other trading sectors. In 2003,
Mr. Spencer purchased BrokerTec Global LLC, an electronic-trading system for U.S. Treasurys, for about
$250 million.
ICAP now handles more than half of all trading in U.S. Treasurys. It also arranges trades of corporate debt
and government bonds and derivatives tied to them, foreign-exchange instruments and energy derivatives.
As trading technology becomes more sophisticated, Mr. Spencer has been beefing up ICAP's
electronic-trading capabilities. "That doesn't mean we won't have any brokers, but more and more of what we
do will be electronic, and brokers will handle the more complex trades," he said last year.
Craig Donohue, the Chicago Merc's chief executive, contends that exchanges have gained the upper hand
over inter-dealer brokerages, which were once regarded as more innovative.
But he isn't counting out Mr. Spencer, whose 21% stake in ICAP gives him a big say in whether the company
combines with a larger competitor. "I wouldn't underestimate him," Mr. Donohue says.

Stocks Take Hit As Dow Finishes At 3-Month Low --- Late Surge Pushes Blue Chips Above 11000 After Sharp Drop; Bernanke's Comments Linger
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Take Hit As Dow Finishes At 3-Month Low --- Late Surge Pushes Blue Chips Above 11000 After
Sharp Drop; Bernanke's Comments Linger
By E.S. Browning
1,052 words
7 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FOLLOWING MONDAY'S selloff, investors continued to flee stocks tied most closely to economic growth, but
a late-day rebound helped to soften the blow.
After falling 199.15 points on Monday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average was down 122.30 points in the
middle of the trading day, well below the 11000 level that it had traded above for most of this year. Then, the
late surge pushed the blue-chip average back above the much-watched 11000 benchmark, leaving it down
just 46.58 points, or 0.42%, at 11002.14.
The Dow industrials now are down 640.51 points, or 5.5%, since hitting a six-year high of 11642.65 on May
10. Back then, they were fewer than 100 points below their January 2000 record close of 11722.98. Now,
they are at a three-month low, although still up 2.7% this year.
For a second day, the main drag on stocks was Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke's surprisingly blunt
warning in a speech Monday that key inflation indicators are running above the level he considers
acceptable. His comment was taken as a warning that the Fed may move to slow the economy further by
pushing its target short-term interest rate up for a 17th time running, to 5.25%, at its next interest-rate meeting
June 28 and 29. Some analysts worried the Fed might raise rates further as the summer progresses. Higher
interest rates hurt stocks by slowing economic growth and crimping corporate profits.
Investors continued to flee stocks that had shown the sharpest gains in recent years, those that had been
propelled higher by booming world economic growth. Investors now fear that those stocks have risen too far,
at a time when central bankers led by Mr. Bernanke seem intent on continuing to raise interest rates and
mopping up excess cash in order to snuff out inflationary pressures.
"The economically sensitive stocks were getting banged around," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at
San Francisco money-management and brokerage firm Nollenberger Capital Partners. But late in the day, as
the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index managed to remain above the intraday low it had hit late in May,
buyers stepped in and stocks "bounced nicely," he noted.
The S&P 500 finished down just 0.11%, or 1.44 points, at 1263.85, still up 1.3% in 2006. Makers of consumer
staples -- daily necessities such as food and household products that are less dependent on economic
growth -- actually finished the day with gains. They are heavily represented in the S&P 500. Procter &Gamble
rose 0.4% and Colgate-Palmolive gained 1%.
Things looked nasty as the day began. U.S. investors awoke to find foreign stocks down sharply in the wake
of Monday's U.S. declines.
Even after the late-day recovery, copper producer Phelps Dodge finished down 1.1%. Construction- and
mining-equipment maker Caterpillar fell 1.2%, and aluminum maker Alcoa fell 2.2%. All three have fallen
more than 15% since the first half of May. Many banking stocks, whose cost of obtaining money rises when
interest rates move higher, continued to decline. J.P. Morgan fell 1.4%, but Citigroup inched up 0.3%.
A wide variety of commodities, which had been soaring amid economic optimism until last month, also
continued their pullback, led by copper and gold. Despite continued worries about summer oil supplies and
despite export-cutoff threats from Iran, crude-oil futures edged down 10 cents to $72.50 a barrel on the New
York Mercantile Exchange. Gold futures fell $13.80 to $629.40 a troy ounce.
Small stocks, which once led the gains, were again among the hardest-hit because small companies are
more dependent on bank borrowing than large ones. The Russell 2000 small-stock index fell 0.41% to
710.96, its lowest finish in nearly four months. It is down 9.1% since hitting a record May 5, although it still is
up 5.6% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.32%, or 6.84 points, to 2162.78, down 1.9% this year.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The dollar, which becomes more attractive to investors when interest rates are higher, rose again against
both the yen and the euro. Although short-term Treasury-note prices fell on the rate fears, pushing yields
higher, longer-term notes and bonds moved the opposite way. That reflects in part a flight to safety as
investors abandon stocks for bonds, but it also suggests that bond investors may be somewhat less
concerned about inflation risks than are stock investors.
Measures of market volatility, based on option trading, moved sharply higher. That reflected fears that
markets have entered a period when investors and Fed officials alike will be uncertain of how much higher
the Fed will have to raise rates, when such decisions will be made based on what hard-to-predict economic
data indicate about inflation risks.
In the short term, investors will be watching to see whether the Dow can hold the 11000 mark, and whether
other indexes can remain above recent lows. If the market moves sharply below these much-watched levels,
another round of selling could follow.
Three more Fed officials spoke yesterday, offering opinions that weren't always fully in line with Mr.
Bernanke's.
William Poole, president of the St. Louis Federal Reserve Bank, and Fed Governor Susan Bies both
suggested that higher inflation could force more rate increases, echoing Mr. Bernanke. But Kansas City Fed
President Thomas Hoenig said inflation pressures should "taper off" as economic growth moderates,
suggesting that he may be less worried about inflation risks.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated; bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield down to 5.010%. The 30-year bond was up 14/32 to yield 5.083%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 113.22 yen, up from 112.17, while the euro
fell against the dollar to $1.2838 from $1.2914.

Gold Drops, Reacting to Bernanke, Now 13% Off Last Month's Highs
Commodities Report
Gold Drops, Reacting to Bernanke, Now 13% Off Last Month's Highs
By Allen Sykora
484 words
7 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Comments on inflation from Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke and a more conciliatory tone from
Iran on its nuclear program sent gold futures sharply lower, analysts said.
June gold lost $13.80 or 2.2% to $629.40 a troy ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange. Most-active August gold fell $14 to $634.70 an ounce.
Gold is now $90.40, or 13%, off its 26-year high hit early last month.
Much of the decline occurred in screen trading ahead of the pit open after Mr. Bernanke addressed the
International Monetary Conference late Monday. He said core inflation readings in the past few months have
been "at or above the upper end of the range that many economists, including myself, would consider
consistent with price stability." This led to expectations for more Federal Reserve rate increases.
"The market is finally listening to what the Fed governors are saying -- that interest rates are going to go
higher," said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management. Much of the selling came in the
form of long liquidation by large speculative funds, he said. Long liquidation is the sale of previously
purchased futures.
Previously, the market had construed a smaller-than-expected 75,000 rise in May nonfarm payrolls, released
Friday, to mean there could be a pause in rate tightening, said John Person, president of National Futures
Advisory Service.
Mr. Bernanke's comments helped to prop up the dollar late Monday and yesterday, which tends to undercut
gold.
Some of gold's recent support had come from stronger crude oil and geopolitical worries surrounding Iran's
nuclear program. Comments from Iran led traders to believe that tensions may be abating, which led crude
oil, and thus gold, lower.
Silver futures followed the downward move in gold. June silver fell 44 cents to $11.805 an ounce.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Comex prices were sharply lower due to the Fed's hawkish view on inflation. June copper closed
down 10.65 cents to $3.5845 a pound.
CRUDE OIL: Futures staged a sharp recovery from losses -- inspired by the conciliatory remarks from Iran --
after the U.S. government warned of potential oil and gas supply disruptions during this year's hurricane
season. The July contract settled 10 cents lower at $72.50 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
CITRUS: The federal government barred Florida citrus growers from sending fresh fruit to other
citrus-producing states this season, fearing diseases that have damaged crops in the state would spread.
Florida officials said they were blindsided by the federal Agriculture Department announcement, though it's
not immediately clear how much it will hurt the industry. About 90% of Florida citrus is processed into juice,
while only fresh fruit is affected by the new rule.
