Dow Falls 1.77% As Fed Chief Adds To Investor Jitters --- Fears of Further Rate Rises Grow as Bernanke Pledges Vigilance Against Inflation
Dow Falls 1.77% As Fed Chief Adds To Investor Jitters --- Fears of Further Rate Rises Grow as
Bernanke Pledges Vigilance Against Inflation
By E.S. Browning and Greg Ip
1,407 words
6 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A surprisingly frank inflation warning from Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke stoked fears of further
interest-rate increases, adding to investor jitters and knocking the Dow Jones Industrial Average down almost
200 points to its lowest finish since March 9.
Speaking at an international bankers' conference in Washington, Mr. Bernanke warned that inflation in
recent months has been running "at or above the upper end of the range that many economists, including
myself, would consider consistent with price stability." He said Fed policy makers would remain "vigilant" to
ensure that recent inflation readings don't become the norm.
Although other parts of Mr. Bernanke's speech indicated that he sees signs economic growth is slowing,
investors quickly concluded that the odds have increased that the Fed will raise target short-term interest
rates at its policy meeting on June 28-29.
Stocks, which already were down earlier in the day on worries about the outlook for oil prices and interest
rates, fell further in the wake of Mr. Bernanke's speech. The Dow Jones industrials tumbled 199.15 points, or
1.77%, to 11048.72.
Fears of more interest-rate increases have been spreading since the Fed warned of that possibility at its last
policy meeting on May 10. The industrial average now is down 593.93 points, or 5.1%, from its May 10
six-year high of 11642.65. That close was less than 100 points short of the industrial average's record finish
of 11722.98 in January 2000.
Since mid-May, inflation fears also have roiled a variety of international markets. The prices of many
industrial commodities, such as metals, have fallen sharply, as have stock markets in developing countries
and in developed nations from Japan to Europe.
Despite yesterday's declines in the U.S., the Dow Jones industrials are still up 3.1% for the year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index, full of volatile technology stocks, fell 2.24%, or 49.79 points, yesterday to 2169.62. It is
down 1.6% for the year.
The concern among investors is that inflation is proving more resilient than expected, despite the Fed's
campaign of interest-rate increases that began two years ago. Over that time, the Fed has raised its target
rate for overnight bank lending to 5% from 1%.
Investor attention now will focus on the government's next release of consumer-price data, due on
Wednesday of next week. If the pace of inflation doesn't show signs of slowing, fears of more interest-rate
increases will spread. Higher rates are bad news for stocks because they weigh on corporate profits and
make alternative investments, such as money-market accounts, more attractive.
The prospect of higher short-term rates also is bad news for bonds, because it suggests that future bond
yields probably will be higher, making existing bonds, with their lower yields, less attractive. In response to
Mr. Bernanke's speech, Treasury-bond prices fell, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to
5.024%.
Mr. Bernanke's remarks were unexpectedly hawkish. He called a recent rise in inflation "unwelcome," and
his comments overall suggested the Fed is more concerned about recent price developments than by signs
of slowing economic growth, such as the sharp slowdown in job growth in May.
The May jobs report had prompted many economists to speculate that the Fed would finally pause in its
tightening of monetary policy, with the idea that a slowing economy would ultimately keep a lid on inflation
pressure.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Bernanke's remarks yesterday, however, sent a different signal. They dwelt heavily on the risks of higher
inflation. By contrast, he called signs of a weaker economy, including the soft jobs data, part of an
"anticipated moderation of economic growth." He made it clear that the Fed's preoccupation, at least for now,
will be keeping inflation down, not supporting economic growth.
"Given recent developments, the medium-term outlook for inflation will receive particular scrutiny," Mr.
Bernanke said. Fed policy makers "will be vigilant to ensure that the recent pattern of elevated monthly core
inflation readings is not sustained."
Inflation, measured by the consumer-price index, was 3.5% in April. By the Fed's preferred measure, which
uses a different basket of goods and services and excludes food and energy, it was 2.1%, just above the 1%
to 2% "comfort zone" of many Fed officials. Mr. Bernanke noted yesterday that in the past three months,
inflation by the latter measure has been running at a 3% annualized rate.
After Mr. Bernanke spoke, futures markets raised their odds to 74% that the Fed would further increase its
target for short-term interest rates to 5.25% later this month from the current 5%. They had put those odds at
48% before his speech.
While one more rate increase is unlikely to weaken the economy much, it does, at the margin, raise the odds
of a sharper economic slowdown than investors had so far been betting on.
Expectations of the Fed's actions have swung wildly in the past month. In late April, Mr. Bernanke told
Congress the Fed might pause in its rate increases, even if inflation remained a risk, to assess the impact of
previous rate increases. He indicated that a pause need not mean a halt. But some investors questioned the
wisdom of signaling a pause when the economy still appeared strong and inflation data had turned
worrisome. Higher inflation data a few weeks later further cemented those concerns, and some analysts said
Mr. Bernanke's anti-inflation credentials were being tested.
While Fed officials don't believe their credibility is in danger, Mr. Bernanke's remarks yesterday may have
been aimed in part at putting to rest any doubts about his commitment to low inflation.
But the remarks don't guarantee another rate increase later this month. If the inflation data reported next
week are tame, or there are signs of a more pronounced slowing in the economy, the Fed could decide to
leave rates alone.
Among investors, meanwhile, worries are spreading that they have gone too far in their pursuit of stocks
related to economic growth and industrial commodities. Shares of equipment maker Caterpillar fell 4.8%
yesterday and aluminum maker Alcoa shed 3.7%.
Some analysts thought investors overreacted to the Bernanke speech. They noted that Mr. Bernanke pointed
to signs of a cooling economy, which could suggest that, if data show inflation relenting, the Fed might be
prepared to pause soon in its rate increases.
"We're still leaving the June hike penciled in, lightly," wrote Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High
Frequency Economics, after the Bernanke speech. "But the key point for medium-term investors is that a real
slowdown is here; a June hike will be the last hike."
But not everyone was so optimistic yesterday.
Financial markets began the day lower in reaction to soaring crude-oil futures. Futures rose as high as $73.40
a barrel after a speech Sunday in which Iran's supreme leader, Ayatollah Ali Khamenei, said Iranian oil
shipments could be "seriously jeopardized" if the West tried to punish Iran for its nuclear program. By day's
end, those worries dissipated, and oil finished up just 27 cents at $72.60. But by then, Mr. Bernanke's speech
had seized center stage.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index suffered its second-heaviest decline of 2006 in percentage terms,
falling within 11 points of its low for the year, which it hit in February. The S&P 500 declined 1.78%, or 22.93
points, to 1265.29, still up 1.4% this year.
The Nasdaq composite's decline also was its second-heaviest of the year, while the Dow's was its third-worst.
Although the Dow Jones industrials still are less than 700 points off their record, the S&P and Nasdaq
composite, which fell harder in the bear market, remain far from records.
The dollar, which becomes a more attractive investment when interest rates rise, gained 0.1% yesterday
against the euro and 0.5% against the yen. It remains down against both currencies for the year.
Gold rose $7.70 to $643.20 a troy ounce.

Commodity ETFs Gain Momentum --- Fund Providers Persevere In Developing Products Despite Slowing Market
Fund Track
Commodity ETFs Gain Momentum --- Fund Providers Persevere In Developing Products Despite
Slowing Market
By John Spence
671 words
6 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Oil and precious-metals prices have lost momentum recently, but that isn't stopping exchange-traded fund
providers from pressing forward with more commodity-related products.
"There's plenty of room for more commodities," said Cliff Weber, senior vice president of the ETF
marketplace at the American Stock Exchange.
Among ETFs in registration is a fund from Barclays Global Investors, the largest ETF sponsor, designed to
track the Goldman Sachs Commodity Index. Other providers have oil-linked ETFs in registration.
Meanwhile, Deutsche Bank AG is developing additional commodity-linked products, though those ETFs are
still on the drawing board and not yet filed with regulators, said Kevin Rich, chief executive of DB Commodity
Services LLC.
The Amex's Mr. Weber noted pent-up demand for commodities ETFs as diversification tools and to hedge
geopolitical risk. The next step could be a move away from indexed approaches to more active strategies,
and the Amex also wants to list options on commodity ETFs, he said.
There are currently five U.S. listed commodity ETFs, which collectively trade more than eight million shares
daily and hold about $10 billion in assets, Mr. Weber said on a recent industry conference call hosted by
Institutional Investor.
The first domestic commodity ETFs -- StreetTracks Gold Trust and iShares Comex Gold Trust -- came to
market less than two years ago. The trusts hold bullion in a vault while investors trade shares representing a
fraction of an ounce of gold. Sibling iShares Silver Trust allows investors to own and trade silver without
purchasing and storing the metal, or opening a futures account.
In February, commodity ETFs took a major step forward when the Deutsche Bank Commodity Index Tracking
Fund began trading on the Amex. Like the exchange-listed metals trusts, the fund isn't a "true" ETF because
it is structured as a commodity pool rather than a registered investment company, although it resembles an
ETF in certain ways.
The Deutsche Bank fund was the first ETF-like product to invest in a broad basket of commodities through
the use of futures, which industry observers say could open the floodgates for more commodity offerings. It
invests in crude oil, heating oil, aluminum, gold, corn and wheat.
Then in April, the first ETF allowing U.S. investors to put money directly on the price of crude, U.S. Oil Fund,
launched on the Amex. It uses energy futures contracts, cash-settled options and other instruments to
achieve the oil exposure.
Similar to the Deutsche Bank ETF, the oil fund holds Treasury bills as collateral, which also supply a yield
used to offset management fees.
Some observers have suggested these unique ETFs are fundamentally changing commodity markets
because they allow new players, particularly individual investors, into what had been a difficult niche to enter.
Michael Lewis, global head of commodities research at Deutsche Bank AG, said commodities are gaining
more credibility, likening them to the status of emerging markets in the mid-1980s.
"Back then few investors had exposure to emerging markets, but today no portfolio would be complete
without them," he said.

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One of the oft-repeated benefits of commodities is diversification, since the sector tends not to have much
relationship with the performance of more traditional asset classes such as stocks and bonds. Strong global
growth, rising interest rates and higher inflation are seen as bullish signs for commodities.
Still, both commodities and emerging markets are known for their above-average risk, and financial advisers
generally recommend that each represent no more than about 5% to 10% of investors' portfolios.
The volatile nature of commodities has been driven home in the past few weeks. The two gold ETFs, for
example, fell about 4% in the four weeks through June 1, and the silver trust plunged more than 13%,
according to investment research firm Morningstar Inc. Meanwhile, the Deutsche Bank commodity basket lost
almost 3% and the volatile oil ETF is also in negative territory.

Page 47 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Is Easy Money Going Down the Drain? --- Anxious Markets Closely Watch Push by World's Central Banks To Cut Off a Speculative Binge
Abreast of the Market
Is Easy Money Going Down the Drain? --- Anxious Markets Closely Watch Push by World's Central
Banks To Cut Off a Speculative Binge
By E.S. Browning and Justin Lahart
1,004 words
5 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE PROBLEMS ROILING the world's financial markets lately can be summarized in a simple sentence:
Central bankers are draining away some of the easy money that has made investors' lives comfortable for
years.
Whether once-hot investments such as Florida condos, developing-country stocks, gold and copper recover
or continue to cool could depend on how serious the central bankers decide to be. If they keep mopping up
the excess liquid cash they had helped pour into the markets, they will eventually slake the world's thirst for
speculative investments, forcing investors toward established stocks, government bonds and other safe
investments. If they decide to go easy, as Japanese officials recently have been doing, then maybe some of
the speculative investments can hang on.
"The question of our time is how much do we, the markets, have to have our hand held by a promise from the
central banks to always be kind and gentle," says Paul McCulley, a fund manager and economist for
bond-fund giant Pacific Investment Management Co.
The situation stems from the painful 2000-2002 bear market. Fearing price deflation that would cause a
recession or even depression, central bankers slashed rates and pumped money into the world's financial
system by buying bonds, funneling deposits into commercial banks and generally tossing money at the
problem.
They avoided economic disaster. But the side effect was an eruption of questionable investments.
Companies couldn't use all that cash to build businesses, so a lot wound up in the financial markets.
Speculators borrowed at tiny interest rates in Asia and profited by buying higher-yielding bonds in the U.S.
Americans loaded up on cheap mortgages, plasma television sets and real estate. With the world economy
booming, the speculative money flowed not only into copper and zinc, but also into gold, silver -- even
timberland.
Many investment pros think those speculative days are gone. "My brother-in-law is no longer flipping condos
in Miami. His credit line has been shut down by the banks. My neighbor's plans for a new kitchen are on hold
-- turns out he made a wrong leveraged bet on commodities," Bank of America market strategist Joseph
Quinlan wrote in a report to clients.
Developing-country stock markets have fallen hard. So have commodity prices. Higher interest rates are
forcing people to cut back on the use of new mortgages to finance spending and investment, says Jason
Trennert, chief investment strategist at New York research and brokerage firm International Strategy
&Investment.
But the markets last week didn't seem quite so sure that the party has ended. Stock markets in countries
such as India bounced back strongly late in the week, as talk spread that the pullback was over. And demand
for U.S. junk bonds and for developing-country bonds has remained surprisingly strong.
During the speculative binge, the difference in interest rates on those risky investments had gotten razor-thin
compared to those of U.S. Treasury notes: Investors were treating them as if they were almost as safe. Now
rates for those more-speculative bonds have risen a bit, but only a bit, and they remain far below the
historical norm.
Of course, some of this could simply be a sign that it takes time for people to adjust to changes in the
investment climate. But there could be another explanation for the resilience of speculative investments:
People aren't persuaded yet that all the easy money really is going to disappear. Since Chairman Ben

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bernanke took the helm on Feb. 1, the Federal Reserve has continued raising interest rates, but it has also
sent clear indications that it is looking for a way to pause in the tightening.
And Japan's central bank, whose policy shift toward tighter money this year was widely considered a strong
signal of toughening in the investment climate, has suddenly shown signs of hesitation.
In an effort to shake Japan out of its long economic malaise, the Bank of Japan long had been the world's
prime source of easy money. When it finally started sopping up the cash it had generated, Japan's central
bank moved very gradually -- not actually raising interest rates but just cutting back on the extra money it
had funneled to commercial banks. It cut that cash hoard down to about 12 trillion yen, or about $110 billion,
in late May from more than 30 trillion yen. But then something unexpected happened: A key overnight interest
rate moved higher. Fearing that it was overly taxing the system, the central bank reversed course and sent
the banks more money, pushing the cash on deposit back up to 14 trillion yen.
That kind of indecision leaves some adventurous investors hoping that the days of easy money may have
longer to run. A similar logic has helped the Nasdaq Composite Index, with its many technology stocks,
rebound almost 3% from its May low.
Many U.S. investment pros are persuaded that this is wishful thinking. To prevent inflation, they say, central
banks will keep going until monetary policy gets tight enough to choke off speculation, pushing people into
safer investments.
"There will be a risk re-evaluation soon," driving down the prices of riskier investments, says Jack Ablin, chief
investment officer at Harris Private Bank in Chicago.
Adds Marc Stern, chief investment officer of money-management firm Bessemer Trust: "We cannot pinpoint
the timing of a change in market sentiment, but history suggests it will play out swiftly when it arrives. . . . Last
month's market turbulence reinforces our belief that a change may be coming. We are emphasizing
high-quality assets in all of our portfolios."
To see whether that viewpoint is correct, investors will be closely monitoring the determination of the world's
central banks to keep draining the huge pool of cash they created.

Page 49 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Goldman's Top Trader Takes Over --- Rejected Early in His Career, Blankfein Became a Power; One Reward: $30.8 Million
Goldman's Top Trader Takes Over --- Rejected Early in His Career, Blankfein Became a Power; One
Reward: $30.8 Million
By Susanne Craig
1,408 words
3 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
MIRANT Corp. recently filed a lawsuit against NRG Energy Inc. asking the court to rule that Mirant didn't use
confidential information gleaned from Goldman Sachs Group Inc. in its recent bid for NRG. A Money
&Investing article Saturday about Goldman Sachs's new top executive incorrectly said NRG had filed suit
against Mirant claiming that Goldman had passed confidential NRG information to Mirant.
(WSJ June 5, 2006)
(END)
THE 5-FOOT-8 Lloyd C. Blankfein often jokes he has to work harder per vertical inch than anyone else on
Wall Street. With his ascension to the top job at Goldman Sachs Group Inc., the 51-year-old executive who
grew up in New York City housing projects and last year received a pay package of $30.8 million has become
one of the tallest men on Wall Street.
The board of the investment bank Friday, as expected, said the BlackBerry-addicted Mr. Blankfein would
become chief executive officer if, as expected, Goldman Chairman and Chief Executive Officer Henry
Paulson Jr. is confirmed as Treasury secretary by the Senate in about six to eight weeks. Despite speculation
the board might name a nonexecutive chairman, Mr. Blankfein was given both titles. Now Mr. Blankfein will
have to pick his new management team, deciding if he will revert to the co-president system that existed prior
to his appointment as president.
Mr. Blankfein's appointment is the latest move in a remarkable career. A former tax lawyer, Mr. Blankfein was
rejected for jobs at Goldman and rival Dean Witter, now a division of Morgan Stanley. He ultimately landed at
Goldman through a back door: One of its subsidiaries hired him as a gold salesman in 1981. Since his arrival
at Goldman, the firm has transformed itself from a private partnership focused primarily on banking corporate
clients to a sophisticated risk-taker, willing to put its own money up for deals alongside its clients and on
trades, racking up millions of dollars in profits.
And his rise signals that the traders, albeit a new breed, have regained the upper hand at Goldman from the
bankers, led by Mr. Paulson. And throughout the firm's transformation into a bolder trading house, Mr.
Blankfein has proven himself as a risk manager, able to navigate these increasingly complicated transactions.
In late 2004, for instance, he played a role in advising a group of private-equity owners on the sale of a
power-plant operator, known as Texas Genco LLC, to NRG Energy Inc. for about $6 billion, a transaction that
involved harnessing various arms of the firm to structure hedges and financing for the deal.
By orchestrating these kinds of deals, Goldman has proved adept at using its own capital to make bets that
drive profits. And the market has rewarded that expertise. Over the past five years, even as its
bread-and-butter mergers advisory business waned, Goldman's stock gained almost 60%, compared with just
25% for the Dow Jones Financial Services Index. Shares of Goldman's main rival, Morgan Stanley, declined
modestly over that period.
Still, Goldman's involvement in many aspects of various deals, from financing to advising to investing its own
money, makes the management of potential conflicts a crucial task for Mr. Blankfein. NRG has filed a lawsuit
against Mirant Corp., a power company, claiming that Goldman had passed confidential NRG information
gleaned on the deal Mr. Blankfein worked on to Mirant in order to help Mirant make an unsolicited bid for
NRG. Goldman says the allegations are "ridiculous."
Navigating the conflict issue is just one of the challenges Mr. Blankfein faces. He is also taking the helm just
as the benign market environment, marked by easy credit and relatively low volatility, seems to be coming

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
slowly to a close. With the Federal Reserve and other central banks raising short-term interest rates,
analysts expect the trading environment to get tougher for the likes of Goldman.
Mr. Blankfein has big shoes to fill. Mr. Paulson posted record profits during his tenure and played a key
leadership role outside of Goldman. Mr. Paulson often made speeches on corporate governance, and, when
a public outcry erupted in 2003 over the outsize pay package of Dick Grasso, he was instrumental in ousting
the former chairman of the New York Stock Exchange.
While lacking the stature of Mr. Paulson, a former college football star, Mr. Blankfein is regarded as a clever
public speaker. And his hardscrabble background indicates that he is hardly a pushover. He put himself
through Harvard on a combination of financial-aid packages and scholarships. After finishing law school in
1978, he landed a job as a corporate-tax lawyer earning $56,000 a year, according to a person familiar with
the matter.
He was soon on track to make partner, but decided to switch gears and move to Wall Street. In 1983, shortly
after landing his job at Goldman subsidiary J. Aron &Co., Goldman laid off a number of people in the division
but Mr. Blankfein survived. A decade later, he was running all of J. Aron. Four years later, he was promoted
to co-head of Goldman's fixed-income, currency and commodities division.
At a firm that defines a worker as either a trader or a banker and where the power base tends to swing
between the two, with one rising as the fortunes of the other falls, he is commonly referred to as a trader.
He dislikes the label, but for a period of time he did have a trading account at the firm. He used to trade a
handful of currencies, notably betting how the U.S. dollar will move against the Japanese yen, although
colleagues say trading is not his forte.
Goldman named Mr. Blankfein vice chairman in April 2002, and in February 2003 he became a board
member. He was promoted to president and chief operating officer in December 2003. Since then he has
made efforts to get to know more of the firm's investment-banking clients, something that is demanded of all
top Wall Street executives.
Not long after he became president he hit the road to meet Berkshire Hathaway Inc. Chairman Warren
Buffett, a Goldman client, according to a person familiar with the matter. "They just don't come any smarter,"
Mr. Buffett says now of Mr. Blankfein.
Within Goldman, Mr. Blankfein is known for what are referred to by some as "Lloydisms," often-contrarian
sayings that are reflective of how he looks at issues.
In recent days at a meeting of Goldman partners in Chicago, the firm's top executives discussed Goldman's
commitment to various emerging markets including Russia. Mr. Blankfein spoke up, noting that the firm had
not so long ago reduced its commitment to Russia when things turned sour. Going forward, Mr. Blankfein
argued, Goldman has to examine whether the opportunity is cyclical or can last over a long period of time.
"This didn't mean we shouldn't invest more in Russia, he just wanted us all to remember the other side and
think twice before we do," said an attendee.
Peter Fisher, a former New York Fed official and Treasury undersecretary and now chairman of money
manager BlackRock's Asian operations, says he feels Mr. Blankfein is always pushing others to understand
the bigger picture. His favorite Lloydism is "the risks are more symmetric than you think." "With that statement
-- that I relentlessly plagiarize -- Lloyd makes you stop and think that when everyone is rushing to one side of
the boat it might be a good time to take a look at the other side," Mr. Fisher says.
Despite his reputation as a shrewd risk manager, Mr. Blankfein is aware the firm could be caught off guard.
The firm recently has posted a string of record quarterly results. "I haven't felt this good since June 1998," he
recently joked when a colleague congratulated him, a nod to his belief that no matter how good things are,
something bad could catch Goldman by surprise.
In June 1998, the markets were caught off guard when Long-Term Capital Management, a Greenwich,
Conn., investment firm, nearly collapsed after sticking too long with ill-fated trades on interest rates, among
other things, as markets reeled from Russia's default on bonds.

Business and Finance

What's News
Business and Finance
433 words
3 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J

Aeropostale, Dress Barn Surge; Entree Gold, M-Systems Decline
By Anjali Cordeiro
444 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small stocks rose sharply, with retailers among the biggest gainers as investors assessed their monthly sales
reports.
Aeropostale climbed $1.89, or 7.6%, to $26.63 on the New York Stock Exchange as investor expectations
that the retailer is in the midst of a turnaround outweighed a drop in its May same-store sales.
United Retail Group added 1.14, or 8%, to 15.34. The large-size women's apparel company said
comparable-store sales rose 9% in May as transactions per average store increased.
Christopher &Banks rose 1.89, or 7%, to 28.83 on the NYSE after announcing better-than-expected
comparable-store sales. Dress Barn soared 2.80, or 12%, to 26.19 after the specialty apparel retailer reported
May same-store sales above analyst expectations and raised its full-year earnings forecast.
Bucking this trend, Cato slid 26 cents, or 1.1%, to 23.33 on the NYSE after saying that its same-store sales
fell 2% in May.
Overall, stocks climbed as economic data helped to ease inflation concerns.
The Russell 2000 index of small-cap stocks gained 15.49 points, or 2.2%, to 736.50. Standard &Poor's
SmallCap 600 Index added 6.64, or 1.8%, to 382.95. These were the biggest one-day percentage gains for
both indexes since April.
"The Institute for Supply Management manufacturing number was a market-friendly number, which appears
to have temporarily assuaged some of the ongoing concerns about higher rates," said Andre Bakhos,
president of Princeton Financial Group. "We are [also] seeing some portfolio rebalancing as some investors
enter the market for perceived bargains."
The metals and mining sector had a choppy session as prices of the underlying commodities moved lower
amid some profit-taking. Entree Gold slid five cents, or 4.2%, to 1.13 on the Amex; and Apollo Gold lost two
cents, or 4%, to 48 cents, also on the Amex.
M-Systems Flash Disk Pioneers declined 4.55, or 14%, to 28.90. The Israeli technology company said it
began an internal review of prior stock-option grants and said it won't proceed at this time with a planned
secondary stock offering.
Movado Group climbed 4.42, or 24%, to 23.11 on the NYSE. The Paramus, N.J., watchmaker increased its
guidance for fiscal 2007.
Domino's Pizza climbed 82 cents, or 3.5%, to 24.50 on the NYSE. J.P. Morgan upgraded the stock to
overweight from neutral, saying it believes the pizza company offers relatively low-risk operating-income
growth in a difficult consumer environment.

Copper Rebounds After Fall Triggers Short Trading Halt
Commodities Report
Copper Rebounds After Fall Triggers Short Trading Halt
By Allen Sykora
379 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Selling of metals futures after their historic rally this spring was especially hard on copper yesterday.
Prices hit a temporary exchange-imposed closure on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange before rebounding slightly.
Most-active July copper settled 15.30 cents lower at $3.4710 a pound. The nearby June futures fell 14.80
cents to $3.5685.
Early in the session, July copper hits its 20-cent daily-fluctuation limit, resulting in the temporary close of
about 15 minutes. When it reopened with an expanded limit, the contract fell to $3.3400, its weakest level
since May 22. The prices rebounded from their lows, which traders attributed to bargain hunting after the
dollar gave up its early gains.
Most analysts and traders attributed copper's decline to liquidation by speculators and funds opting out of
bullish positions, since copper hasn't been able to return to record levels above $4 a pound hit this spring.
"We hit a bunch of sell stops underneath the markets," said Frank Lesh, broker and analyst with FuturePath
Trading LLC in Chicago. "Metals continued their correction." Sell stops are preplaced sell positions.
Copper fell along with other metals, including gold, silver, platinum and aluminum.
Some observers suggested there was spillover selling from aluminum weakness after a tentative labor deal
between Alcoa Inc. and the United Steelworkers was forged late Wednesday before the old contract expired.
Some cited talk of China's State Reserve Bureau possibly selling some of its copper stockpile, although
Chinese officials denied this.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Prices extended their losses, ending sharply lower after six nations reached an agreement on a
package of incentives and penalties to be presented to Iran. The July contract settled at $70.34, down 95
cents, or 1.3%, on the Nymex.
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex reacted harshly to Federal Reserve comments released
late Wednesday that hinted that another rise in interest rates is likely given increasing inflation, sending
gold to a six-week low of $625.70 an ounce. The benchmark August contract slid $15.50 to $633.50 an
ounce. Nearby June fell $14.90, or 2.3%, to $627.60.

Thursday's Markets
Stocks Surge as Inflation Fears Ease On Factory Data, Falling Oil Prices
By E.S. Browning
504 words
2 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS JUNE replaced May, hope replaced fear, and stocks moved strongly higher.
A fresh batch of economic reports spurred hopes that inflationary pressure might be easing, and the Dow
Jones Industrial Average, which has been under pressure for almost a month, rose 91.97 points, or 0.82%, to
11260.28. It is up 5.1% this year.
Investors shifted money to stocks they had fled. The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose beleaguered
technology stocks had pushed it into the red for 2006, surged 1.88%, or 40.98 points, to 2219.86. It is up for
the year again, by 0.7%.
Chain stores reported strong May sales, belying an earlier lukewarm report from Wal-Mart. The reports
softened fears that high gasoline costs were stifling consumer spending.
A wide variety of commodity prices fell, including oil and gold, easing inflation fears. Crude-oil futures fell 95
cents to $70.34 a barrel, while gold fell $14.90 to $627.60 a troy ounce.
A report on May manufacturing activity was lighter than expected, easing fears that a hot economy would
force the Federal Reserve to push short-term interest rates higher at its meeting June 28 and 29. New
jobless claims for last week came in stronger than expected, also helping calm inflation fears.
"The inflation scare has settled down a little bit," said Tim Smalls, head of U.S. stock trading at Greenwich,
Conn., brokerage firm Execution LLC. "As long as there are no negative surprises on some of the economic
numbers, I think the market is still in decent shape."
The reduced inflation fears pushed bond prices higher, sending yields lower. Falling yields help hold down a
variety of market interest rates, which also was good news for stocks.
Investors now will be examining this morning's much-awaited May hiring report, looking for signs of
inflationary pressure in this indicator, which is said to be much-watched by the Fed.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, whose 3.1% May decline was its worst monthly drop in two years,
rose 1.23%, or 15.62 points, to 1285.71, up 3% for 2006.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.20%, or 0.43 point, to 214.74.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.42 billion
shares, as 2,566 stocks rose and 720 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 5.108%. The 30-year bond was up 12/32 to yield 5.201%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 112.70 yen, up from 112.54, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2799 from $1.2816.

Dow Recovers; Major Indexes End May Poorer
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Recovers; Major Indexes End May Poorer
By E.S. Browning
516 words
1 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS RECOVERED some ground on May's last trading day, but not enough to protect the Dow Jones
Industrial Average from its worst monthly decline in 11 months.
The Dow industrials yesterday rose 73.88 points, or 0.67%, to 11168.31, down 1.7% in May but still up 4.2%
for 2006.
Amid continuing fears that resurgent inflation will force the Federal Reserve to slow the economy with more
interest-rate increases, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 3.1% in May -- its biggest monthly decline
since 2004. The S&P 500 yesterday was up 0.81%, or 10.25 points, to 1270.09, a gain of 1.7% this year.
Stocks gyrated following the afternoon release of minutes from the Fed's May 10 interest-rate meeting. The
minutes indicated that some Fed policy makers remained worried that inflationary pressures are lingering.
Some analysts feared that the Fed will be more likely to raise short-term rates again at its next meeting, June
28-29. Rising rates slow economic growth, putting pressure on corporate profits and making existing bonds
less remunerative than future ones.
But money managers, many of whom are required to be fully invested at month's end, continued buying
stocks following Tuesday's selloff, and that helped to push the market higher.
Bonds, however, reacted negatively to the prospect of higher rates.
Stocks benefited from a decline in oil futures, as tensions eased somewhat following the U.S. offer to join in
Europe's talks with Iran on its nuclear program. But crude-oil futures finished off their lows, down 74 cents at
$71.29 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold futures fell $11.40 to $642.50 a troy ounce.
Stocks' immediate prospects will depend on today's reports on May chain-store sales and manufacturing
activity, and tomorrow's data on May hiring and labor costs. Investors are hoping for signs that the economy
is cooling a bit on its own.
"You are going to have two pretty good days of economy-watching, with a lot of economic data out," said Bob
Basel, senior trader at Citigroup's New York brokerage arm. "How stocks do will depend on the economic
data."
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.65%, or 14.14 points, to 2178.88. But after its 6.2% May decline it is
down 1.2% so far this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.62 billion shares,
as 2,453 stocks rose and 850 fell.
Bonds prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.06 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 5.131%. The 30-year bond was down 19/32 to yield 5.227%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 112.54 yen, up from 112.22, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2816 from $1.2866.

Bush Taps Paulson as Treasury Chief --- Firm Expected to Elevate Blankfein, Underscoring A Shift Toward Trading --- Mr. Inside vs. Mr...
Bush Taps Paulson as Treasury Chief --- Firm Expected to Elevate Blankfein, Underscoring A Shift
Toward Trading --- Mr. Inside vs. Mr. Outside
By Susanne Craig and Randall Smith
1,409 words
31 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With the departure of Chief Executive Henry Paulson Jr., Goldman Sachs is expected to turn to No. 2 Lloyd
Blankfein -- replacing a classic, meet-and-greet investment banker with the sort of savvy trading manager
who has been on the rise on Wall Street.
Goldman's board of directors is expected to tap Mr. Blankfein, the firm's president, to succeed Mr. Paulson as
soon as this week, according to people close to the firm. Both were attending a previously scheduled
Goldman Sachs partners' retreat in Chicago yesterday, following President Bush's nomination of Mr. Paulson
as Treasury secretary. In a memo to Goldman employees, Mr. Paulson said he will remain at the firm during
the nomination process, which he expects to take six to eight weeks.
Mr. Blankfein, a sharp-witted history buff with detailed knowledge of the markets, is a former gold salesman
who made his name at Goldman by managing trading and risk-management operations -- the complex
business of profiting from hundreds of complicated bets on securities. Those operations have helped boost
Goldman to record profits in recent years, as the firm has put more of its own money at risk.
His manner contrasts sharply with that of the 60-year-old Mr. Paulson, a square-jawed former football player
who built his reputation as an investment banker skilled at wooing clients. The less-imposing Mr. Blankfein --
once known for padding around the office in his socks -- has devoted more time to clients since becoming
president in 2004, but his focus has remained on amping up Goldman's risk-management and trading
expertise.
His efforts have been crucial to Goldman. By emphasizing trading in recent years, the investment bank has
been able to hold its own on Wall Street despite being smaller -- with only 24,000 employees -- than some of
its peers. Recent record profits have kept it poised atop the Wall Street heap, with an industry-leading $5.63
billion in 2005.
The question now is whether that can continue as the markets get bumpier. The environment that Mr.
Blankfein so skillfully mined for profit was marked by low interest rates and easy credit. That looks to be
changing as the Federal Reserve continues to boost short-term interest rates. Meanwhile, investment
banking is booming again amid a wave of mergers and buyouts.
Lauren Smith, an analyst at Keefe, Bruyette &Woods Inc., notes that Goldman's trading revenue in
fixed-income, commodities and currencies doubled between 2002 and 2005, to $8.5 billion. It accounted for
about a third of total revenue last year, up from 18% in 2000. To continue growing off that lofty base "gets a
lot more challenging," she says. "It's riskier. We're in choppier markets right now than we were even six
weeks ago."
Part of the answer will rest on who else is elevated. Mr. Blankfein is known as a fiercely loyal executive and
yesterday many were speculating he could promote longtime friend Gary Cohn, co-head of the firm's
securities division, to a senior post. Insiders said he also is likely to promote a powerful banker, possibly
bringing back the co-president structure that existed before Mr. Blankfein became president.
Other contenders for president include Jon Winkelried, co-head of investment banking; J. Michael Evans,
president of Goldman Sachs Asia; Tom Montag, co-head of securities and co-head of Japan; Michael
Sherwood, co-head of securities and co-head of Europe; and Eric Schwartz, co-head of investment
management.
Promotions could help break a management logjam at the top levels of the firm. That could be good news for
Goldman, which despite its clout has in recent years lost a number of key executives, some frustrated that
their upward path was blocked -- Mr. Paulson had long indicated he planned to stay for some time. Just this

--- Page 63 ---
Page 63 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
month, George W. Walker, a top Goldman executive and cousin of President Bush, left for rival Lehman
Brothers Holdings Inc.
Goldman faces some other challenges. The firm has drawn increased fire recently for its handling of alleged
conflicts of interest -- for instance, when it acted as adviser to both sides in last year's merger of the New
York Stock Exchange and Archipelago Holdings.
It also has come under attack for increasingly investing its own capital alongside that of clients, which rivals
claim is a potential conflict. In early April, Mr. Paulson sent a rare edict to his management committee
warning that if Goldman became too aggressive in its own private-equity acquisitions, it could damage the
firm's standing with its clients.
Besides putting more of its own money on the line, making bets on everything from the direction of interest
rates to the price of coffee, Goldman has boosted its presence in "alternative" investments such as
private-equity and hedge funds. Other Wall Street firms had retreated from such areas until recently, citing
possible conflicts.
Nonetheless, Goldman hasn't been burned. In the first quarter of this year, its asset-management revenue
soared 99% to $1.45 billion. Roy Smith, a former Goldman partner who is now a finance professor at the New
York University business school, notes too that Goldman has avoided some problems encountered by other
big banks. For example, Goldman didn't play a prominent role in underwriting Enron Corp. or WorldCom Inc.,
sources of two of the biggest scandals of the stock-market bubble. Both were backed by many of Wall
Street's biggest banks.
The firm continues to rank at or near the top in most high-margin businesses, including merger advice and
underwriting of initial public offerings of stock. Though Goldman has slipped in some lower-profit areas such
as debt underwriting, where bigger commercial banks have muscled in, it ranked No. 1 globally in merger
advice and No. 3 in stock IPOs last year, according to Thomson Financial, which tracks investment-banking
transactions. It also ranked No. 1 in U.S. IPOs and No. 3 in U.S. investment-grade debt.
Goldman's stock also has stayed strong. Though its shares were down 2% to $149.83 in 4 p.m. New York
Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, over the past year they have risen about 52%.
Moreover, the nomination of Mr. Paulson only adds to the bank's unparalleled reputation for producing top
talent. In recent years, Goldman alumni filling powerful jobs have included former president John Thain, now
CEO of NYSE Group Inc., which runs the New York Stock Exchange; former CEO Jon Corzine, now the
governor of New Jersey; and Robert Rubin, who served as Treasury secretary under President Clinton and
now chairs the executive committee at Citigroup Inc. Another alumnus is current White House Chief of Staff
Josh Bolten, who played a role in convincing Mr. Paulson to head to Washington.
Mr. Blankfein's ascension to the same senior ranks has been forecast at least since late 2003, when he was
picked as president. It marks a remarkable rise that began in the Bronx, where he grew up in a housing
project, and included a degree from Harvard, which he attended on a scholarship.
The job substantially raised the executive's profile. Though a longtime employee of the firm whose holdings in
Goldman are valued at approximately $390 million, Mr. Blankfein hadn't attended an annual meeting until
2003, after he was named to the board of directors. After he was picked, as other executives mingled, he
went back to the office to work.
Since becoming president, Mr. Blankfein has continued to played Mr. Inside to Mr. Paulson's Mr. Outside, but
also has become much more involved with clients. He also has worked hard to increase his public presence.
He has shaved off his beard and shed about 50 pounds on a low-carbohydrate diet.
It has been noticed. "I have always respected his capital-markets expertise, and since he has become
president I have seen a real positive transformation in him," says Lehman Brothers Holding Inc. CEO Richard
Fuld Jr., one of Wall Street's longest serving top executives. "Today he is much more a leader of the full firm."
(See related article: "New Recruit: Goldman CEO Is Promised More Power Than Snow; Banker With Green
Bent --- A Big Test With the Dollar" -- WSJ May 31, 2006)

Dow Plunges 184 On New Concern Over Rising Rates --- Drop in Consumer Confidence, Wal-Mart's Weak Sales Hurt; Investors Can't Wait for June
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Plunges 184 On New Concern Over Rising Rates --- Drop in Consumer Confidence, Wal-Mart's
Weak Sales Hurt; Investors Can't Wait for June
By E.S. Browning
852 words
31 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SELL IN MAY and go away: The old Wall Street saying wasn't such bad advice after all.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average resumed its May pullback, falling 184.18 points, or 1.63%, to 11094.43
yesterday, its lowest finish in more than a month. The plunge reflected resurgent fears that slowing economic
growth caused by high oil prices and rising interest rates could put a lid on corporate profits. It was the
third-sharpest percentage daily decline of the year, with all 30 stocks in the Dow industrials falling for the first
time since 2004. Other indexes also dropped sharply.
The pullback left the Dow industrials down 2.4% for May. Unless the Dow recovers sharply, the monthly
decline will be the sharpest since April of last year, when the Dow fell 3%.
The immediate catalysts were a drop in consumer-confidence numbers for May and a disappointing May
sales report from Wal-Mart Stores, which Wal-Mart blamed in part on high gasoline prices. Making things
worse, crude-oil futures rose 66 cents to $72.03 a barrel, the highest finish since May 12 on the New York
Mercantile Exchange.
The Dow industrials still are up 3.5% this year. But stocks often are at their strongest from October through
April, and their weakest from May through September, which is the source of the widely quoted saying about
selling in May.
Stocks have been soft since May 10, when Federal Reserve policy makers warned that they could be forced
to continue raising short-term interest rates to hold down inflation. Making things worse yesterday was a
comment from Michael Moskow, president of the Chicago Federal Reserve Bank, that inflation is running in
the upper end of the range he considers acceptable, and that he would prefer to see it in the middle of the
range. That remark reinforced fears that the Fed will continue pushing short-term rates higher.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index has fallen 3.9% this month, and unless it recovers strongly today, it
will suffer its worst monthly percentage decline since 2002. It fell 1.59%, or 20.32 points, to 1259.84
yesterday, leaving it up just 0.9% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index, showing a decline of 6.8% so far
this month, is down 1.8% for 2006. Yesterday it fell 2.06%, or 45.63 points, to 2164.74.
Small stocks, which had outpaced larger stocks since 2002, fell even harder. The Russell 2000 small-stock
index, which had been setting records for months, declined 2.54%, or 18.51 points, to 711.04. It still is up
5.6% this year.
U.S. investors began the morning with news that foreign stock markets were down again. As declines in U.S.
stocks persisted through the day, many investors decided to sell now, for fear that things would get worse.
People are nervous about a series of economic reports due this week, including today's release of the
minutes of this month's Fed meeting. After that comes a report on manufacturing activity and prices
tomorrow, and another one on May hiring and unemployment levels, due Friday. The Fed is believed to
watch hiring levels closely as a sign of inflation risks. Investors worry that more bad news could send stocks
lower.
"The real selloff occurred in the last 45 minutes. People said: `Just get me out of this market. It isn't coming
back,' " said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at brokerage firm Cowen &Co. Big investment institutions
unloaded stock using computerized sell programs, causing a sharp increase in trading volume at day's end.
In addition to the interest-rate fears, Mr. Leone said, investors were nervous about news that, at a time when
markets are disrupted, Treasury Secretary John Snow will step aside and be succeeded by Henry Paulson,

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
chief executive of New York brokerage firm Goldman Sachs Group. "A new face scares people a little bit," Mr.
Leone said.

uncertainty about the U.S. economy.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Stocks Had Few Bright Spots in May --- Almost No Sector or Region Pulled...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Stocks Had Few Bright Spots in
May --- Almost No Sector or Region Pulled Free of the Undertow; Tech Suffers a Major Hit
By Peter A. McKay
699 words
30 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AN OLD SAW on Wall Street advises investors to "sell in May and go away." Seems like investors the world
over suddenly remembered that tip.
Almost regardless of what industry or country an investor might be dabbling in, stocks have suffered.
Technology stocks have been especially hard hit, with the Dow Jones World Technology Index off 6%, or
23.86 points, to 373.97 for the month. That drop was in line with the pullback in the widely followed Nasdaq
Composite Index, which has shed nearly 5%.
U.S. investors who turned to foreign shares found no redemption: The Dow Jones World Composite Index
has shed 3.4%, or 8.83 points, to 248.86 in May.
Dow Jones indexes tracking individual regions and sectors showed even bigger losses. For example,
European basic-materials stocks were off 12.8%, and the Japanese travel sector was off 5.6%.
There have been a few bright spots in which investors have eked out small gains, or at least broken even, but
they weren't big moneymakers by any means. For example, the Dow Jones Japan Utilities Index is up 2.6%,
or 2.44 points, to 97.11 this month.
Gold prices have gyrated, soaring first to multidecade highs of more than $720 early this month, then
plunging on expectations of a stronger dollar. For the month, gold futures are off just 0.1%, or 90 cents, at
$650.90 a troy ounce on the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division.
Analysts say that skittishness about the U.S. economy hurt a variety of stocks across the globe in an era in
which economies and markets are increasingly connected. Coming into the month, Wall Street pros had
expected that the Federal Reserve was nearing the end of its interest-rate increases, which effectively take
dollars out of circulation and prevent them from flowing to various investment products.
Recent data showing hints of inflation have prolonged worries that the Fed might continue to tighten. "In the
end, I don't think inflation will get the upper hand," says strategist Stuart Schweitzer, of J.P. Morgan Asset
&Wealth Management, adding that the central bank seems ready to "invoke a significant slowdown in the
U.S." that could crimp job growth, consumer spending and other measures.
Such a scenario in some ways echoes the observation that sometimes the medicine is worse than the
affliction it is trying to cure: The same steps the Fed will take to bat down inflation could put a lid on
companies' ability to increase revenue and profit.
In such uncertain times, so-called hot-money traders often move quickly in and out of different asset classes,
searching for returns and safety. Sure enough, speculators this month have fled from one economically
sensitive sector -- commodities -- but broad indexes tracking raw materials still are moderately higher. The
Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index is up 2%, or 3.53 points, at 179.3 for the month.
Many global market observers doubt the several-year uptrend in commodity prices has run its course. Walter
Pye, head of commodities trading at Macquarie Bank, says industrialization in China and a previous lack of
investment in global commodity production are part of a "fundamental story [that] is still very much in place."
For now, at least, commodities also are being helped by a lack of strength in the U.S. dollar. Because
commodities are traded globally in dollar terms, they often move in the opposite direction of the currency. The
stronger the dollar gets, the fewer it takes to purchase a given amount of commodities.
Whenever the Federal Reserve stops raising interest rates, most analysts believe that will help commodities
and hurt the dollar, as the supply of dollars will no longer be shrinking.

For the month so far, a trade-weighted J.P. Morgan index tracking the dollar's value against a basket of
foreign currencies is off 0.6%, or 0.5 point, at 88.3.

Turning Tide May Divide Markets --- `Correlation' in Global Investments Could End as Easy Money Dries Up; Bulls, Bears Fret Over What's Next
Abreast of the Market
Turning Tide May Divide Markets --- `Correlation' in Global Investments Could End as Easy Money
Dries Up; Bulls, Bears Fret Over What's Next
By E.S. Browning
1,204 words
30 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WALL STREET HAS a new buzzword: correlation.
That is a big word for a simple idea. A lot of the world's financial markets have been moving in the same
direction at the same time in recent years. For a long time, they all were up. Then earlier this month they fell
heavily, and now they have been tentatively rising again.
Since 2002, this kind of correlation has been good for investors. Expanding economies in the U.S., Europe
and Asia have supported one another and made stocks strong -- even stronger abroad than in the U.S.
Market correlations also have taken place among some commodities and currencies that usually move in
divergent directions.
What happens as economies show signs of slowing? Do the world's markets all turn down together? Or do
stocks and other investments start going their separate ways?
"The tide lifted all boats, but when the tide goes out there will be fewer places to put money," says Joseph
Quinlan, chief market strategist at Bank of America's global-wealth and investment-management group.
In recent months, the correlation between large U.S. stocks and developing-country stocks has been as high
as 70%, compared with a correlation of about 40% at the start of 2003, according to Ned Davis Research in
Venice, Fla.
Industrial commodities, which typically move independently of stocks, have sometimes moved in tandem with
them. Even gold and oil, which often are falling when stocks are rising, lately have had small positive
correlations with the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, Ned Davis Research reports. One explanation for the
increased correlations: Low interest rates have helped create a lot of cheap money, which has found its way
into a variety of investments.
One of the more odd recent parallels came last year, when gold and the dollar both rose. Gold often is
considered a hedge on dollar weakness, and it isn't supposed to track the dollar. To the relief of purists, the
dollar turned down earlier this year as gold continued rising -- until gold turned down, too.
Stocks have been wavering this month because of a global version of what Americans call "fear of the Fed."
Investors fear the Federal Reserve, seeking to prevent inflation, will raise interest rates high enough to stifle
economic growth. Authorities in Europe, Japan and even China also show signs of wanting to raise local
interest rates to cool their economies.
The world-wide bull market has been to a large degree the result of those central banks' easy-money policies.
If they begin raising rates and mopping up that easy money, which they seem to have begun to do, the
world's economies will feel the effect together.
With interest rates higher and money less easy to get, investors will withdraw from some investments. The
stocks that have been the big leaders -- those tied to economic growth and to industrial-commodity
production -- could face head winds.
Investors had a respite from the May selling this past week, as U.S. stocks finished with three days of gains.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 134.55 points, or 1.2%, for the week, to close Friday at 11278.61.
The market was closed yesterday for the Memorial Day holiday. Foreign stocks also finished the week with
rebounds. But while stocks in Britain and Brazil joined the Dow industrials in showing a net gain for the week,
Indian and Japanese indexes declined.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In Europe yesterday, the pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 Index slipped 0.1%, nearing the end of a
month during which it has lost 4% of its value. London was closed for a holiday.
Among Europe's emerging markets, which plunged this month on fears of higher interest rates, Prague's
PX50 slipped 0.8%. In Moscow, the dollar-denominated RTS Index fell 0.2%.
Now, the investing world finds itself divided into three groups. There are the true pessimists, who fear that
stocks might have peaked earlier this month and that this past week's U.S. recovery is a temporary respite. In
that view, the world's stock markets rose together, and now they are going to fall together.
"If it is going to be a significant decline, you would expect it to be global in scope," says Tim Hayes, chief
investment strategist at Ned Davis Research. He says he and fellow Ned Davis analysts are debating the
market's future and are concerned. The bull market is getting old, with the number of stocks participating in
the gains and the number reaching new highs narrowing. Mr. Hayes worries that tight monetary conditions,
aimed at cooling economies, often foreshadow serious stock-market declines.
"We don't have enough evidence to say the market has peaked, but we certainly think the risks are high," Mr.
Hayes says. The next few weeks could give a signal of whether the bull market is over, he says, as stocks
either continue rebounding or break below their recent lows. If a true bear market -- a 20% decline from a
high -- begins, Mr. Hayes says, the safest place to hide would be in bonds.
Then there are those who say the recent market turmoil marks a new phase of the bull market. They don't
see a bear market on the horizon, but they do say investors have to be pickier in choosing stocks. Mr.
Quinlan of Bank of America says he believes U.S. stocks could benefit from troubles in developing-country
stocks as investors retreat to safer waters.
"The U.S. is a good place to hide," he says. U.S. multinational companies in particular would benefit from a
weakening dollar, which makes U.S. exports cheaper against foreign competitors. He likes big,
dividend-paying stocks such as Caterpillar, involved in "moving dirt, building roads, transportation." He also
likes broadly based, service-oriented multinational companies such as International Business Machines.
Some in this camp expect the current market gyrations could mark the start of a prolonged period of volatility,
with stocks bouncing up and down until some time in the autumn, close to the November congressional
elections.
Finally, there are those who say the recent pullbacks were a much-needed correction that sets stocks up for
more gains. In that view, the moderate financial crackdown by the world's central banks hasn't been enough
to stifle growth, which should leave most stocks with plenty of room to rise.
"Our analysis suggests that global growth going forward is solid and inflation is likely to be contained. Stay
the course, and remain moderately bullish," says Tim Swanson, chief investment officer at the private-client
group of Cleveland banking group National City, in a note to clients.
Adds Sophia Collier, portfolio manager of the Citizens Value Fund of Portsmouth, N.H. "The good news is
that the economy is still strong and the market is quite reasonably valued right now. It is time to start looking
around in attractively valued stocks in oil services, financials and awakening giants like a 3M and a Johnson
&Johnson."

HONG KONG -- Asian stock markets advanced Friday as Japan and South Korea led battered regional

European shares also rallied.
Steelmakers were lifted by consolidation hopes after Russia's Severstal and Luxembourg-based Arcelor said
they'd merge to form the world's biggest steel company.
Japan's Nippon Steel, the world's No. 3 steel producer, gained 2.6%, while Posco, of South Korea, added
1.8%. China's Baoshan Iron &Steel gained 1.4% in Shanghai trading.
In SYDNEY, the world's top miner, BHP Billiton jumped 4%. BHP Friday announced new pricing agreements
with steel customers in Europe, Japan and Korea, committing to a 19% increase in iron-ore prices. The
pricing agreement was in line with earlier agreements struck between steel producers and Australia's No. 2
miner, Rio Tinto, which gained 4.6%.
Around the region, South Korea's Kospi Index led the region's major markets, gaining 2% to 1322.43. Japan's
Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies rose 277.01 points, or 1.8%, to 15970.76. The broader Topix index
of Tokyo-listed shares gained 29.07 points, or 1.8%, to 1613.78.
Shanghai's Composite Index, which includes A and B shares, advanced 1.4% to 1613.894. Singapore's
Straits Times Index was up 1.7%. India's 30-stock Sensitive Index ended up 1.3% at 10809.35. Hong Kong's
Hang Seng Index was up 1.3% at 15895.10.
"The overall sentiment has improved," said Castor Pang, a strategist with SHK Financial Group in Hong
Kong. He cautioned that head winds are still against the markets. "People are still concerned about
commodity prices. Gold and copper are not stable, and this should have a negative effect upon H shares,
Japanese stocks, and emerging markets." He said that attention from Monday would focus squarely on the
prospects for additional interest-rate hikes in the U.S.
In TOKYO, the financial sector rose after a release by Standard &Poor's Ratings Services that showed
Japan's big banks returning to fiscal heath. Resona Holdings was up 7.3%, while Mitsubishi UFJ added 1.4%.
Oil player Inpex Corp., which has signed development agreements for oil fields in Iran, added 3.4%. Games
maker Nintendo rose 9.7%, a day after announcing strong profits for fiscal 2005 but predicting a profit drop
this year.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 index added 2%, or 113.30 points, to 5791.00, as other major European markets
also rallied, helped by a second straight session of benign U.S. core-inflation data. Mining stocks rose, as
did home builders including Persimmon, George Wimpey, and Barratt Developments.

Dow Industrials Climb 93.73 Points As Economic Data Come in Just Right
By Ian McDonald
607 words
26 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
EMBOLDENED by just-right readings on the economy, investors gave the Dow Jones Industrial Average a
two-day winning streak for its 110th birthday.
Jittery traders cheered a Commerce Department report showing ample economic growth in the first quarter,
but not enough to stoke the inflation and interest-rate-increase fears that rattled the markets for two weeks.
The Dow industrials gained 93.73 points, or 0.84%, to 11211.05 amid a flurry of late-afternoon buying. It was
the industrial average's biggest gain in almost three weeks. The Dow average, which made its debut on May
26, 1896, is up 4.6% on the year and is back within 4% of its all-time high of 11722.98 that it neared on May
10.
"After a difficult couple of weeks, stocks were oversold," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at
money-management group T. Rowe Price. "The economic figures made people comfortable."
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 14.31, or 1.14%, to 1272.88, while the tech-laden Nasdaq
Composite Index gained 29.07 or 1.34% to 2198.24. Small stocks posted the biggest gains with the Russell
2000 adding 14.31 points, or 2.01%, to 725.58.
Commodities recovered from declines posted Wednesday. Crude oil gained $1.46, or 2.09%, to $71.32 a
barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold advanced $11.40, or 1.79%, to $648.40 a troy ounce on
the Comex division of the Nymex. Crude and gold are up 17% and 25%, respectively, so far this year.
Bond prices and the dollar slipped.
Traders' spirits were buoyed before the market opened when the Commerce Department raised its estimate
of the economy's first-quarter growth, but left inflation measures mostly unchanged. Investors also digested
unsurprising tallies of jobless claims and existing-home sales.
A deal between two behemoths helped pushed technology shares higher. Online giants Yahoo and eBay
unveiled a partnership to better compete with search-engine giant Google Inc. Shares of Yahoo and eBay
rose 3.6% and 12%, respectively, on the Nasdaq Stock Market.
Markets didn't seem to react when a federal jury in Houston found former Enron Corp. Chairman Kenneth Lay
and former Enron President Jeffrey Skilling guilty of conspiracy and fraud charges stemming from that
company's 2001 collapse.
Still, yesterday's gains may be a brief respite, with investors scrutinizing each economic data point. "This is
the fox-trot economy," says Jeffrey Saut, chief investment strategist at Raymond James. "One figure is fast
and the next is slow."
Shares of General Electric, the only initial Dow component included in the current industrial average, rose 16
cents or 0.5%, to $34.42 on the New York Stock Exchange.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.58, or 0.75%, to 212.95.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the Big Board was 2.46 billion shares, as 2,541 issues
rose and 745 fell.

Rarity for Stocks: an `Up' Day --- Dow's Gain Is First Since Friday; Nasdaq Continues a Turnaround

The Wall Street Journal

C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS FINISHED HIGHER for the first time since Friday, after faltering through much of the day as
investors remained anxious about the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 18.97 points, or 0.2%, to close at 11117.32, up 3.73% for the year.
It was only the third gain in the past 10 sessions for the Dow, which is off about 4.5% from its recent high on
May 10. At the time, it was tantalizingly close to its all-time record, 11722.98.
At its low point yesterday, the Dow industrials were off almost 68 points, or 0.6%.
The turnaround this month has been even more dramatic for the Nasdaq Composite Index, which yesterday
gained 0.5%, or 10.41 points, to end at 2169.17. But the technology-stock-heavy index has plunged 7.5%
from its high earlier this month and has fallen into negative territory for the year, off 1.6%.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index yesterday rose 0.2%, or 1.99 points, to 1258.57, up 0.8% for the year.
The Commerce Department said orders for durable goods -- jargon for big-ticket consumer products like
refrigerators and furniture -- fell 4.8% in April. The decline was larger than expected, yet investors seemed to
disagree whether that was good or bad for stocks.
Bears take such signs of economic weakness as bad news for most businesses -- thus a signal to sell stocks.
Bulls believe that any economic "softening" might dissuade the Federal Reserve from raising interest rates
further, a reason to buy stocks.
The problem, said strategist Jack Caffrey, of J.P. Morgan Private Bank, is that the next meeting of the Fed's
policymakers is still more than a month away, with several other pieces of economic data due beforehand
that may yield more surprises.
In congressional testimony Tuesday, Federal Reserve Chairman Benjamin Bernanke said the central bank
shares investors' concerns about inflation but will remain very "data dependent" until its next meeting.
"It's not exactly the calming words a skittish market wants to hear," Mr. Caffrey said.
Mr. Caffrey said he does expect the Fed to raise its key interest rate by another quarter point, to 5.25%, at
policymakers' next meeting. until then, he believes the stock market will remain volatile, awaiting the Fed's
commentary as a more definitive statement about the state of the economy.
Gold futures for May delivery plunged $36, or 5.3%, to $637 a troy ounce, but still up 23.2% on the year on
the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division.
George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures, said a wave of selling gold started in
Asia, where worries are spreading about the avian-flu virus. An outbreak of that virus would tend to deflate
the region's economy, Mr. Gero said, which would be bad for gold, traditionally considered an alternative
form of money.
He said yesterday's wave of gold selling, once it hit the U.S., also was driven by big funds that wanted to lock
in their profits from gold's recent run to multi-decade highs.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 3.13 billion shares,
as 1,385 issues rose and 1,943 fell.

CAPITAL: How Will Bernanke Ride the Storms of 2006?
CAPITAL:
The Economy
CAPITAL: How Will Bernanke Ride the Storms of 2006?
By David Wessel
879 words
25 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
EVERY SO OFTEN, economic forces and financial markets collide in ways that make for a tumultuous year --
the stock market crash in 1987, the Asian financial crisis and bond-market paralysis in 1998, the bursting
stock bubble in 2000.
Suddenly, this year has all the ingredients of a Big One.
The dollar is sinking. Global stock markets are volatile. Bond-market interest rates are climbing. Oil prices
are up. Gold is at a quarter-century high. Housing prices are softening. Protectionist pressures are
intensifying. General Motors Corp. is a candidate for bankruptcy court. Iran may be on the verge of going
nuclear. A nasty, partisan congressional election looms. And a new Federal Reserve chairman's
inflation-fighting resolve is being tested.
Alan Greenspan certainly picked a good time to retire.
Just a few months ago, the U.S. economy seemed to be shaking off the ill effects of Hurricane Katrina and
shrugging off higher energy prices. For the first time in years, there were signs of growth in Japan and hints of
optimism about Europe. Emerging-market stocks were soaring. India's Sensex index rose 34% from the start
of the year through mid-May.
Summing up the past few months, economists at the Organization for Economic Cooperation and
Development said: "The world economy has once more confirmed its ability to withstand shocks and maintain
momentum."
In the past couple of weeks, however, the wind has shifted abruptly. The Dow Jones Industrial Average
peaked on May 10 and has fallen about 5% since. India's stock market has plunged 16% in two weeks. "We
told you it was too good to last," the Cassandras are chanting. And the OECD, while reiterating an upbeat
forecast, allowed this week that "risks surrounding this scenario have increased."
WHAT GIVES? Some of this is just the way financial markets behave: periods of euphoria punctuated by
episodes of despair. But there is more to the recent mood change -- and a lot hinges on how deftly Fed
Chairman Ben Bernanke steers the U.S. economy.
Clearly, a long period of easy money and very low interest rates -- a deliberate strategy to ward off the risks
of Japanese-style deflation, an unwelcome downward spiral in prices -- fueled borrowing and speculation in
markets from zinc to Florida condominiums. That chapter is over.
The threat du jour is the return of inflation, hints of which show up almost daily. So the Fed and other central
banks are boosting interest rates, and that has pricked some asset-price bubbles.
But markets appear convinced, depending on the moment, either that Mr. Bernanke will worry too much
about inflation and raise rates so much that he chokes off economic growth or that he will worry too little
about inflation and raise rates so hesitantly that the price stability of recent years will seem to have retired
with Mr. Greenspan.
Trying to explain the latest market gyrations, economist Richard Berner of Morgan Stanley writes: "Mr. Market
was beginning to sniff out a scenario reminiscent of 1987, with rising inflation risks, a falling dollar and Fed
officials seemingly more concerned about downside risks to growth than upside risks to inflation." In a world
of huge flows of money and sophisticated computers, so much still seems to turn on the judgment of a few
individuals, especially the one who walks in the shoes of the Great Greenspan.

--- Page 81 ---
Page 81 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A few decades ago, in the time of the Great Inflation, central bankers didn't look so smart or so potent. Arthur
Burns, Fed chairman from 1970 to 1978, titled a 1979 lecture, "The Anguish of Central Banking." In it, he
despaired that political, social and economic forces had rendered central bankers ineffective in combating
"the chronic inflation of our times." (Among near-term worries at the time, he said, were "political convulsions
in Iran . . . enormous new increases in oil prices . . . limited progress in developing an effective energy policy
in the U.S. . . . reacceleration of inflation.")
PAUL VOLCKER, who had been Fed chairman for just two months, was in the audience. He was secretly
preparing to do what Mr. Burns deemed impossible: to break the back of inflation, at the cost of a
devastating recession, by pushing interest rates up to double-digit levels. That restored the reputation of
central bankers, though not their popularity. In 1990, with a nod to Mr. Burns, Mr. Volcker gave a lecture to
the same forum. He titled it, "The Triumph of Central Banking?"
Dan Yergin, who is both an energy analyst and a sharp historian, was ruminating a few weeks ago on the
reasons the global economy was weathering this energy storm better than those of the 1970s and 1980s.
"The shorthand answer is that central bankers are smarter," he said. This time, he said hopefully, they won't
panic into raising rates and triggering a recession just because oil prices are up, but they won't let higher oil
prices infect the global economy with virulent inflation either.
The markets seemed to think the same for a while. Now they aren't so sure.

AFTER STAGING a tentative rebound for most of the day, stocks couldn't hold their gains. A late-day selloff

Fighting Global Undertow, Dow Slides 18.73
Monday's Markets
Fighting Global Undertow, Dow Slides 18.73
By E.S. Browning
458 words
23 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
