C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN ANOTHER VOLATILE day, the Dow Jones Industrial Average initially plunged 103.99 points in the wake of
sharp stock declines abroad, but then bounced back, finishing down just 18.73 points.
U.S. stocks fell hard at first, following the lead of Asian and European markets. But as European markets
began to close, a variety of commodity futures including oil and gold, which also had been sharply down,
recovered their losses. Investors began to put money back into stocks as the Dow industrials neared the
psychologically important 11000 level and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index almost hit 1250. Those two
indexes briefly went into positive territory, and then sagged to finish the day slightly down.
The Dow industrials finished down just 0.17% at 11125.33, still up 3.8% in 2006. They have fallen 4.4% since
hitting a six-year high of 11642.65 on May 10, the sharpest eight-session drop in two years. The S&P 500 fell
0.39%, or 4.96 points, to 1262.07, up 1.1% this year. Trading volume was heavy.
The more-volatile Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.96%, or 21.02 points, to 2172.86, down 1.5% for 2006.
Bond yields continued to decline, helping ease fears that rising interest rates could stifle economic growth.
The yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note dipped briefly below 5% and finished at 5.042%, its lowest
level in late New York trading since April 24.
U.S. stocks could benefit from worries that foreign markets have risen too much on speculative investment,
said Matthew Johnson, head of stock syndication at Lehman Brothers. "The U.S. market is going to be a
place where money gets safely parked," he said.
Stocks benefited from comments by the president of the Dallas Federal Reserve Bank, who said a more

Investors now nervously await Friday's report of April inflation based on consumer-spending data, thought to
be closely watched by the Fed.

fell 1.87%, or 4.03 points, to 211.50.

against the dollar to $1.2869 from $1.2774.

Jitters Over Rising Interest Rates Send Emerging Markets Tumbling
Jitters Over Rising Interest Rates Send Emerging Markets Tumbling
By Craig Karmin
1,200 words
23 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks in developing countries tumbled yesterday, extending one of their biggest losing streaks in nearly a
decade, as growing jitters about the global economic outlook amid rising interest rates prompted investors to
abandon riskier markets world-wide.
The pullback in markets as far-flung as Turkey, India, Russia and Brazil has broad ramifications for U.S.
investors. Emerging-market stocks have been the world's top performers over the past three years, soaring
nearly 200%, based on the Morgan Stanley Capital International Emerging Market Index, a key yardstick.
During that period, U.S. investors, led by hedge funds and more aggressive mutual funds, have poured
money into even the riskiest countries at a record pace in search of better returns than they could obtain at
home.
Yesterday, as many of those same investors sold across the board, some of the highest-flying emerging
markets became the biggest losers. Russia's RTS Index declined 9%, and the Turkish market fell 8%. By
midday in India, stocks were down 10% -- enough to trigger an automatic halt to trading there -- though they
came back later to close down 4.2%. Some analysts speculated that government-controlled investment funds
started buying to bolster the market.
Overall, the MSCI Emerging Market Index fell 4.6% yesterday, its largest one-day decline in two years,
erasing $86 billion in market capitalization. The index has declined 15% since hitting its highest level ever just
two weeks ago. Emerging-market bonds have also taken a hit over the past two weeks, and currencies like
the Turkish lira and the Brazilian real have retreated more than 9% against the dollar.
Driving the sell-off: Expectations that central banks around the world, from the U.S. to Europe and eventually
Japan, may be preparing to raise rates more aggressively than previously anticipated. News last week that
the U.S. consumer-price index rose faster than expected raised the specter that interest rates will have to
climb further to curb inflation. Even the recent retreat in commodity prices has not been enough to blunt
inflation fears, and since many developing countries are commodity producers, these declines have weighed
on their economic outlooks.
Selling by hedge funds and other more speculative investors is aggravating emerging-market losses. These
investors, who aim to lock in short-term gains, will often sell their best stocks to ensure profits and cover any
losses in other positions. So a selloff in India may cause pain in Russia or Brazil, despite different economic
outlooks and stock valuations.
And given the current global economic scenario, "these markets are going to be in for a rough couple of
months," said Carlos Asilis, a Miami-based portfolio manager for Vega Plus Capital Partners, which has $2
billion under management.
The ripple effects are being felt in developed markets. Yesterday in Europe, the London market's FTSE 100
Index fell 2.2%, the German DAX Xetra 30 Index fell the same, and the French CAC-40 Index slid 2.7%. And
while the Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped just 0.2% yesterday, stocks have been down sharply since
May 10, when the Federal Reserve indicated it may not be done raising rates. U.S. government bonds --
considered the least risky investment -- edged higher as some investors sought safety.
A big contributor to the rout in emerging-markets stocks can be traced to Japan, where central-bank officials
earlier this year indicated they were preparing to end a long period of near-zero interest rates. Those
extraordinarily low rates -- part of Japan's effort to pull out of a decade-long economic slump -- had become
an important and lucrative tool for savvy global investors in recent years. Many investors had been borrowing
money in Japan (at almost no cost to them), and then investing in high-yield debt in New Zealand, Iceland,
Turkey and other overseas markets. The popular maneuver became known as the "carry trade."

--- Page 89 ---
Page 89 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One of the first signs that the prospects of higher interest rates were unraveling this strategy struck Iceland
in February, when vast sums of foreign money were pulled out of that tiny economy, sending the stock market
and currency tumbling there. Since then, gold and other commodities have also been slumping after big
run-ups in recent years.
While emerging markets haven't been battered this badly since the financial crisis of 1997-98 -- when a series
of Asian currency devaluations and Russia's debt default roiled markets around the world -- fund managers
point out that much has changed since then. In the intervening years, many developing countries have turned
trade deficits into surpluses and have allowed their currencies to trade more freely, giving their economies
greater flexibility and helping to insulate them from selloffs as violent as those of the past decade.
"That theory is now being put to the test," says Jack Ablin, chief investment officer for Harris Private Bank in
Chicago, with $48 billion in assets under management.
He remains cautiously bullish on emerging-market stocks, with 10% of the firm's stock portfolio still invested
in developing countries. His reasoning is that share valuations still look more attractive in these markets than
in the U.S. Also, emerging-market economies are expected to grow faster than developed economies in
coming years. India, for example, is still expected to record some of the strongest economic and profit growth
in the world this year.
Nevertheless, many hedge funds -- lightly regulated investment pools that cater to wealthy investors -- have
decided that after years of profiting in emerging markets, there's little incentive for them to stick around to see
what happens next. In recent days, they have been among the markets' most active sellers, though they
could easily jump back in when they see brighter prospects. Mr. Asilis, the hedge-fund manager, says it
would take a 15% to 20% drop from current levels to tempt him to buy emerging-market stocks.
Some investors said they are simply selling and hoarding cash until things calm down. Vladimir Milev, an
analyst for the Metzler/Payden European Emerging Markets Fund in Los Angeles, said that 20% of his $90
million fund is now in cash, up from nearly nothing a month ago.
Whether emerging-market fever among U.S. individual investors will persist after the recent declines remains
to be seen. In 2005, these investors poured a record $13.8 billion of net new money into emerging-market
stock funds, according to AMG Data Services -- a record surpassed in just the first four months of this year.
As of last week, investors had put $19 billion into these funds.
Russia's stock market, one of the hottest performers this year as investors flocked to companies like state
gas monopoly OAO Gazprom, was among the hardest hit yesterday.
"This is part of a global trend," said Chris Weafer, analyst at Alfa Bank in Moscow. "$30 billion has been
absorbed into global emerging markets to date this year, compared to just over $20 billion for all of last year.
That's driven markets up faster and higher."
---
Eric Bellman and Gregory L. White contributed to this article.

Miners Hit in the Global Rout --- India, Russia Halt Trading After Indexes Fall Sharply
World Stock Markets
Miners Hit in the Global Rout --- India, Russia Halt Trading After Indexes Fall Sharply
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
895 words
23 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal

C12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT WAS HARD to find a global stock market that did well yesterday.
Even as emerging-markets shares were pounded, in a selloff that came as investors appeared to decide they
had had enough of the high-risk markets for now, many developed-country indexes also pulled back by 2% or
more.
In Europe, as traders watched India, Russia and other emerging markets slide, the main markets weren't
immune.
The pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 Index fell 1.6% and is back in the red for the year to date. Mining
stocks were hammered, especially copper miners.
In LONDON, shares of miners Kazakhmys and Xtrata were both down 7.1% and Antofagasta was off 6.7%.
Among national markets, the U.K. FTSE 100 fell 2.2%, or 124.70 points, to 5532.70. Germany's DAX dropped
2.2%, or 126.04, to 5546.24. In Paris the CAC 40 shed 2.7%, or 131.07, to 4813.50.
Europe's emerging markets suffered more. Prague's PX-50 Index skidded 5.9% to 1345.7, and Warsaw's
WIG20 Index dropped 5.6% to 2862.68. The BUX Index in Budapest lost 3.9% to 21351.83. Turkey's
IMKB-100 plunged 7.6%.
The recent slide in commodities prices and fears that global interest rates could rise further -- slowing growth
in those countries and drawing U.S. and other investors back to countries where less-risky bonds are
becoming increasingly attractive -- contributed to investors' nervousness. The selling in India and Russia
spilled over to emerging markets in Europe, and then to Latin America, traders said.
In TOKYO, falling commodities prices also weighed on Tokyo's benchmark Nikkei Stock Average of 225
companies. The index lost 1.8%, or 297.58 points, to 15857.87. It was the first time the Nikkei closed below
16000 in more than two months.
The Japanese market was pounded by a slump in stocks of raw-material-related firms. Decliners included
Sumitomo Metal Mining, off 3.9%, and Nippon Oil, which shed 2.4%. Investors also dumped industrial robot
maker Fanuc, electronics-parts producer TDK and other tech exporters amid concerns about the U.S.
economy.
Latin American markets were under pressure. In SAO PAULO, late in the day, Brazil's Bovespa index was
down 3.3%, or 1235.94 points, to 36496.92. Mining giant Companhia Vale do Rio Doce fell 1.29%.
In MEXICO CITY, shares of Grupo Mexico, one of the world's largest copper miners, dropped 4.07%. The
IPC benchmark stock index retreated 4.03, or 812.85 points, to 19369.29. It was its steepest one-day drop
since Sept. 19, 2002. In addition to the damage done to mining stocks by the recently falling prices for
copper, gold, oil and other commodities, cellphone operator America Movil, the most heavily weighted stock
in the IPC index, plunged 6.1%.
The main focus was in India and Russia, of course. In MUMBAI, trading was suspended for an hour after the
benchmark index fell 10% at one point, its daily limit. The Sensex index ended down 4.2%, bringing its losses
to about 17% since hitting a record earlier this month.
In MOSCOW, the Micex exchange halted trading after its main index fell 11% late in the day. The nine-day
selloff there has sliced off more than a quarter of the value of Russian stocks.
Market heavyweight Gazprom lost 12%, or $29 billion, in value.

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The ruble-denominated Micex exchange suspended trading until this morning. Yesterday's fall was the
biggest in percentage terms since the arrest in October 2003 of Mikhail Khodorkovsky, the then-chief
executive of oil major Yukos who was later jailed for tax evasion and fraud.
The dollar-denominated RTS Index, the market's main benchmark, tumbled 9%, bringing losses since it
peaked on May 10 to 27%. Despite that, the RTS is still ahead 17% for the year to date.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index plunged 3.1%, or 507.84 points, to 15805.52, dropping below the

In SYDNEY, Australian mining stocks were slammed, pushing the S&P/ASX 200 lower by 1.4%, to 5030.8.
Smaller, less diversified resource stocks were hit particularly hard. Zinifex skidded nearly 15%, and Oxiana
fell almost 12%.

Bears Ponder How Far Stocks Might Pull Back
Abreast of the Market
Bears Ponder How Far Stocks Might Pull Back
By E.S. Browning
999 words
22 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FOR WEEKS, conservative investors have been warning that the stock market was overdue for a pullback of
5% to 10%, perhaps even more. Now, that pullback seems to be under way, and Wall Street is trying to figure
out how bad it will be.
The optimists already are reassuring clients that the worst is over and that stocks will resume their gains. The
pessimists point to signs that hot-money investors have been selling first and asking questions later, dumping
some of the risky investments to which they had flocked in the past few years.
"Market bears are coming out of the woodwork," said Nick Raich, research director at Cleveland banking
group National City's private-client group, in a report to clients.
Mr. Raich dismissed the doomsday predictions he has seen, including more interest-rate increases, a dollar
collapse, a stock rout, popping bubbles in housing and commodities and a recession.
"We cannot define at this time whether a cyclical bear market (a decline of 20% or more) is at hand or
whether a well-deserved correction (a decline of 10% to 20%) may be in place," wrote Louise Yamada,
founder of Louise Yamada Technical Research Advisors.
The S&P 500's decline from this month's five-year high, currently 4.4%, "is likely to reach the minus 10%
threshold," wrote Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage house Miller Tabak in a
report to his clients.
Among the hardest-hit investments are those that had benefited from speculative excess. India's main
stock-market index finished last week down 13.3% from its May high, Brazil's was down 10.1% and
Singapore's was down 6.2%. Silver futures have plunged 17%, zinc 14% and copper 9.7%. Gold, which
historically has been a refuge in times of trouble but which lately has been a magnet for speculators, was off
8.8%.
The volatile Nasdaq index has fallen 6.4% since May 8. In contrast, the Dow industrials are down 4.3% since
May 10, when the index hit a six-year high.
Several things make investors worry:
At the top of the list is inflation, which some analysts fear will force the Federal Reserve to keep raising
interest rates into summer or even autumn. Past Fed rate increases sometimes have gone too far,
provoking shocks such as a bear market, a real-estate collapse or a recession.
In each of the past two months, "core" inflation, which excludes food and fuel, has increased more than 2%
from a year earlier. That is above the level Fed officials have indicated they consider acceptable, suggesting
the Fed could keep raising rates to cool the economy and thereby tamp down inflationary pressures. Fed
officials have indicated that they are determined not to create an economic shock this time, but the inflation
numbers may put them in a bind.
What made the stock market boom this year was the prospect that the Fed would stop raising rates at a time
when the economy and corporate profits continued to surge. Analysts currently expect profits at big
companies to rise 14% or more in the second half of this year. If the Fed is forced to cool the economy
further, profits could suffer, and the Wall Street euphoria could fade further.
Since early 2003, when anxiety over the Iraq war knocked stocks down, the Dow industrials haven't fallen
10% from a high. Many consider such a decline overdue. They note that the April and May stock gains were
led by a limited group of stocks that were thought to benefit most from low interest rates and a strong
economy. Such narrow gains often are a sign that trouble could be lurking.

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The optimists say that, even if major indexes are in a 5% to 10% pullback, the worst is over. "Before we start
to panic and jump on the doomsayers' bandwagon, let's take a look at the facts, figures and trends," National
City's Mr. Raich wrote to clients. "Global demand is strong, core inflation remains well contained" and stock
prices are attractive compared with expected profit gains, he wrote. As long as world growth remains strong,
so should stock prices.
The prices of stocks in the S&P 500 have fallen to 14.3 times forecast profits for the next 12 months from a
ratio of 15 before the recent declines, according to Thomson Financial, which tracks profit forecasts. That
makes stocks "drastically undervalued," Thomson research analyst David Dropsey said. If profits don't miss
expectations, stocks could well rebound.
What is more, Fed officials have gone out of their way to indicate that they want to pause in their interest-rate
increases and see whether the delayed impact of their 16 previous rate boosts will cool the economy without
further actions on their part. If the Fed is able to pause at its next interest-rate meeting, on June 28 and 29, or
even if it indicates plans to pause when it meets again on Aug. 8, investors could take that as a sign that it is
safe to bid stocks higher.
The June meeting is some time off, and for now, investors remain jittery. In March, demand for sturdy
Swiss-franc investments was light, a sign that investors weren't seeking havens, State Street Global Markets
notes in a report. Last week, demand for Swiss francs surged.
Demand for another safe investment, Treasury bonds, also jumped, which worriers took as another sign of
market jitters. The rising Treasury-bond prices pushed yields lower. But that indicator cuts both ways.
Generally, when bond investors fear inflation, they sell bonds and push yields higher, figuring that market
interest rates will rise and that new bonds will carry higher rates. Their willingness to buy bonds at lower
yields suggested inflation fears may not be as widespread among bond investors as among stock investors.

The dollar's ability to extend its rally this week is largely dependent on what happens in other investment

Declines in commodity and stock prices led investors to unwind negative dollar bets, pushing the dollar into a

With volatility likely to persist, particularly in the precious-metals market, and uncertainty about the Federal
Reserve's position on rate increases befuddling investors, the battle this week will be fought in the
commodities and stock markets, analysts said.
Any short-term dollar strengthening most likely will come as a result of investors looking for a haven currency

There has been a brief respite for the dollar, but "the downside is more the way to go," said Brian Dolan,

Late Friday, the euro was at $1.2774 down from $1.2821 late Thursday. The dollar was at 111.74 yen, up
from 111.03 yen late Thursday. The euro was about 142.74 yen, up from 142.36 yen. The British pound was
trading at $1.8779, down from $1.8907.

Japanese currency is likely to stay between 110.5 yen and 113.5 yen to the dollar, according to analysts.

Friday. Gold has fallen 11% in a week from its recent 26-year high of $730 an ounce.
"Once volatility picks up, it has a cycle of carrying itself through," Mr. Dolan said. "More likely than not,
volatility will continue [this] week as long as position adjustments continue in the metals and emerging
markets."
The foreign-exchange market ended the week unclear about the Fed's intentions at its June meeting, even
after several Federal Reserve officials toward the end of the week affirmed the importance of fighting
inflation.

Style -- Luxury Goods: Why Jewelry Is Slimming Down --- High gold and silver costs aren't just raising prices -- they're changing styles
PURSUITS
Style -- Luxury Goods: Why Jewelry Is Slimming Down --- High gold and silver costs aren't just
raising prices -- they're changing styles
By Cheryl Lu-Lien Tan
1,012 words
20 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
P6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
LINKS OF LONDON sells jewelry made of gold, silver or a "bimetal" combination. A Pursuits article Saturday
on how the high cost of gold and silver is changing jewelry designs incorrectly implied that the company no
longer sells pieces made solely of gold and silver.
(WSJ May 26, 2006)
(END)
JEWELRY DESIGNER Geoff Thomas is best known for chunky silver cuffs and necklaces with spear-like
pendants that are several inches long. His latest designs, however, sport a decidedly different look: The
bracelets are slender and the pendants petite.
The reason for his shift in artistic direction: skyrocketing prices of gold and silver. "These metal prices have
subconsciously compromised my creativity," says Mr. Thomas, who works in Santa Monica, Calif., and sells
pieces online for $150 to $10,000.
The jump in gold and silver prices to 25-year records is increasing raw-material costs for everything from
dental crowns to semiconductors. But in some corners of the jewelry world, it's changing styles, too. With
gold having topped $700 an ounce in recent weeks, some trendsetting jewelry makers say they're rethinking
designs and using less metal. The goal: Preserve profits without big increases in retail prices.
Amy Levine, a New York designer whose work is sold in high-end boutiques, is making slimmer necklaces
with more stones that she says give them "a bigger look without feeling the effects of the rising gold prices."
Links of London, which has four stores in the U.S. and used to sell pieces in either gold or silver, displays
"bimetal" bangles and necklaces made of sterling silver accented with gold. The reason: They're more
affordable than gold jewelry. A bimetal Union Link bangle sells for $490, compared with $2,700 for a gold
version.
Judith Ripka just came out with an 18-karat gold and diamond Athena pendant in two sizes, using a strategy
the company developed a few years ago when gold prices started rising. The smaller pendant, in yellow or
white gold, uses 34% less metal than the large pendant and, at $1,200, sells for half the price.
"The rise in costs has gone beyond what we anticipated," says Judith Ripka President Janice Winter, who
checks the metal markets every morning but has held off on raising prices. "Even if [your] price goes up just
5%, you have to be very, very careful. You may price yourself out of the category and all of a sudden,
someone says, `Whoa!' "
Though the price of gold fell on Friday, it has more than doubled over the past five years and jumped by
about a third since the start of this year. Silver, which often follows gold and also fell Friday, has risen about
40% this year to over $12 an ounce. The prices are being driven by fears of inflation, terrorism and a dollar
decline.
Small designers are more vulnerable to price jumps than large companies because they typically buy metal in
small amounts, sometimes only when they get orders. Mr. Thomas, for example, buys metal once a month, in
amounts as small as three ounces.
Big companies, by contrast, buy thousands of ounces a year and often contract with suppliers who agree to
sell them gold or silver at prices that are fixed for three or six months at a time -- or longer.

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A few months ago, big shopping mall retailer Zale locked in gold prices through next Valentine's Day -- at a
level lower than current prices, says David Sternblitz, a vice president. The Irving, Texas, company also
hedges its bets by executing forward contracts, which are agreements to buy a certain amount of metal on a
set date at a set price. And by moving some production to China and Turkey during the last year, the
company cut costs, giving it more flexibility to absorb higher raw-material prices.
Small designers don't have that luxury. If they don't make the pieces themselves, they use jewelry-production
firms. Duran Jewelry, one such company in New York, increased its prices by 30% to 40% in recent months
to cover higher metal costs, says owner Erol Civi. Gold prices have been so volatile that Michael Toback, a
metal supplier in New York who used to change his prices daily, now adjusts them two or three times a day.
"When the markets move this quickly we have to move with the market," he says. "A change of $10 or $30
has a big impact on my business."
For high-end jewelers that make expensive pieces, metal also tends to account for a smaller percentage of
costs. Jim Haag, managing director of jeweler Jacob &Co., estimates that diamonds or other precious stones
account for 70% to 90% of the cost of most of the jewelry the New York company makes.
Smaller designers are trying new materials. Tina Tang, who has two stores in New York and sells online, now
makes gold-filled pieces, which have a layer of gold bonded onto a base metal. One necklace costs $75 but
would have sold for $400 in 14-karat gold. Mauri Pioppo, who used to design mostly 18-karat gold jewelry,
now sells less-expensive 14-karat versions. And high-end New York designer Whitney Boin is testing
lower-cost palladium versions of his $1,850 to $10,000 platinum rings. To promote the metal, which looks like
platinum but sells for about a third of the price, he plans an ad campaign touting palladium as "the new white
metal."
Some customers are warming up to the new looks. Savvy Cavanaugh, a homemaker in Delray Beach, Fla.,
who used to buy only white gold and diamonds, says sticker shock has pushed her to try silver. "If I buy
something that's $500 as opposed to $1,500 and it's well made," she says, "I don't think people can tell the
difference."

Page 99 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Surviving a Market's Ups and Downs --- Playing the Falling-Dollar Game: Overseas Holdings Get a Boost But Currency Bets Can Be Risky
Surviving a Market's Ups and Downs --- Playing the Falling-Dollar Game: Overseas Holdings Get a
Boost But Currency Bets Can Be Risky
By Ian McDonald in New York, Kate Linebaugh in Hong Kong and Andrew Morse in Tokyo
1,334 words
20 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JUST AS THE foreign-stock boom starts looking as if it could run out of steam, it is getting a fresh boost --
from the weaker U.S. dollar.
So far this year, the dollar has lost ground against an array of currencies. That pumps up returns for
Americans who own stocks or bonds abroad, because it means each yen, euro or pound earned overseas
buys a greater number of dollars. It is starting to have a significant impact on portfolios: So far this year, the
MSCI EAFE Index, a broad measure of foreign stocks, is up about 2% -- but the same index is up more than
10% in dollar terms.
The dollar's decline is spurring some big-name investors to look abroad for ways to cash in. This month
Warren Buffett said his company, Berkshire Hathaway Inc., intends to buy more companies abroad in order to
have "a fair amount of our earning power coming from other currencies." Mr. Buffett also stated his belief that
the dollar is probably in for a long-term slide.
For individual investors, however, shopping abroad for investments is a tough call. Foreign stocks already
have had a three-year runup, with the MSCI EAFE averaging more than 25% annual gains -- more than
doubling the return of U.S. stocks, according to MSCI. Some analysts worry that the biggest foreign-market
gains may be in the rear-view mirror.
Meanwhile, playing currency swings can be a particularly risky game, and even pros have a spotty record. On
Friday, for instance, the dollar reversed course and gained nearly 1% against the yen -- a sizable one-day
jump -- after Japan's central-bank chief indicated he wouldn't be rushing to raise interest rates there.
Further complicating things, even if you predict the dollar's direction correctly, measuring its effects on the
bottom line isn't simple. Some foreign companies are hurt by a falling dollar because it raises the sticker price
of their products in the U.S., hurting their sales.
Still, there are strategies for investors to incorporate a weaker dollar into their portfolio strategy. Among the
newest: a burgeoning array of mutual funds designed to let people make bets that the dollar will weaken
further. (Or, if they prefer, bet that it will rebound.)
One of the most straightforward strategies to play the dollar is to simply invest in U.S. companies with
substantial sales overseas. Americans "should be buying their own multinationals -- they earn 50% to 60% of
their profits offshore," says Malcolm Wood, Asian equity strategist at Morgan Stanley (whose firm is the
majority owner of the MSCI EAFE index). When those companies translate those foreign-currency sales back
into dollars, they benefit from the currency windfall.
More than half of Procter &Gamble Co.'s revenue last fiscal year came from selling the company's
toothpaste, detergent and other goods overseas, Mr. Wood says. Banks such as HSBC Holdings Inc. and
Citigroup Inc. also derive much of their earnings from emerging markets where the dollar is weak.
There are, of course, plenty of mutual funds targeting U.S. investors looking for ways to send money
overseas. Indeed, given the lofty foreign-market returns -- partly because of the falling dollar -- investors have
flocked to foreign funds. Net investments in foreign stock and bond funds topped $72 billion in the first
quarter, compared with a little more than $20 billion for funds that invest in U.S. stocks and bonds, according
to Boston fund consultant Financial Research Corp.
However, for people looking to invest in overseas mutual funds to take advantage of a weakening dollar, one
caveat: You will need to avoid funds that try to protect themselves against currency swings by investing in
financial instruments designed to cancel out moves in the dollar. That has its own risks, however, because
so-called unhedged funds will give you the benefit of the weaker dollar, but also leave you exposed to
potential losses if currency markets swing the other way.

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The dollar is in a funk right now partly because the U.S. economy is importing considerably more than it
exports. In currency markets, that boils down to a simple supply-and-demand issue: Foreign currencies are
needed to buy those imports, so they are relatively more in demand than dollars are.
At the same time, the U.S. government is carrying a heavy debt load, and a weaker dollar could potentially
help with that because it would mean the U.S. could eventually pay back its debts with weaker currency.
"The more you owe, the more it becomes attractive to devalue the currency," Mr. Buffett said earlier this
month at his annual meeting, citing that as one reason the dollar might continue showing signs of weakness.
Given that currencies are so tough to predict, one of the safest strategies is to allocate a set percentage of
your portfolio to foreign mutual funds, and stick to it, says Ray Mills, manager of the $1.6 billion T. Rowe Price
International Growth &Income Fund. Some financial advisers have aggressively moved into foreign securities.
Steve Henningsen, a financial adviser with Wealth Conservancy in Boulder, Colo., has had about half his
clients' assets in foreign securities for four years now. "The currency play is key" to that allocation, he says.
Whether or how much money an investor invests overseas comes down to each person's risk tolerance and
financial situation. "To me, anywhere from 25% to 50% of a long-term investor's portfolio in foreign stocks
makes sense," says Russ Kinnel, Morningstar's director of fund analysis. "I'm not a believer that there's an
exact number."
There is also an expanding group of funds designed to let investors bet on the dollar's moves, including
offerings from Franklin Templeton Investments, ProFunds and Rydex Investments. Franklin Templeton Hard
Currency Fund, for instance, protects investors against the dollar's depreciation by making money-market
investments overseas. The fund averaged a nearly 10% annualized gain over the past five years.
Funds such as the Falling U.S. Dollar ProFund and the Rydex Dynamic Weakening Dollar also invest in
money-market instruments, as well as overseas debt and more complex financial instruments, in an effort to
rise when the dollar is falling. The two funds, launched last year, are up more than 7% and more than 15% so
far this year, respectively. (The firms also offer similar funds designed to bet on a strengthening dollar.)
Additionally, online bank EverBank Financial Corp. offers savings accounts and certificates of deposit in
foreign currencies to protect against a dollar slide.
While each of these is a legitimate option, none should dominate a portfolio. "Having unhedged foreign stock
and bond funds is probably a good idea for diversification," Mr. Kinnel says. "But you don't want currency
plays to drive your investments. The most important thing over the long term is always whether the fund
manager is buying good stocks or bonds."
Another strategy: modest investments in niche mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that invest primarily
in gold, other metals or mining companies. Metals have historically been a good hedge against dollar
depreciation, and have surged during recent quarters.
While they can hedge against a dollar fall, they can be tremendously volatile and are investments only for
people with a strong stomach for risk. Metals funds are up 90%, on average, over the past year, according to
Morningstar. They also are down more than 8% over the past month. During tough years, such as 1997, the
average metals fund lost more than 40% of its value, according to Morningstar.
(See related article: "Friday's Markets: Topsy-Turvy Stocks Calm a Bit With Dow Posting a Modest Gain; How
to Navigate the Pitfalls" -- WSJ May 20, 2006)

Copper Tumbles Amid Concern On Equities, Rates, Inventories
Commodities Report
Copper Tumbles Amid Concern On Equities, Rates, Inventories
By Allen Sykora
495 words
20 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Copper futures fell sharply, mostly because of liquidation that was also occurring in other commodities,
traders and analysts said.
They cited several contributing factors for copper's weakness in New York trading Friday: recent declines in
equities, worries about inflation and thus more U.S. interest-rate increases, a report of a global copper
surplus in the first two months of the year, a weekly increase in Shanghai Futures Exchange inventories and
a stronger U.S. dollar.
The most-active July copper contract fell 24.2 cents to $3.469 a pound on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange while the nearby May copper contract slid 22.65 cents to $3.643.
The futures hit their daily, exchange-imposed fluctuation limit of 20 cents in the first hour of trading, resulting
in a temporary closure before the market reopened with an expanded 40-cent limit.
"All of these commodities are kind of moving in tandem," said Edward Meir, an analyst with Man Financial.
The stock market was lethargic, and that "deprived the commodity markets of some follow-through," he said.
A trader cited economic worries creeping into the market after the stock market's recent weakness, with
concerns about inflation leaving traders wondering if interest rates will keep rising.
Besides liquidation pressure on copper, some traders are also outright shorting the market, or selling, on the
bet that prices may fall further, he added.
Another trader described much of the speculative selling as "stale liquidation" from accounts that are opting
out simply because copper hasn't been able to return to and break through its recent records.
Mr. Meir characterized a Thursday report from the International Copper Study Group, an industry group, as "a
bit bearish" even though the data are for a few months back. "It still points to the fact that we're running a
surplus," said Mr. Meir. "I don't think that helped."
The organization reported that the global refined copper market was in surplus by 24,000 metric tons in
February, or 52,000 after seasonal adjustments.
Also bearish were weekly data showing an increase of 7,076 metric tons of copper stored in Shanghai
Futures Exchange warehouses, taking the total to 45,457 metric tons, said Jim Quinn, commodity floor
analyst with A.G. Edwards.
In other commodities markets Friday:
GOLD: Prices for gold on the Comex division of Nymex slid as the continuing correction from recent 25-year
highs was mixed with preweekend liquidation. Some analysts contended that gold has met a temporary top
but that the bull run remains intact. The nearby May gold contract fell $22.90 an ounce to $656.70.
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell to their lowest level since April 7, capping a weeklong slide sparked by concern
about high prices eroding global demand. The June crude-oil contract settled 92 cents lower at $68.53 a
barrel on Nymex.

Surviving a Market's Ups and Downs --- Topsy-Turvy Stocks Calm a Bit With Dow Posting a Modest Gain; How to Navigate the Pitfalls
Friday's Markets
Surviving a Market's Ups and Downs --- Topsy-Turvy Stocks Calm a Bit With Dow Posting a Modest
Gain; How to Navigate the Pitfalls
By Jane J. Kim and Peter A. McKay
1,105 words
20 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT MIGHT BE FUN -- if it were a roller-coaster.
Over the past few weeks, stock prices zoomed toward record levels, only to go into a dive. On Friday, they
reversed again, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average climbing 15.77 points to close at 11144.06.
The gyrations are causing headaches for investors trying to decide if it means they should be selling (to lock
in profits) or buying on a dip.
The biggest fear: inflation. If it's stirring again -- and in recent days there were discouraging signs that
consumer prices are rising -- the Federal Reserve might keep raising short-term interest rates more than
investors anticipate. Such rate increases could slow inflation, but higher rates also can crimp corporate
profits.
Friday brought a slight respite from the steep declines in stocks over the past week or so. The Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index gained 0.4%, or 5.22 points, to end at 1267.03, and the Nasdaq Composite Index
gained 0.6%, or 13.56 points, to 2193.88.
Late on Friday, shares got a boost from comments made by Thomas Hoenig, president of the Federal
Reserve Bank of Kansas City, who said in an interview that the central bank should be careful not to
"overshoot," or continue raising interest rates for longer than necessary.
Still, the Dow industrials ended the week down 2.1%.
One sign of the widespread concern that interest rates will rise: On Friday gold took another big hit, with
gold futures for May delivery tumbling 3.4%, or $22.90, to $656.70 a troy ounce on the New York Mercantile
Exchange's Comex division. It added up to a weeklong decline totaling 7.8%. In general, gold loses value --
and the dollar gains strength -- if interest rates rise.
Gold's decline "could yet go deeper," said fund manager Joe Foster, of Van Eck Global, a New York
money-management firm. "But in the long run, I think the dollar will be weak, which is very supportive of
gold."
On Friday the dollar actually strengthened slightly against the yen and euro, but overall it remains weaker for
the year. For U.S. investors, the slumping dollar is starting to sharply boost the returns they're earning on
their investments abroad. A weaker dollar has the effect of amplifying earnings abroad, and since the start of
the year, the dollar is down 8% against the euro.
Despite these amped-up foreign returns, and uncertainty at home, many investment advisers are telling their
clients that U.S. stocks still have room to run. "This break in the markets is a buying opportunity," says
Elizabeth Bramwell, a portfolio manager at Sentinel Asset Management Inc., a unit of National Life Group.
Indeed, stocks could bounce back from recent weakness and make another bid for higher ground.
Among the reasons: U.S. companies recently turned in stronger-than-expected first-quarter earnings. That's
prompting some Wall Street firms to raise their earnings forecasts for this year and next. Recently the chief
U.S. strategist for Lehman Brothers raised his earnings estimate for companies in the S&P 500 by 50 cents a
share, to $83.50, and his 2007 estimate by $1.25 a share to $90.50.
At the same time, many companies are sitting on historically large piles of cash -- money that could be used
to boost dividend payouts or buy back stock. Those activities may help shareholders by either putting money
directly in their hands, or by boosting earnings per share through a buyback.

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Factors like these have led some Wall Street firms recently to start recommending investors increase the
amount of money they allocate to stocks. Prudential Equity Group LLC, a unit of Prudential Financial Inc.,
now says 70% of investors' portfolios should be in stocks, up from its previous recommendation of 65%.
Merrill Lynch &Co. raised its stock allocation to a still-cautious 50% from 40%, and Lehman Brothers Holdings
Inc. went to 70% from 65%.
One argument for skewing toward stocks is the fact that the combination of inflation fears and potentially
higher interest rates make bonds a scarier place to be. When interest rates rise, bond prices take a hit. Just
this month Merrill Lynch investment strategist Richard Bernstein -- who for much of the stock market's
three-year-long bull run urged his clients to invest in bonds, as well as stable companies that perform better in
a slowing economy -- pared back his allocation to bonds amid concerns about inflation and rising rates.
Not everyone has warm feelings for stocks. Jeremy Grantham, chairman of GMO, a Boston-money
management firm, says he thinks nearly every class of assets is currently overpriced. So he's been holding
more cash and recommending that investors trim back on risk by tilting toward higher-quality stocks, big
companies and fixed-income investments.
Other advisers are adjusting where they put their money because of a changing global financial landscape --
everything from the falling dollar and higher oil prices to inflation and potentially higher interest rates. For
instance, they're starting to favor companies that make things like aircraft engines and construction
equipment, which benefit from a weaker dollar, strong business spending and the industrialization of
developing nations such as China.
At the same time, many strategists also are keeping an eye on cooling housing markets and advising
investors to lighten up on investments that could be hurt by a slowdown in consumer spending. Companies
that are particularly sensitive to interest rates, including some banks and utilities, also could suffer in the
current environment, they say.
Investors lately have been sending the lion's share of their mutual-fund investment dollars overseas, as
international stock markets have outperformed the U.S. Now, there are signs that trend may be turning
around. Money going into U.S. stock funds in April is expected to exceed flows into foreign funds -- the first
time in nearly a year, estimates TrimTabs Investment Research.
Some money managers are saying it's time to take some profits from emerging markets, which have had a
strong run for the past three years and have recently shown some signs of weakness. For instance, stock
prices in India took a hit over the past week. Others say developed international markets remain attractive.
(See related article: "Playing the Falling-Dollar Game: Overseas Holdings Get a Boost But Currency Bets Can
Be Risky" -- May 20, 2006)

Latin America's New Imperialist
Americas
Latin America's New Imperialist
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
1,082 words
19 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The U.S. decision on Monday to ban arms sales to Venezuela symbolizes the growing concern around the
region about the saber-rattling President Hugo Chavez.
But it's not likely to have much of an effect. First, because Venezuela can probably get much of what it wants
in the weapons department via its friends in Russia. And second, because Mr. Chavez's "Bolivarian
Revolution" -- which promises to unite the entire region under a neo-Marxist flag -- is being carried out
through less traditional means than modern brute force. So far he has picked off Bolivia and serious concerns
are now emerging about his financial backing of Sandinista National Liberation Front candidate Daniel Ortega
ahead of Nicaragua's November presidential elections.
The trouble is that Mr. Chavez is awash in cash and that is what he is using, both overtly and surreptitiously,
to extend his influence in the poorest countries of the region. A better way to clip the Venezuelan despot's
wings would be for the Federal Reserve to use the tools at its disposal -- including open market operations --
to sop up the excess of dollar liquidity that is sloshing around the globe. Oil, like gold, has spiked in price
under this inflationary bias, creating windfall profits for the world's worst type-A tyrants.
Of course, political uncertainty has added to petroleum price hikes. But an explicit Fed objective of dealing
with inflationary expectations by, for instance, bringing gold back to $400 an ounce, is guaranteed to rein in
oil prices. Writing in the May 22 issue of Forbes magazine, Steve Forbes estimates that "At least $20 of the
price of a barrel of oil today is pure inflation and inflation-induced speculation."
Slashing the inflation premium would be great for Americans at the pump and next winter. But by putting an
end to too many dollars chasing world petroleum output, the U.S. would also cut its worst enemies down to
size. Note that Iran's mullahs haven't been this aggressive since the 1970s, when the U.S. last went on an
inflationary binge. In this hemisphere, as the weapons ban indicates, cash-rich Venezuela has become
equally menacing.
Venezuelan democrats will tell you that there is not much hope for a change in government without an
oil-price retreat. Until that happens, Mr. Chavez will be too powerful. That's bad news for 26 million
Venezuelans who are experiencing sky-rocketing crime rates and declining living standards under Chavismo.
But the risks of a sustained period of extraordinary wealth in the hands of Mr. Chavez go well beyond the
damage he is doing to his own country or even his military buildup. Equally worrying is that in his role as the
region's Daddy Warbucks, Mr. Chavez is also using his wealth to extend his revolutionary tentacles in politics.
Gustavo Coronel, a former member of the board of directors of the state-owned oil company PdVSA,
estimates that Mr. Chavez has already officially spent $17 billion internationally to advance his agenda. Given
Venezuela's lack of transparency and massive corruption, it's hard to dismiss suspicions that the real number
is much higher.
Exhibit A is Bolivia, where Venezuela's president has actively coached his disciples and where well-financed
and orchestrated street violence brought down two elected governments in recent years and produced the
Evo Morales presidency. Bolivia has now become a virtual Venezuelan colony flush with Cuban agents.
A number of governments in the hemisphere have expressed alarm about Mr. Chavez's extracurricular
activities around the region. Mexico is worried about the growth of Chavez-funded Bolivarian Circles -- groups
that preach and enforce Fidel Castro's view of the world -- springing up at its universities and trying to
influence its July presidential elections.
This sort of political manipulation is why Mexican President Vicente Fox expelled Venezuela's ambassador
last year. Presidential candidate Felipe Calderon has been even more explicit, stating that "evidence has
emerged" of Venezuelan meddling. "This should be investigated and the full weight of the law should be

--- Page 109 ---
Page 109 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
brought to bear to prevent Hugo Chavez from actively interfering and attempting to sway the outcome of the
vote in Mexico."
Brazil, headed by a left-winger once viewed as a Chavez ally, is now publicly critical of Venezuelan
interference. Last week Brazil's foreign minister warned Mr. Chavez to back off in Bolivia, noting that "when
certain threats [from Bolivia to Brazil] were being transmitted through the press, that happened parallel with a
large presence of PdVSA personnel" in Bolivia. In Peru, where a runoff between Chavez-wannabe Ollanta
Humala and former president Alan Garcia is scheduled for June, there are also strong suspicions of
Venezuelan interference. President Alejandro Toledo has cautioned Mr. Chavez to stay out of Peruvian
affairs.
Latin America's biggest players might be able to fend off Venezuela. But Central America is far more
vulnerable. Mr. Chavez has made no secret of his support for Nicaragua's Cold War relic Daniel Ortega, still
doing Castro's will but now with the Venezuelan's money.
As he has done all over Latin America and the Caribbean -- and in Massachusetts -- Mr. Chavez is selling
discounted energy to Sandinista-controlled municipalities, including the capital of Managua. Mr. Ortega
claims that Venezuela promised to use some of the gasoline income it gets in the transaction to capitalize a
development bank for Nicaragua. This week the Nicaraguan daily La Prensa reported that Mr. Chavez has
sent a gift of 10,000 tons of fertilizer to the Sandinista mayors and is planning to send another 10,000. The
mayors are also controlling the distribution of gifts from Cuba -- obviously funded by Venezuela since Cuba
has no money -- which include a multi-million dollar literacy program and thousands of free television sets.
Venezuela has a program to ferry Nicaraguans to Caracas for free eye surgery and recently hosted 80
pro-Ortega mayors for a retreat in the capital.
This is not passive generosity. It's classic directed political support for the hard left. One way to compete
against it would be to spend as hard as Mr. Chavez. A better way would be to bring oil prices back to earth by
draining dollar liquidity. The beauty of decisive action on the monetary front is that it would weaken the
Venezuelan tyrant while at the same time making U.S. consumers (and ultimately Venezuelan ones, too)
better off.

Adam Smith Growls
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Adam Smith Growls
519 words
19 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks continued their weeklong plunge yesterday, selling off again at the end of the day on more inflationary
fears. The way to understand this washout -- knocking 4.4% off the Dow -- is that Adam Smith is giving the
U.S. financial establishment a warning.
We're using our favorite moral philosopher here as a proxy for the ruthless discipline of financial markets.
They can be brutal in punishing economic mismanagement, and for several days Mr. Smith has been
growling that the Federal Reserve and its easy-money cheerleaders on Wall Street and in Washington need
to sober up, or there will be far worse to come. The press is calling this an "inflation scare," but for those who
want to know how markets respond once a real inflation settles in, we suggest reading an economic history
of the 1970s. It isn't pretty.
The 4.4%-cold shower may even prove to be beneficial if it changes the psychology in elite political and
economic circles. For the last year especially, they've been ignoring signs of incipient inflation, first by
trusting in Alan Greenspan's assurances, later pointing to low long-bond rates, or any other excuse to avoid
facing up to a world awash in dollar liquidity and soaring gold, oil and other commodity prices. This is the kind
of psychology that often takes hold at this stage of an expansion, with everyone enjoying the good times and
desperately afraid the Fed will spoil the fun.
But the lesson of the past 30 years is that the economic pain is far more severe the longer the Fed stays too
accommodative and lets pricing pressures build. The consumer price index reading that triggered
Wednesday's selloff is a lagging indicator, after all, the kind that tells you inflation is here only after it has
already arrived. As Paul Volcker -- the man who finally broke the 1970s inflation -- once noted, the actual
inflation data comes in months later but spot commodity prices are a real-time signal. Investors have finally
begun to doubt the Fed's happy talk about a "pause" in raising interest rates or that inflationary pressures
are "contained."
All of which suggests that Mr. Greenspan's successor as Fed Chairman, Ben Bernanke, is facing a rough
passage. He's arrived at a Fed that has clearly made a mistake in letting inflation expectations build and has
been very slow to admit it. The only questions now are the magnitude of that mistake and of the resulting
financial casualties.
It's worth noting that while stocks fell yesterday, bonds rallied after comments by a couple of Fed officials that
inflation may be gathering steam. "I pay a lot of attention to inflation expectations," said St. Louis Fed
President William Poole, which makes us wonder what he and his colleagues have been paying attention to
the last year.
The sooner Mr. Bernanke shows that he understands the problem, and has the fortitude to do something
about it, the fewer casualties there will be. Sooner or later Adam Smith will take over if Mr. Bernanke doesn't.

Commodity Boom Beats Up Skeptic; Will He Be Right?
Commodity Boom Beats Up Skeptic; Will He Be Right?
By Henny Sender
1,371 words
19 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
PAUL TUDOR JONES of Tudor Investment Corp. was incorrectly referred to as John Paul Tudor in a Money
&Investing article Friday. Also, the London Metal Exchange was incorrectly called the London Metals
Exchange.
(WSJ May 20, 2006)
(END)
FEW OUTSIDE the rarefied world of high finance have heard of Dwight Anderson. But as commodity prices
take a dive, he and his New York investment firm, Ospraie Management LLC, are at the center of the
upheaval.
Mr. Anderson -- along with the rest of Wall Street -- must decide whether falling prices for everything from
platinum to oil are a temporary hiccup or a lasting response to rising global interest rates. Gold fell
yesterday to $679.60 a troy ounce and is now down $40.20 from the latest 26-year high it hit about a week
ago.
Other commodities are also hovering near record or multidecade highs.
The booming economies of China and India have contributed to the rise of commodities prices over the past
two years. But the biggest impetus for the gains had come from speculators pouring into the market, hoping
for lucrative returns in the face of low interest rates in more-traditional investments.
Mr. Anderson, a longtime investor in and student of commodities markets, has been caught in the crossfire.
For years he has ranked among the most successful traders in commodities and the companies that traffic in
them. But amid the speculative fever of the past few months, he had his worst quarter ever, with Ospraie
down 13%.
The reason: He concluded that the boom couldn't last.
Commodities trading can be a brutal way to try to make money. Gains can soar by double-digit figures one
month, only to fall the next. It is especially brutal for traders, like Mr. Anderson, who grew up in a world where
prices were determined more by fundamental supply and demand than by speculative fever chasing the
market's upward momentum.
Until a few years ago, many on Wall Street had little interest in commodities. The reason: The companies that
produced and distributed the stuff -- say, Exxon Mobil Corp. in oil or Cargill Inc. in grains -- would always
have an edge in understanding supply, demand and prices because they controlled the flow.
Commodity prices tend to be cyclical, rising and falling in tandem with the overall cycle of global economic
growth. However, sometime in the past two years, the charts show these prices making an abrupt leap above
their historical trading range.
Price spikes like these have consequences in the real world, since they amount to a redistribution of wealth --
stoking the prosperity of resource-rich countries and the anger of consumers frustrated at the expense of, for
instance, filling their gas tanks.
The volatility of the past few years also transformed stodgy commodities into one of the most profitable
trading sectors. Wall Street firms usually don't break out numbers, but analysts say that commodities have
added as much as $1 billion each to the profits of Morgan Stanley and Goldman Sachs Group Inc. in recent
years.

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ospraie now has about $4 billion under management, a figure that includes Mr. Anderson's flagship fund as
well as the money of a number of managers that trade under his flagship brand in related areas. Last year,
Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. took a 20% stake.
"He decoded the basic-materials world when nobody else was in it," says Michael Novogratz, a founding
partner of Fortress Investments and a longtime basketball partner of Mr. Anderson.
Mr. Anderson's path to trading had some twists and turns. As a kid growing up in upper New York state, he
says, he wanted to be a veterinarian.
After graduating from Princeton, he ended up working at Amsterdam Printing, an upstate New York paper
and printing company, after first working as a consultant for a company that developed software to help
automate manufacturing operations.
In 1993, he went to graduate school at the University of North Carolina on a university scholarship funded by
Julian Robertson's legendary hedge fund and eventually ended up working for Mr. Robertson. Mr. Anderson
then moved on to the hedge fund of the equally legendary John Paul Tudor, Tudor Investment Corp., where
he formed Ospraie -- named for the raptor -- as part of Tudor, then took the fund out of Tudor (with a lot of
Tudor money) in 2004.
Like all traders, Mr. Anderson has made some bad bets. For instance, he underestimated how high
scrap-steel prices would go in 2004, hurting some companies whose stock he held. He also took a bath on
oil-services company McDermott International Inc. when the company was hit by a sudden surge in asbestos
claims from the days when it built ships a quarter of a century earlier. In recent months, he suffered as the
markets, driven by speculative funds riding trends, reached highs that seemed irrational by the standard of
veteran traders and investors.
To figure out his strategy, he studies not only price charts, but also the balance sheets and cost structures of
the individual industries. The goal: to figure out what price levels might force the industry to take major steps
that change the supply-and-demand dynamics, such as adding extra production capacity if prices soar or
shutting down some of the current capacity if prices fall.
It is a complex mix of old-fashioned number crunching, probability theory and a measure of luck. To see how
it works, consider copper, demand for which is tied closely to the world economic cycle. On average, copper
costs about 80 cents a pound to produce; if prices fall below that, production isn't profitable. However, mines
won't cease production if the price falls only to, say, 77 cents a pound, because shutdowns entail costs too. It
takes a drop to about 72 cents before it makes economic sense to start cutting back.
In late 2001, copper plunged to 60 cents a pound after the Sept. 11 attacks. That gave producers every
incentive to cut production. But would they?
"At those price levels, the industry was bleeding. You could expect to see massive closures," Mr. Anderson
says. But "nobody wants to be the first to shut down and let others benefit from the reduced supply,"
At that point, Mr. Anderson took a bet and began accumulating a big position in copper on the London Metals
Exchange, betting that the prices and the profit margins of major copper producers would bounce back -- and
held his breath.
Mr. Anderson kept his eye on the high-cost producers, such as Phelps Dodge Corp., of the U.S. Phelps
Dodge was the first to crack, announcing a temporary cutback in production. Then BHP Billiton did the same.
By January 2002, copper bounced to more than 75 cents. Mr. Anderson maintained his position, because he
knew the price hadn't yet risen high enough to give producers an incentive to reopen capacity.
Now, though, he is less certain what his next move should be. Mr. Anderson takes out a chart mapping
copper prices from 1988, the year he began to be interested in commodities trading. "I have never seen these
levels of copper prices in my career," he says. "Everyone is making money at these levels. Everyone wants to
reopen old mines and finance new mines. At some point, there will be a surplus and the price will inevitably
drop 40% or 50%."
To back up that statement, he compares this decade with the 1950s, the most comparable period he could
find, as Japan and Europe rebuilt after World War II. He assumes a rate of growth in the price of copper of
5.1% a year, far more generous than the 2.8% average of the 1990s, to reflect the emergence of India and
China on the world economic stage. And he concludes that copper will still shift to surplus sometime in the
next two years.
"I can see (a) where we are now, and I can see (c) where we are going," he says. "I just can't be sure of (b)
when we get there -- and with how much volatility."

Stocks Get the Inflation Shakes --- Dow Industrials Slide 214.28 Points, Bond Yields Climb on Rate-Increase Fears
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Get the Inflation Shakes --- Dow Industrials Slide 214.28 Points, Bond Yields Climb on
Rate-Increase Fears
By Peter A. McKay
818 words
18 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE OLD WALL STREET bogeyman, inflation, thoroughly spooked investors, who sold stocks and bonds
furiously in what proved to be one of the roughest trading sessions this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average dropped 214.28 points, or 1.9%, to 11205.61, leaving it up 4.6% on the
year. It was the Dow's largest point drop in three years, but in percentage terms, the second-largest of the
year (behind Jan. 20).
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index shed 1.7%, or 21.76 points, to end at 1270.32, up 1.8% for 2006. The
Nasdaq Composite Index lost 1.5%, or 33.33 points, to 2195.80, pushing the technology-stock-heavy
indicator into negative territory for 2006, off 0.4%.
The dollar was a rare bright spot, rising for the second time in the past three sessions against the euro and
yen.
Behind the markets rout: The Labor Department reported a seasonally adjusted 0.6% increase in its
consumer-price index for the month of April, or a 0.3% increase when volatile food and energy costs are
excluded from the measure. The latter measure, sometimes referred to as core inflation, is the one most
watched by economists and investors. The year-over-year rise in core prices reached 2.3% in April. While the
Federal Reserve has no official inflation target, inflation above 2% is said to make the central bank's policy
makers nervous.
The consumer-price data reignited fears that the Fed will prolong its series of interest-rate hikes to tame
inflation. Coming into 2006, many investing pros predicted that the economy would be showing clear signs of
slowing by the spring, encouraging the Fed to hold off on further rate increases.
Instead, many experts are expecting another quarter-percentage-point increase in the Fed's target for
short-term interest rates, to 5.25%, at policy makers' meeting in late June. Mickey Levy, chief economist at
Bank of America Corp, said in an interview that such a move is "virtually guaranteed."
At the Chicago Board of Trade, a futures contract that tracks traders' expectations about future rate moves
priced in about a 48% chance that central bankers will boost their rate target to 5.25% at their June meeting,
up from about a 36% chance in Tuesday's trading.
Since last week's Federal Reserve meeting, when the central bank sounded a note of uncertainty about
when it would cease raising rates, the Dow is off more than 430 points, or 3.7%.
"The data we saw today, from a policy makers' perspective, are very untimely," Mr. Levy added. "It's exactly
what they do not want to see in order to maintain their inflation-fighting credibility."
Investors are still learning to decipher statements made by Fed Chairman Benjamin Bernanke, who took over
the top job this year, said Frank Davis, head of trading at Lek Securities, a New York brokerage firm.
"That makes investors tend to read even more into the economic numbers -- rather than Bernanke's words --
and they'll react emotionally if they see something they don't like," Mr. Davis said.
Treasurys suffered, too. While bonds sometimes hold up well when stocks are falling, inflation is their worst
enemy. That's because the rising rates associated with inflation cause bonds, which pay fixed rates, to lose
their luster as prevailing rates climb.
The price of the 10-year Treasury note fell 14/32, or $4.38 for each 1,000 invested, to push up the yield to
5.159%. The 30-year Treasury fell even harder, losing 29/32 to yield 5.280%.

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With a slew of other economic data still due in the six weeks before the next Fed meeting, the best prospect
for a market rally might lie in economic news that would otherwise seem downbeat, said strategist Jim
Russell, of Fifth Third Asset Management. For instance, he said that signs of higher unemployment might
counteract the impression that the economy is too hot. "The market will be focused like a laser beam on any
signs of weakness," Mr. Russell said.
As for the dollar, it traded at 110.94 yen, up from 109.76 yen, while the euro fell against the dollar to $1.2750
from $1.2857. As it pushes up rates, the Fed effectively takes dollars out of circulation, so the greenback
sometimes rises amid rate concerns.
By contrast, the price of gold, which is still viewed as an alternative form of money in some parts of the world,
fell modestly. Gold futures for May delivery shed $1 per troy ounce, or 0.1%, to $690.50, and are up 33.5%
on the year at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.

In Gold We Trust
In Gold We Trust
By David Ranson and Penny Russell
1,107 words
18 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Widespread fear of another global energy crisis is rife, especially in light of the confrontation with Iran. But the
second of Murphy's Laws cautions that what actually goes wrong is seldom what we anticipate. While the
markets are clearly indicating something ominous, the current situation is mischaracterized as an energy
crisis -- even if the price of a gallon of gas goes past $3 and stays there. Energy prices are simply keeping
pace with the rising prices of gold and other commodities. What we are facing is a money crisis: an alarming
outbreak of inflation and all its consequences.
It's silly to blame the rise in commodity prices on foreigners; no country, not even China or Saudi Arabia, has
the market power to set off the kind of across-the-board acceleration in prices that we have been witnessing.
Nor can prices rising this consistently and at this speed be attributed to an excess of global demand over
supply, or fears about the political situation in Iraq or Iran. Speculators, another convenient scapegoat, also
lack the power to drive world commodity markets, in spite of their rapacious reputation. The real culprit is the
precipitous decline in the world's mightiest currency, the dollar, which has lost more than 60% of its gold
value in just four years.
The run on the dollar is largely being ignored by Washington and Wall Street, and most of the financial press
is only beginning to take note. When analysts do comment on the strength or weakness in the dollar, they are
most often referring to its value in terms of foreign exchange; little attention is given to the value of the dollar
in terms of gold. When headlines do herald that the price of gold is rising, there is little recognition that, since
gold is quoted in dollars, it is just as true that the dollar is falling. It is gold that is a benchmark for the value
of the dollar -- not the other way around. When the dollar price of gold is on the rise, the dollar prices of oil
and other commodities have historically kept pace.
It is only the nominal price of oil that has reached record levels; in real terms, we are still on the road to
recovery from the genuine energy crisis that culminated with Hurricane Katrina. Although it is the accepted
convention to calculate the real price of an asset from official government measures of inflation such as the
CPI, this would be a mistake in the case of energy. Since oil is traded in fast-moving markets worldwide, its
real price can only be assessed by comparison with other internationally traded commodities; for this purpose
the U.S. cost of living index is irrelevant. We suggest instead using an index of precious-metals prices.
The adjacent chart shows the divergent picture of the oil market that emerges when representative oil prices
(West Texas Crude) are expressed in real terms. The solid line charts changes in the ratio of the price of oil
to a price index for precious metals over the past half century. While the chart shows abundant variations in
the real price of oil over time, it also shows the real price gravitating around a slowly rising trend. The dotted
line represents the equilibrium real price of oil. We believe that its gradual upward slope reflects the fact that
oil is gradually becoming scarcer with the passage of time.
The chart confirms that the real price of oil peaked out late last year and has been on the decline since. In
other words, the dollar has been falling relative to its precious-metals benchmark faster than oil has been
rising relative to the dollar. That's why we believe that the current rise in the dollar price of oil is merely the
process by which this price converges toward equilibrium. Far from entering into a new crisis phase, the oil
market is still cooling off from the last one.
We estimate from nearly a half century of history that the equilibrium price of oil, converted back to current
dollars, was about $61 a barrel in April. In other words, the long-term history of the ratio between oil and
precious-metals prices, given the prices that these metals have now reached, implies that the oil market
would be in balance at $61. The actual price in April was $70. Reading from the graph, we see that the real
price of oil has moved two-thirds of the way back to equilibrium since Hurricane Katrina.
It's not the time, however, to breathe a collective sigh of relief. It is just as foolish to fail to recognize a true
crisis in the making as to conjure one up. As of this writing, gold is fluctuating around the $700 mark, with
silver and platinum up at least as much. The gold value of the dollar appears to be going into free fall, and

--- Page 120 ---
Page 120 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the further it declines the more dire the consequences, including still higher nominal prices for energy, even
without any further change in the real price. Absent some miraculous reversal, $3-a-gallon gasoline may be
here to stay.
If none of the usual suspects is responsible for gold's sharp rise, what is? We believe it represents an equally
sharp decline in the confidence of investors -- large and small -- in the likelihood that Washington will pay
back its mounting obligations in undepreciated money. Throughout history, and especially in wartime,
governments have escaped from fiscal over-commitments by letting their currencies depreciate. Ambitious
spending initiatives, threats of international conflict and even Washington's political unpopularity all contribute
to the fear that this is happening again now.
Gold is the barometer of public confidence in fiat money, and it is difficult to rebuild confidence in a currency
once it has been allowed to slide. Gold has been a reliable harbinger of many economic troubles -- not just of
escalating prices at the gas pumps, but of inflation, rising interest rates, stagnation and poor investment
performance on the part of bonds and equities alike. Changes in the price of gold are an excellent predictor
of all of these. The dollar's collapse is nothing less than a body blow to capitalism. When we downplay the
significance of energy prices, we are not denying that a crisis is looming. It's just a lot more threatening than
an increase in the cost of a tank of gas.
---
Mr. Ranson and Ms. Russell are principals of H. C. Wainwright &Co., Economics, an investment-strategy
research firm.

Nasdaq Drops To a New Low For the Year --- While Indexes Are Damped By Doubts About Rates, Growth, Commodities Stage a Rebound
Tuesday's Markets
Nasdaq Drops To a New Low For the Year --- While Indexes Are Damped By Doubts About Rates,
Growth, Commodities Stage a Rebound
By E.S. Browning
494 words
17 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMMODITY PRICES rebounded a bit from Monday's rout, but stock indexes fell back, leaving the Dow
Jones Industrial Average more than 300 points from its record and the Nasdaq Composite Index at another
low for the year.
Bond prices rose and yields fell, as April wholesale-inflation numbers came in at reassuring levels. But
stocks continued to suffer from uncertainty about the future of interest rates and of global economic growth.
Adding to the uncertainty, investors learned that in April housing starts fell to their lowest level in 17 months
but industrial production was stronger than expected.
The industrials fell 8.88 points, or 0.08%, to 11419.89, still up 6.6% for 2006, and still in sight of the Jan. 14,
2000, record finish of 11722.98.
The Nasdaq composite fell 0.42%, or 9.39 points, to 2229.13, its lowest finish since the last day of 2005.
Nasdaq is up 1.1% for the year, clinging to the remainder of its gains on 2006's first trading day. But more
stocks rose than fell on both the New York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq.
"The market's concerns on inflation aren't 100% alleviated, and without enough good news on earnings to
push stocks higher, we had a kind of directionless day," said stock trader Brian Williamson at Boston Co.
Asset Management.
Futures in copper, zinc and several other industrial commodities rose again after Monday's losses, as did oil
and gold futures, reawakening inflation concerns. Crude-oil futures rose 12 cents to $69.53 a barrel; gold
rose $7.90 to $691.50 an ounce.
April wholesale inflation figures came in slightly higher than expected overall, but "core" numbers, excluding
volatile energy and food costs, rose less than expected. One reason stocks changed little was that many
investors were awaiting April consumer-price numbers, due today.
Home Depot fell 5% after quarterly sales missed expectations, but Wal-Mart Stores beat expectations and
rose 1.4%. Both are components of the 30-stock Dow industrials.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.19%, or 2.42 points, to 1292.08, up 3.5% this year.
After regular trading hours, investors got some good news: Hewlett-Packard's quarterly profits came in well
ahead of expectations, and the stock rose 3.3% in after-hours trading.
In major U.S. market action:
Stock indexes fell as bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 13/32, or $4.06 for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield down to 5.105%. The 30-year bond was up 22/32 to yield 5.215%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 109.76 yen, down from 110.35. The euro rose against the
dollar to $1.2857 from $1.2786.
