Commodity Fever Finally Breaks --- Oil, Copper Surrender Some Gains, Gold Has Biggest Fall in 2 Years; But the Dow Rises 47.78 Points
Monday's Markets
Commodity Fever Finally Breaks --- Oil, Copper Surrender Some Gains, Gold Has Biggest Fall in 2
Years; But the Dow Rises 47.78 Points
By E.S. Browning
532 words
16 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTMENTS TIED TO world economic growth plunged yesterday, especially commodity prices and
developing-country stocks, as expectations spread that recent interest-rate increases will slow world
economic growth.
Defensive investments, such as bonds, drug-company stocks and consumer stocks, benefited from the
worries.
Amid hopes that a cooling of the commodity rally will hold down inflation, blue-chip stocks rose. The Dow
Jones Industrial Average, which had fallen heavily late last week, regained some ground, rising 47.78 points,
or 0.42%, to 11428.77. It is now up 6.6% for 2006 and less than 300 points from its record finish of 11722.98,
on Jan. 14, 2000. It was within 81 points of the record last Wednesday.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index also rebounded, gaining 0.25%, or 3.26 points, to 1294.50, up 3.7%
this year. But the Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks suffered, dropped to its lowest
2006 finish, falling 0.23%, or 5.26 points, to 2238.52. It still shows a gain of 1.5% for this year. More stocks
fell than rose on the New York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq Stock Market.
The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, fell 1.84%.
"These commodities had such momentum, and they had been up so much that they had to come down at
some point," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at Cowen &Co. But declining commodity prices, together
with a fall in bond yields, helped ease inflation fears. In the U.S., "the rest of the market hung in there pretty
well," he said.
Traders finished the day hoping to see a revival of the U.S. stock rally. Investors once again began hoping for
a "Goldilocks" economy -- not too hot, not too cold -- in which the Federal Reserve wouldn't need to cool the
economy by raising interest rates and stocks would enjoy steady gains.
The big losers were industrial-commodities futures. In London, zinc fell 11%, tin 8.6% and copper 7%. But in
New York, later in the day, copper futures were down 2.5%. Crude oil in New York fell $2.63, or 3.7%, to
$69.41 a barrel. Gold, which has tracked other commodities as a hedge on inflation, fell 3.8% in New York,
or $26.70, to $683.60 a troy ounce -- the biggest percentage decline in two years.
Energy and mining stocks, strong this year, slid. Shares of copper maker Phelps Dodge Corp. plunged 6.5%.
Investors will get the government's April wholesale-price data today, and April consumer prices tomorrow.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed; bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 9/32, or $2.81 for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield down to 5.155%. The 30-year bond was up 16/32 to yield 5.264%.
The dollar strengthened, to 110.35 yen, up from 110.02, as the euro fell to $1.2786 from $1.2929.

Crude Prices Drop Below $70 on Yuan Moves
Commodities Report
Crude Prices Drop Below $70 on Yuan Moves
By Masood Farivar
383 words
16 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Oil prices fell below $70 a barrel as a move by China to allow its currency to appreciate helped send energy
and other commodities markets tumbling.
China set a key trading benchmark for the yuan under 8.0 to the dollar for the first time, in effect allowing its
currency to appreciate more rapidly.
The move was seen as largely symbolic, but analysts said it could make Chinese exports of goods more
expensive, which could result in a reining in of the country's manufacturing sector and a curbing of demand
for raw materials.
The Chinese central bank's announcement was "the major force behind the selloff" in commodity markets,
said Phil Flynn, an energy analyst at brokerage firm Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago. "It could slow Chinese
demand for commodities" at a time when rising oil prices are cutting into world energy consumption.
The June crude contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange closed $2.63 lower, or 3.7%, to $69.41 a
barrel.
The June gasoline contract slid 12.45 cents, or 5.7%, to $2.0540 a gallon. The June heating-oil contract
dropped 10.17 cents, or 5%, to $1.9450 a gallon.
The Chinese announcement came at a time when oil prices were already falling in response to a recent
slowdown in demand and rising supply.
Demand concerns surfaced last week after the International Energy Agency, the energy watchdog for the
world's most developed nations, slashed its 2006 oil-demand-growth expectations in response to rising
prices.
In other commodity markets:
PRECIOUS METALS: Futures on the Comex division of the Nymex fell as long liquidation weighed on prices.
A downturn in the energy markets while the dollar made gains against the euro also hit gold and silver prices.
May gold fell $26.70 an ounce to $683.60, down 3.8%. May silver fell 90 cents an ounce, or 6.4%, to
$13.246.
COPPER: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex fell sharply on profit-taking after last week's price
surge, along with spillover pressure from a commodity-wide selloff. Weakness in the euro also weighed on
prices. May copper fell 9.75 cents, or 2.5%, to $3.8690 a pound.

Tough Times for Treasurys Are Likely to Continue
Credit Markets
Tough Times for Treasurys Are Likely to Continue
By Laurence Norman
633 words
15 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The hard times are set to roll on for U.S. Treasurys this week, with the 10-year note expected
to stay under pressure amid inflation concerns, tame foreign appetite for dollar assets and solid U.S. data.
Consumer and wholesale prices top the bill on the data front, although investors also will keep a close eye on
the latest housing data and a host of U.S. Federal Reserve speakers.
The 10-year note fell for the sixth week in seven last week. The yield curve -- which charts the difference in
yields between two- and 10-year notes -- steepened slightly as the Federal Reserve pinned its flag firmly to
the data-dependency mast and the market had to digest $34 billion in new 10-year Treasury notes and a
flood of corporate issuance.
Late Friday, the yield on the 10-year note was at 5.192% -- its highest close in four years -- and the two-year
rate was around 5%, the same level the Federal Open Market Committee lifted its overnight-lending rate to
last Wednesday. (Bond prices and yields move inversely.)
Center-stage this week will be Wednesday's consumer-price index. A CNBC-Dow Jones Newswires survey
sees CPI up 0.6% in April, although the core reading -- which excludes food and energy -- is expected to rise
0.2%. That compares with March's 0.4% and 0.3% headline and core readings.
Wholesale prices are seen up 0.5% when reported Tuesday, while on Thursday investors will watch the
capacity-utilization data in the April industrial-production report. The U.S. also reports housing data for April
this week.
With core CPI remaining contained for so long, analysts say a stronger-than-expected reading would be
eye-catching, adding to the pressure on investors to price risk premium into the long end of the Treasury
curve.
That is particularly true right now, with some investors concerned that the Fed's talk of a near-term pause in
its rate-tightening cycle risks letting the inflation genie out of the bottle. The U.S. central bank's statement
after its meeting last week didn't lessen these worries as policy makers made clear that further rate increases
will depend on the incoming data.
In addition to pushing yields higher on longer-term Treasurys, those price concerns have led investors to buy
Treasury inflation-indexed securities, or TIPS.
"When gold hits 25-year highs and oil is stubbornly above $70 per barrel . . . clearly there are some [who are]
concerned about inflation," said Ray Remy, head of fixed income at Daiwa Securities in New York.
The dollar's sharp recent declines add to the inflation fretting since a weaker dollar means higher import
prices.
Daiwa's Mr. Remy sees more selling in long-end Treasurys in coming days, with the yield on the benchmark
note heading up to 5.25%.
One big question hanging over Treasurys is the role of foreign investors, who played a crucial part in keeping
long-term Treasury yields low during late 2004 and 2005 as the Fed engaged in a protracted tightening cycle.
However, with central banks tightening policy in Europe, Japan, Australia, China and Canada on the back of
strong or improving economies, some are concerned that foreign investors may have begun to move away
from U.S. fixed income in recent months.

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Tomoya Masanao, Pimco's head of portfolio management in Tokyo, said global markets are only beginning to
feel the reverberations from stronger growth and policy tightening in places like Japan. "I think it's a steady,
gradual process," he said. Japanese government bond rates "have not reached the critical level to accelerate
these [global] fund movements yet," Mr. Masanao said.

It's Just Blue Chips (and Dip) as Record Nears --- Milestone Causes Pause in Dow As Investors Digest the Bull Run; Where Are the Nasdaq and...
Abreast of the Market
It's Just Blue Chips (and Dip) as Record Nears --- Milestone Causes Pause in Dow As Investors Digest
the Bull Run; Where Are the Nasdaq and S&P?
By E.S. Browning
1,003 words
15 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE PARTY TO celebrate a record for the Dow Jones Industrial Average has been postponed.
There are plenty of reasons, one of which is that not everyone got an invitation.
As the Dow industrials surged 5% in less than a month, returning almost to the record close of 11722.98 that
dated from Jan. 14, 2000, two other major indexes were running out of steam and rising more slowly -- 3% for
the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index and 1.5% for the Nasdaq Composite Index. Both hit records in March
2000 and neither is near a return to those levels.
The divergence troubles some investors.
"What you'd typically want to see is a broad-based rally with all boats rising," says Peter Wall, chief
investment officer for Chase Private Client Services, a brokerage and money-management arm of J.P.
Morgan Chase. The fact that some indexes are looking weaker than others "is a sign that we are in the
mature or the end phase of a bull market."
Many investors scoff at such worries. They see last week's market pullback on Thursday and Friday as a
temporary bout of profit-taking and inflation anxiety, which will be overcome as the economy and corporate
profits continue to perform strongly.
It is common for prominent indexes to fall back just as they bump up against an important market level, such
as a longstanding record. Similar to-ing and fro-ing occurred when the Dow ran into other milestones, such as
10000 and 11000. Professional investors often use the milestone as an opportunity to step back and reflect
on how far stocks have come, to take some profits and to see how stocks react.
It took more than a decade for the Dow industrials to break lastingly through the 1000 level, and the 10000
level also proved to be a longtime barrier.
The Dow industrials closed last Wednesday at 11642.65, just 80.33 points from their record. They then
tumbled to 11380.99 by week's end, but still were up 6.2% for this year.
Other barometers have been at or near records in recent days, including the Russell 2000 small-stock index,
the Dow Jones Transportation Average and indexes of economically sensitive stocks, banking stocks and
foreign stocks. They all were responding to reports showing the world economy expanding strongly and
corporate profits coming in ahead of expectations.
But the S&P 500 still would have to rise 18% to return to its record, and the Nasdaq would have to more than
double.
The reason isn't complicated. Both those indexes actually are up more than the Dow average since the latest
bull market began in October 2002. But they both had far more exposure to technology stocks than the Dow
during the bull market of the 1990s. When the tech bubble burst, the Nasdaq fell 78% before hitting bottom.
The S&P 500 fell 49%, while the Dow fell 38%. The Dow had less distance to retrace before returning to a
record.
There is something else going on, as well. Although technology stocks led the market higher in the first part of
the current bull market, they have been flagging of late, amid worries about intensifying competition among
makers of tech gear. That has hurt the Nasdaq and the S&P. With commodities such as oil, copper, zinc,
nickel, gold and silver soaring to levels not seen in many years, the companies that can raise prices are
those in commodity-related businesses. Stocks of construction- and mining-related companies such as
Caterpillar and Phelps Dodge have reached records.

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But rising commodity prices, together with surging bond yields and a sinking dollar, have put a squeeze on
some other companies' profits and rekindled inflation fears, which has been bad news for some other stocks.
Fewer stocks are hitting new highs now and some that were on the rise have suddenly fallen victim to
profit-taking.
So far, inflation in commodity prices including oil and gasoline hasn't pushed up other prices much, because
labor costs have remained moderate. That has permitted the Federal Reserve to continue indicating that it
hopes to take a pause before long in its campaign of interest-rate increases, which is aimed at preventing
inflation. But investors continue to bite their fingernails over the risk that broader inflation could appear.
"If the Fed engineers a soft landing," or a cooling of the economy without a recession, "we should see stock
gains continuing for the rest of the year," says Russ Koesterich, senior portfolio manager at Barclays Global
Investors in San Francisco. "But if the Fed has to keep raising rates, there is a higher risk" to stocks. "I don't
think it is helping that you've got long-term bond yields thumping up against four-year highs."
In the heart of the bull market of the 1990s, all the major indexes were hitting repeated records, and the gains
were fairly broad. "You want to see confirmation of all the indexes. It is a confirmation of broader strength,"
says Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md.
Bull markets in which major indexes fail to return to prior highs can be a sign of longer-running stock
weakness, of the kind investors suffered during the inflation-riddled 1970s, when bull markets repeatedly ran
out of steam, says Phil Roth, chief technical market analyst at New York brokerage firm Miller Tabak.
Even many investors who expect stocks to continue to rise this year warn that the gains could be single-digit
gains, not the blistering gains that stocks enjoyed during the bull market of the 1990s.

program.
Overall, small-caps underperformed the broad market on Friday and for the week as a whole.

10.3% year to date.
The S&P 600 declined 7.44, or 1.89%, to 386.69. For the week, the index lost 4.4% and year to date is up
10.3%.
The Russell 2000 saw its biggest weekly percentage decline since January 2005, while the S&P SmallCap
600 saw its largest weekly percentage fall since April 2005.
"More than anything, inflation fears were the underlying catalysts for the last couple of days," said James

If bond yields level out a little next week, the stock market could snap back, he added.

Taking TIPS on Inflation Concerns --- Hot Commodities Market And Doubts on Fed Stance Widen `Breakeven' Spread
Credit Markets
Taking TIPS on Inflation Concerns --- Hot Commodities Market And Doubts on Fed Stance Widen
`Breakeven' Spread
By Michael Mackenzie
711 words
13 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Red-hot commodity prices and doubts over the Federal Reserve's sanguine outlook for inflation are driving
investors into Treasury inflation-indexed securities, known as TIPS.
This demand has caused the difference in yields between cash 10-year Treasurys and TIPS, known as
breakevens, to near its widest levels since the inflation-protected government bond market was established
in 1997.
Breakevens are one of several inflation indicators followed by the Fed. The widening signals that investors
are worried about inflation pressures picking up.
Breakevens on 10-year TIPS stood Friday at 2.75 percentage points, just shy of the 2.78 percentage-point
peak seen in March. The 10-year Treasury note, the cash market's benchmark, lost 13/32 point, or $4.06, on
$1,000 face value, to 99 16/32 point. Its yield rose to 5.19% -- the highest in four years -- from 5.162%
Thursday, as yields move inversely to prices.
The move comes as prices for a range of commodities, from copper to gold, have been rising, though they
came off their highs Friday.
"It's more the commodity story heating up," said Gerald Lucas, Treasury trading strategist at Banc of America
Securities in New York. But he also said the market is a little confused about the message the Fed is sending
when it comes to fighting inflation.
Christopher Sullivan, portfolio manager at United Nations Federal Credit Union in New York, said investors
are pricing in higher risk premiums on longer-dated debt.
"The market's focus is upon the inflation outlook, commodities are on a tear, and TIPS [breakevens] have
been widening," said Mr. Sullivan.
Barclays Capital, in a report published this week, said the trend of rising inflation risk premiums "has further
to run, [and] is justified on several grounds," notably, "the sheer persistence of rising headline inflation."
March headline inflation was up 3.4% on the year, while core inflation stood at 2.1% -- at the upper end of
the Fed's tolerance zone.
Despite these pressures, the Fed appears to be fairly sanguine on inflation. As it raised the federal-funds
rate to 5% Wednesday, it said inflation expectations remain contained and rises in energy and other
commodity prices had had only a modest impact on core prices.
That has led Treasury market investors to think the Fed may not raise rates again at its meeting at the end of
June, a view reflected in the two-year yield sticking to just under 5%, below the fed-funds rate.
"If we combine a pause in monetary tightening with no evidence of slower U.S. nominal demand growth,
inflation risk premia on U.S. bonds can only move higher," wrote Barclays Capital in the report.
Add into this mix the dollar's sharp declines since late April -- which could add to inflation pressures as
imported goods prices become more expensive and also make foreign investors leery of buying U.S. assets
-- and there are reasons for concern.
Indeed, there are signs that the bond market is set to throw down a classic inflation challenge to the FOMC.
"Bond markets are recovering their historical role as inflation vigilantes, suggesting if the Fed does not
tighten, the bond market will," said Barclays.

Page 138 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fannie Mae's Surprise
Mortgage giant Fannie Mae surprised fixed-income markets Friday by announcing that it would not sell
noncallable benchmark notes in May. Investors had been expecting the company to announce plans to issue
$3 billion to $5 billion of two-year notes.
The last time Fannie skipped issuing benchmark notes was in December 2004. Fannie spokesman Jason
Lobo said the agency had indicated previously that it might exercise its option not to issue at some point.
Another government-sponsored enterprise, the Federal Home Loan Bank, meanwhile, announced it intends
to sell $4 billion of new two-year debt.
The FHLB tapped Bank of America Corp., Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. and J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. to
lead the deal, which will price next week.
Risk premiums on outstanding two-year agency debt, after narrowing on Fannie Mae's announcement,
moved back to nearly unchanged by the end of active trade.

Stocks Retreat As Inflation Fear Crimps the Rally
By E.S. Browning
527 words
12 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A DAY AFTER PUSHING to within 81 points of a record, the Dow Jones Industrial Average suffered its
biggest point and percentage decline since January, as investors took profits amid renewed inflation fears.
The Federal Reserve sent an uncertain signal Wednesday about how soon it might be able to pause in its
campaign of interest-rate increases, aimed at preventing inflation, and investors used that as a reason to
take some profits after the recent stock run-up.
The Dow, which had risen more than 5% in less than a month, yesterday fell 141.92 points, or 1.22%, to
11500.73. It remains up 7.3% for the year, but is 222.25 points from the Jan. 14, 2000, record close of
11722.98. Other indexes also fell, with small stocks falling harder than large ones.
Inflation worries were spurred by the dollar's continuing decline against the euro, and by sharp increases in
commodity prices. Gold futures jumped $16.10 to $719.80 a troy ounce, the highest finish since 1980.
Crude-oil futures rose $1.19 to $73.32 a barrel. Copper futures climbed to their highest level since the early
1980s.
Reflecting the inflation concerns, the yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note rose to 5.162%, a
four-year high.
The Dow industrials had risen for five consecutive days before Wednesday's Fed meeting. Investors were
hoping the Fed would signal how soon it could call a pause in the drumbeat of rate increases that has been
going on since June 2004. The central bank indicated that it hopes to pause at some point, but also
suggested that a decision on when to do it would depend on what economic data show about inflationary
pressure.
"The Fed left quite a bit of uncertainty on the table," said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund
group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. That, together with the inflation worries, gave people a reason to
cash in some chips.
Technology stocks, which have been in the doldrums lately, were among the biggest losers, sending the
Nasdaq Composite Index down 2.07%, or 48.04 points, to 2272.70. It still is up 3.1% in 2006. The Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index declined 1.28%, or 16.93 points, to 1305.92, still up 4.6% this year. Neither index is
close to its record, which in both cases dates from March 2000.
The Commerce Department's report on April retail sales came in lighter than expected, but that didn't stave
off the inflation worries.
American International Group, a component of the Dow industrials, fell 5% following a disappointing quarterly
profit report.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds retreated. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested. The
30-year bond was down 18/32 to yield 5.234%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 110.53 yen, up from 110.13. The euro rose against the
dollar to $1.2845 from $1.2807.

Tech Remains a Four-Letter Word To Investors Despite a Strong Year
Fund Track
Tech Remains a Four-Letter Word To Investors Despite a Strong Year
By Shefali Anand
761 words
12 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MUTUAL FUNDS that invest in technology stocks have risen in recent months, but despite the surge,
investors continue to pull money out of them.
In the year ended April 30, technology-focused funds returned nearly 31%, according to data firm Lipper Inc.
While some of that is because stocks overall have been rising, tech has been doing particularly well,
especially medium-size and smaller companies.
That hasn't been enough to persuade investors -- many still gun-shy after the 2000 dot-com bust -- to pile
back in to tech-stock funds. In the first three months of this year, investors pulled out $1.1 billion from the
sector. "People haven't forgotten what tech funds did to them in the bear markets," says Morningstar analyst
Karen Dolan.
The perils of tech were on display yesterday, when the sector's stocks took a tumble amid concerns that they
are particularly vulnerable if inflation rears its head. The tech-heavy Nasdaq composite fell 2.1% yesterday --
its second-biggest decline of the year.
For investors, it is a reminder that narrowly defined sector funds can be particularly volatile. Indeed, in the late
1990s tech funds clocked in with annual gains of 100% or more. However, after the Internet bubble burst,
average annual returns tumbled to negative 30% or 40% for several years running. Investors have pulled
money out of tech funds every year since 2001.
The reason tech has been doing better the past year or so is that companies have been posting better
earnings. These improvements have been concentrated in smaller companies that make hardware
components for computers, and some consumer-oriented companies like Google, which did well last year.
The biggest stocks -- Microsoft and Intel -- have been down.
Meanwhile, investors are still focused on other hot sectors. Natural-resources funds, for instance, have
received around $22 billion in inflows since the start of 2004, on the back of returns averaging 41.7% in the
three years ended April 30. Funds that invest in gold-oriented companies, which returned an average 42.14%
in the period, have received $2.3 billion in inflows.
Though no one is talking about a bubble in these sectors yet, investors should be cautious about putting in
too much money. The huge returns can't be expected to continue in the next few years, says Ms. Dolan, so
investors need to be "toning down" expectations. The best way to play well-performing sectors is to invest in
large diversified funds that have a significant position in these companies, she says.
Sector funds tend to be very cyclical, causing investor money to slosh back and forth from the
poor-performing to the well-performing funds, says Kurt Brouwer, at California financial advisory firm Brouwer
&Janachowski. He points to how stocks of commodity-based companies provided meager returns for 20
years since the 1980s, but then started to perk up in the past few years -- attracting huge inflows. "This
happens over and over again," he says.
For investors who want to play sector funds, the best strategy might be to take a contrarian view and put
money into funds that aren't doing very well right now. "Funds that are dogs today will be darlings tomorrow,"
Mr. Brouwer says. That takes guts, however, and is a game only for investors who are prepared to take
significant risks with their money. One strategy is to invest in multiple sector funds that are out of favor, with
the idea that one or more will eventually come back into style.
Arnie Berman, technology strategist at investment bank Cowen &Co., is optimistic about tech stocks in the
next two to four years. The reason: In recent months the companies have started investing more money in
expanding their businesses and on new projects. However, he says the short-term outlook of two to four
months is bearish because the results might take some time to show up.

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Among technology mutual funds, there are some key differences in their investment strategy today compared
with the late 1990s. Today, fund managers are paying more attention to companies' business fundamentals
and "tend to be less aggressive" in their stock picks, says Todd Trubey, an analyst at Morningstar.
Many also are looking abroad for potential stock picks, something that Mr. Trubey thinks is necessary for their
future, given that many hardware makers are now emerging in Asia. Janus Capital Group Inc.,
OppenheimerFunds and AllianceBernstein are among fund firms that offer global-technology funds.

Fed Raises Rates, Keeps Its Options Open for Future --- Central Bank Cites Worries Of Inflation Amid Growth, But Hints of a Possible Pause
Fed Raises Rates, Keeps Its Options Open for Future --- Central Bank Cites Worries Of Inflation Amid
Growth, But Hints of a Possible Pause
By Greg Ip
1,148 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As it raised interest rates by a quarter-point for the 16th consecutive time, the Federal Reserve, in its
accompanying statement, struck a careful balance between concern about inflation and a willingness to
consider pausing in its two-year rate-raising campaign.
The Fed, as expected, boosted short-term rates to 5% from 4.75%. In its statement, it laid out a rationale for
a pause in the series of rate increases that began in mid-2004, while signaling such a break need not be a
halt.
In recent weeks, Fed officials, led by Chairman Ben Bernanke, have tried to signal that they are less certain
of where rates are going and that while they may pause soon, they could raise rates again later. But those
signals have come while recent data have shown that the economy remains strong and inflationary pressure
is growing. Markets have questioned the wisdom of the Fed's game plan by showing greater concern about
inflation.
Yesterday's statement was less explicit than Mr. Bernanke's Congressional testimony two weeks ago on the
prospects of a pause. It restated the Fed's bias to raise interest rates further because of the risk of higher
inflation. But it also laid out a forecast of slowing growth that would allow it to pause on rate increases.
Growth has been "quite strong," the Fed said, but will "likely . . . moderate to a more sustainable pace, partly
reflecting a gradual cooling of the housing market and the lagged effects of increases in interest rates and
energy prices." Inflation expectations are "contained," but tightening capacity usage and high commodity
prices could add "potential" inflation pressure.
Further rate increases "may yet be needed to address inflation risks," the Fed said, slightly tweaking the
guidance it gave on interest rates after its previous two meetings. But it added that it "emphasizes that the
extent and timing of any such firming will depend importantly on the evolution of the economic outlook as
implied by incoming information." That wording suggests that the Fed still sees a reasonable chance of
having to raise rates again this year, but without the predictability of the past 23 months.
Stock and bond markets weakened following the Fed's statement, and finished mixed on the day, while
futures markets raised to 40% from 38% the odds of a quarter-point increase in rates at the Fed's next
meeting on June 28-29. The dollar recouped some of its losses against the euro, but remained lower on a
day when the highlight was a Treasury report that criticized China for holding down the value of its currency
but stopped short of calling it a currency manipulator.
All 11 voting members of the 18 member Federal Open Market Committee voted to raise the target for the
federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans among banks. (One FOMC seat is vacant.) The Fed also
raised the less-important discount rate, charged on central-bank loans to commercial banks, to 6% from
5.75%. Eleven of the Fed's 12 regional banks requested the increase; the board of the Kansas City Fed did
not, just as in March. In response, U.S. commercial banks raised their prime rate, a benchmark for many
business and consumer loans, to 8% from 7.75%.
The Fed and its new chairman are at a delicate juncture. At 5%, short-term rates are now about neutral --
neither so low that they stimulate spending, nor so high that they restrain it. And there are signs that, after two
years, the Fed is having an impact: Long-term rates have started to rise, and home sales and price
appreciation have slowed.
But the overall economy grew at a robust 4.8% annual rate in the first quarter, and though it's expected to
slow to between 3% and 3.5% in the second quarter, the latest data have been strong. Moreover, underlying
inflation recently ticked up to the top of the Fed's comfort zone of 1% to 2%. Thus, Mr. Bernanke is caught
between the risk of appearing soft on inflation and the risk of raising interest rates too much and triggering
a serious slowdown.

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
They are "concerned with the lagged impact of earlier tightening on economic activity -- it's the reason the
[notion of a pause] has been introduced," said Al Broaddus, former president of the Federal Reserve Bank of
Richmond. "But . . . they mustn't give anyone the idea their ultimate focus on inflation has diminished. It's
very tough to walk this fine line."
Robert Parry, former president of the San Francisco Fed, thinks the Fed will likely have to raise rates further
this year, in part because it usually has to raise them above neutral to get the economy to slow to a desirable
rate and keep inflation in its preferred zone.
Even so, he said the Fed would be justified in pausing at its next meeting because after several years in
which the direction of rates was straightforward, "it's much more complex, and one has to be careful and also
circumspect."
Mr. Bernanke seems more willing than his predecessor Alan Greenspan to lay out his forecast for the
economy to justify the Fed's rate actions. Edward Gramlich, a former Fed governor who is now interim
provost at the University of Michigan, said "it's inevitable" that the Fed must now rely on forecasts. The only
reason it can consider a pause, he said, is that it believes rates at current levels "have already dampened
spending enough to bring [inflation] under control."
However, that forecast may not yet ring true with people in the markets who still see a strong economy,
buoyant financial markets and inflation signals, such as gold prices hitting a 25-year high.
Hank McKinnell, chief executive of Pfizer Inc. and chairman of the Business Roundtable, an association of
chief executives, said yesterday: "Interest rates aren't at a point where they've started to bite into financing
capability. There's plenty of money around."

-- December 2005 "Measured increases "likely to be needed."
-- January 2006 Increases "may be needed."

Page 150 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fed-Side Manner
Fed-Side Manner
By David Malpass
1,178 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With a 16th rate hike just completed, calls for the Fed to pause are loud, as they were after almost every
previous increase. For the full-speed-ahead crowd, part of the American dream is to borrow low to buy a
home, car or college education. For the gloomier set, the economy is too far off track to survive, much less
prosper, with yet another hike. Less remarked upon are a stunning buildup of extra dollars at corporations
and central banks, the rush to gold, signs of rising inflation and accelerating global growth.
The question facing the Fed is whether interest rates are at an appropriate level. With the economy surging
and the dollar weak, the data argue that the Fed should adopt a more aggressive stance, not mull a pause.
The safer path, and likely the growth-maximizing one, is to take this opportunity of robust growth to stabilize
the dollar and get ahead of the inflation risk.
Some are saying the Fed can be as aggressive as it wants; it can't stop the 1970s-style flight from the dollar.
They talk of a Carter-Bush malaise and "benign" neglect of the dollar. More likely, the dollar, the Fed and its
new chairman have deep reservoirs of market confidence and respect, just not at this level of interest rates.
But until the Fed tests the impact of a hawkish tone -- including the flexibility for a 50 basis point hike -- it is
hard to know whether the dollar's latest plunge is terminal or just a wrong bet that the Fed will remain dovish.
Looking back, the Fed should have hiked rates faster in 2005. Now, with the economy equally robust, the
dollar weaker and inflation higher, logic argues for an aggressive stance to make up for last year's
accommodation, not a pause to absorb last year's hikes. Since the first rate increase in June 2004, the Fed
has talked softly and carried a little stick, hiking gingerly on the view that the recovery was fragile and might
soon slow. Quarter after quarter, the economic data has been strong, but the two-year buzz has assumed a
near-term slowdown due to rate hikes, the out-of-money consumer and high gasoline prices.
The gloom ignores reality. The U.S. is the world's biggest exporter, importer, producer, saver, innovator and,
for the industrialized world, job-creator. With inflation rising, real interest rates are still low despite the hikes.
The increases in disposable income from a healthy labor market outweigh high gasoline prices. The U.S.
household sector, the world's biggest net creditor, has shown increasing strength as rates rise. As a net
borrower, the business sector will bear the biggest impact if the Fed gets tight, yet profits are still hitting new
records, especially for the smaller, more credit-hungry businesses most sensitive to rates.
The result has been a Fed chasing "neutral," the level of interest rates consistent with price stability. But
each time it chooses small hikes, it encourages projects, investments and schemes dependent on the Fed
undershooting. While interest rates have risen, so have opportunities. The profitability of borrowing has
gained from innovation, leverage and the broadening of growth beyond the U.S. Borrowers have had time to
lengthen maturities while rates were low, insulating themselves from hikes. Also pushing up neutral, the
pipeline of excess liquidity is still filling despite rate hikes, most visibly in commodity speculation and record
levels of corporate cash.
Confidence in the Fed and the dollar are vital U.S. assets, especially with Washington's political climate
deeply polarized. Judging by the CPI and the broader GDP deflator, actual inflation has already reached its
highest level since 1991. The primary indicators of excess dollar liquidity -- gold, commodities and the dollar
itself -- are signaling doubts about the Fed. Of course, the core CPI and the core personal consumption
deflator (to be revised in July) argue that inflation excluding energy was somewhat contained through March.
Inflation-indexed Treasurys are also cited as providing a quiescent inflation indicator, calculated by
subtracting their yield from the yield on normal Treasury bonds. But unlike gold, the inflation track record on
TIPS is short, ambiguous -- and heavily distorted by illiquidity, oil prices (which are included in their return),
and the same excess liquidity "conundrum" that has been confusing yields across the bond spectrum.
The Fed has to make the decision, but it should be based on price stability and the value of the dollar, not the
market's consistent underestimates of U.S. economic prospects. The negative view of the economy runs
deep, penetrating Wall Street, both political parties and sometimes the president and Fed. Some see the U.S.

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
as a has-been, burdened with deficits, a housing bubble, low savings and grim prospects. Those criticisms,
made loudly in 2004 and 2005 as well, coincide with polls showing that a majority thinks the U.S. is on a
wrong economic path. Even many Republicans cringe at the tax debate over rewarding the rich (forgetting
that the lower cost of capital causes job growth and innovation), think America is broke and ponder a $100
gasoline rebate to shore up the consumer.
Both parties rue the trade deficit; but with the exception of the post-WWII rebuilding of Europe and Japan, the
trade deficit and mirror-image capital surplus have been fixtures of the American boom since colonial days,
adding to growth, investment and innovation. The writer Gregg Easterbrook has argued that looking on the
dark side may be deep in our genes -- a survival mechanism from the days of tigers, and well before the bulls
and bears that depress us now.
Lost in the talk of malaise, small businesses, innovation and labor flexibility are driving an economic
juggernaut that sailed past Katrina, rate hikes and high energy prices. The "wrong-path" U.S. proudly led
global relief to victims of the tsunami, Pakistan's earthquake, Saddam and African AIDS, with our private
sector donations often outweighing the rest of the world's entire contribution. For bold growth policies to
blossom in Washington, the hesitant self-congratulation needs to turn into a deep conviction that the
economy is sturdy and successful. Inflation and dollar weakness are the biggest risks, not the slowdown that
dominates the news.
The Fed plays a key role in controlling these risks and setting the economic tone. It has allowed the
perception that it expects a weaker economy because of past hikes, not a strong one because of past
accommodation. This keeps interest-rate expectations too low to support the dollar, which in turn verifies the
gloom. A better path would be for the Fed to recognize the U.S. achievement: a robust, profitable economy
nearing full employment and deserving a strong and stable dollar. It's time rates actually got to neutral, rather
than just creep higher. A Fed funds rate high enough to stop dollar weakness would help shake off the
malaise.
---
Mr. Malpass is Bear Stearns's chief economist.

Dollar Declines Broadly On Fed Rate Indication --- Signal of Pause in Raises, Treasury Decision on

11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal

The dollar was lower across the board, after the Federal Reserve set the stage for a possible pause in rate
increases and the U.S. Treasury declined to name China a currency manipulator.
Choppy trading dominated markets as the dollar bounced up and down following the Fed's statement and
Treasury's report. The dollar had initially fallen a bit following the Fed's statement, but recovered modestly
after the semiannual Treasury report on international economic and foreign-exchange policies.
Prior to the afternoon's news, the dollar had been down versus its widely traded rivals, but its losses weren't
aggravated by either the Fed decision or the Treasury report. In overnight trading, a rise in the price of gold
to a new 26-year high above $700 an ounce had weighed on the U.S. currency.
"Neither of the responses have been very big," said Eric Darwell, currency strategist at Citigroup in New York,
referring to market reaction to the Fed statement and the Treasury report.
"We saw a bit of a dollar rally immediately after the Fed but then a loss, and the same is happening now [after
the Treasury report]," he added. "There isn't much take-away" to give the market direction, Mr. Darwell said.
Late in New York, the euro was trading at $1.2807 from $1.2752 late Tuesday. The dollar was at 110.13 yen
from 111.12 yen late Tuesday. The euro was at 141.24 yen from 141.76 yen, and the dollar was quoted at
1.2155 Swiss francs from 1.2212 francs late Tuesday. Sterling was trading at $1.8667 from $1.8653.
The Fed raised rates for the 16th consecutive time at its meeting yesterday but left in doubt what direction it
will take at its next meeting as markets interpreted the accompanying statement as neutral.
In the wake of the statement, the dollar gyrated in choppy trading as investors attempted to discern if the
statement could be construed as dovish or hawkish. The euro initially dropped near the $1.2750 level
following the statement, then suddenly snapped back above the $1.2800 level to touch fresh one-year highs
versus the dollar at $1.2838.
Most analysts agreed that the statement is little different from previous statements and that the Fed is still
data dependent.
"The market took the Fed's view on board and decided to continue to sell the dollar as they did before," noted
Marc Chandler, global head of foreign exchange at Brown Brother Harriman in New York. "The move seems
to be about sentiment and psychology."
Indeed, the dollar wound lower through most of the session prior to the Fed news, dipping to a series of
one-year lows versus the euro and eight-month bottoms versus the yen.
"In the current dollar-negative environment [the Fed statement] isn't sufficient to cause a rally in the dollar,"
said Brian Dolan, the head of currency research at Gain Capital in Bedminster, N.J. But the Fed's statement
"will allow the dollar to stabilize at lower levels."

Bull Run -- Behind Surging Stock Market: Old-Fashioned Economic Boom --- Defying Gloomy Predictions, Profits Rise, Prices Don't; Dow's...
Bull Run -- Behind Surging Stock Market: Old-Fashioned Economic Boom --- Defying Gloomy
Predictions, Profits Rise, Prices Don't; Dow's Record Approaches --- The Return of `Goldilocks'
By E.S. Browning
2,038 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After Hurricane Katrina hobbled economic growth late last year and as oil prices soared, many investors
thought the economy, corporate profits and the stock market would be running on empty by now.
Instead, an economic rebound has sent corporate profits to an 11th consecutive quarter of double-digit gains,
the longest streak since at least the 1950s. Surprisingly strong growth in the economy and corporate profits
has shaken stocks out of their doldrums. The Dow Jones Industrial Average is now within sight of its record
close.
Unlike the great 1990s bull market, which was sustained by a wave of new technology, this one has the feel
of an old-fashioned economic boom, the type investors saw in the 1950s and 1960s. What many thought
would be a limited rebound created by Chinese industrial demand has turned into a long-running story as
once-unloved sectors such as commodity producers and oil drillers continue to thrive. Helping fuel the U.S.
stock surge are once-skeptical investors, who are now funneling money into the market in the hope of getting
in on a lengthier boom.
Despite yesterday's 16th consecutive interest-rate increase by the Federal Reserve, the Dow industrials
finished at 11642.65, up 2.88 points, just 80.33 points short of the record hit at the height of the technology
bubble on Jan. 14, 2000. (Markets often take a short breather in the face of milestone numbers.) After falling
a little less than 1% last year, the Dow has risen 8.6% in 2006. In total, it's gained almost 60% since the bear
market ended in October 2002 with the blue-chip average at 7286.27.
"The economy continues to surprise people and earnings continue to surprise people," says Charles Mayer,
who heads the U.S. money-management team at Pioneer Investments in Boston, where he supervises about
$50 billion in investments. "A lot of these stocks still have a lot of strength." He's sticking with oil companies,
commodity producers and mining companies, which have powered much of the gain in his funds.
Bearish investors warn that the latest market surge is just a "blowoff" in which stocks make a final push
before turning down, perhaps later this year. They point to lurking inflation pressures and worry that the
market already is showing signs of age, with fewer and fewer stocks hitting new highs. That means indexes
are being driven by an ever-smaller group of companies.
Investors are closely watching whether corporate profits continue to exceed rising expectations, or start to fall
short, as they did when the last bear market began in 2000. Notably, both the Standard &Poor's 500-stock
index and the Nasdaq Composite Index remain well below their own records because of their heavy exposure
to hard-hit technology stocks.
What helped drive stocks higher in the late 1990s was the rise of the Internet and information technology.
They combined to boost labor productivity and to spark the market's love affair with technology companies
such as Cisco Systems Inc. and Dell Inc.
Today, the stock market is benefiting from something broader: consumer demand at home and powerful
economic growth abroad.
A series of developments that skeptics thought would kill the bull market -- resurgent inflation, rising labor
costs, falling consumer spending, a collapsing housing market -- haven't materialized. Federal Reserve
Chairman Ben Bernanke gave investors a boost in late April when he told Congress that surging energy
prices have so far failed to bleed into other prices.
While the Fed raised its benchmark overnight lending rate to 5% yesterday, many investors saw the Fed's
subsequent statement as an indication that it's prepared to call at least a temporary pause to its rate
increases, possibly as soon as its next meeting in late June. Any decision will depend on whether signs of
inflation turn up in the economic data.

--- Page 154 ---
Page 154 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"You think of how crude oil has gone through $40, $50, $60, now $70" a barrel, says Michael Driscoll, who
trades oil stocks at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. "Every prognosticator has said that these kinds of
oil-price increases are going to force the economy to a grinding halt. It hasn't happened."
Despite other inflation forerunners, such as high gold prices, the falling dollar and rising bond yields,
inflation hasn't yet turned into a serious problem. The yield of the benchmark, 10-year Treasury note, which
drives other interest rates such as fixed mortgage rates, remains low by past standards even though it
jumped to 5.13%, from less than 4% last year.
The situation resembles the Goldilocks economy of the 1990s -- not too hot, not too cold -- with inflation
moderate and economic growth downright strong. The annual economic-growth rate hit 4.8% in the first
quarter, the fastest pace since 2003. Compensation costs for employers rose only 0.6% in the first quarter,
the smallest quarterly gain in nearly seven years.
Despite sluggish wage growth, consumer spending rose at a 5.5% annual rate in the same period. In April,
the first month of the new quarter, chain-store sales surpassed expectations, reinforcing the idea that
consumer spending remains strong.
Large companies reported first-quarter profit increases of 14%, the 11th consecutive quarter of double-digit
gains, according to Thomson Financial, which tracks profit reports. That's the longest streak since Thomson
began tracking such data in 1954. The profits reflect both the economy's strength and extensive cost-cutting
during the 2000-02 bear market, which left companies more careful about how they spend.
The stock market's ability to keep powering ahead will depend heavily on whether this scenario can be
sustained. Despite doubts, some money managers are barreling into small, fast-growing stocks of the kind
that have led the market higher. Unlike in the 1990s, the market's leaders aren't big household names such
as Intel and Dell, but smaller companies involved in businesses such as mining and industrial equipment.
Market bull Douglas Cote is buying the stocks of companies whose performance is closely tied to economic
growth, notably small companies such as JLG Industries Inc., which makes machinery for mines and industry,
and Christopher &Banks Corp., a women's clothing retailer.
"The reason I am bullish is that corporate profits continue to defy expectations," says Mr. Cote, who oversees
$15 billion at New York money-management group ING Investment Management. "If corporate profits are
surging, you should be in the market."
Small stocks tend to be more volatile and less protected from economic vagaries than larger stocks. They're
often the first to decline when a bull market peaks. Mr. Cote, however, is convinced that small stocks still
have room to rise, even though their indexes have been hitting records for months. By his calculations,
analysts' forecasts call for profits at small companies to grow twice as fast as at large ones, something that
happens when the economy remains strong.
It is a truism of investing that markets respond not to actual news, but to investors' expectations. Bull markets
often begin after recessions when rebounding companies easily exceed expectations. As a bull market
matures, expectations rise and the rate of economic progress tends to slow. Bull markets ultimately fail when
expectations get so high that corporate performances no longer can surpass them.
Since the start of the 20th century, bull markets have averaged a little more than two years in duration. Some
analysts worry that this one, which has been going on for three and a half years, is overdue for at least a 10%
pullback.
Others note that bull markets have been getting longer and stronger. The longest since 1900 was the bull
market of the 1990s, which ran from late 1990 until early 2000. The current one is the fifth longest, according
to Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla.
Economists and money managers who thought that mundane commodities had no place in an
information-age portfolio have been proven wrong. Exxon Mobil's stock has doubled since the middle of 2002
and is up almost 14% this year alone. Its market capitalization is now greater than that of 1990s
market-darlings General Electric Co. and Microsoft Corp. Exxon now has a total market value of about $390
billion, the world's largest.
Surging economies in developing countries are helping drive this phenomenon. China and India are running
through industrial commodities at unprecedented rates, bolstering the performance of U.S. commodity stocks.
Short-term copper futures now trade at six times their 2001 levels and oil and nickel futures have more than
quadrupled.
Optimists say this kind of world-wide economic growth is one reason to believe the economy has entered a
phase of "secular," or long-running growth, not just a temporary "cyclical" upswing. They also cite the

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
cost-cutting of the last bear market, the changing economics of oil and low interest rates. Many believe the
skeptics will continue to be surprised at the strength of the economy, and the market.
"The move in energy, commodities and industrials is a larger secular move than some people give it credit
for," says Mr. Mayer of Pioneer Investments.
"To produce $1 of GDP, you use half as much oil as you did in 1964," adds Mr. Cote. "That is because we are
transitioning from a manufacturing economy to a service economy and you need a lot less oil in a service
economy. Oil isn't going to bring this economic growth to a halt any time soon."
Further supporting the stock run-up, some long-time skeptics recently have thrown in the towel.
For much of the current bull market, Merrill Lynch investment strategist Richard Bernstein urged clients to
invest in bonds and stable companies such as food and drug makers that perform better in a slowing
economy. He thought aggressive Fed interest-rate increases, aimed at preventing inflation, would hold back
the economy. But since late last year, the Fed has been giving ever-stronger indications that it's preparing to
halt its interest-rate increases.
"I personally think that the Fed is making a mistake, but who cares" what I think, Mr. Bernstein says.
Ultimately, he says, the Fed may be forced to fight inflation by raising rates again, but for now, he wants
clients to go where the money is. That means switching to more-speculative stocks that benefit from a strong
economy, such as technology, biotech and mid-tier energy companies.
However, some money managers who have been bullish for years are using the rally as an opportunity to
take profits. Jon Brorson, head of growth-stock investing at New York money-management firm Neuberger
Berman, is paring holdings of the stocks that gave him solid profits in recent years, such as Boeing Co.,
Murphy Oil Corp., copper-maker Phelps Dodge Corp. and oil refiner Valero Energy Corp.
Low interest rates have pumped huge amounts of what economists call liquidity, or available cash, into the
stock market. Mr. Brorson believes it will begin to dry up, producing a stock pullback centering on the biggest
recent gainers. Although he sees no signs yet that the economy is slowing, interest-rate increases often take
months to show their effects. "I think the Fed eventually will get its man: It will slow the economy down," Mr.
Brorson says.
Money manager Steve Leuthold, chairman of Leuthold Weeden Research in Minneapolis, is trying to have it
both ways. Since the autumn, he has been cutting his exposure to U.S. and foreign stocks for his main
investment fund, figuring that both groups are due for a pullback.
But he kept hold of commodity-related stocks, believing that the commodity boom has more room to run.
Even though his fund lost some money because of the collapse of commodity trader Refco Inc., his
commodity-related stock investments have soared. That helped push his main investment fund up 15% last
year, even though he's been wrong -- so far at least -- about the direction of the overall stock market.

Politics &Economics: Focus of Nigerian Fraud Inquiry Shifts to Prominent Swiss Banker
Politics &Economics: Focus of Nigerian Fraud Inquiry Shifts to Prominent Swiss Banker
By Charles Fleming and Russell Gold
733 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GENEVA -- In a move that could deepen international inquiries into whether the late Nigerian dictator Sani
Abacha stole billions of dollars and took large bribes from U.S. and European contractors, Swiss investigators
in the case are focusing on a prominent Geneva banker.
Gabriel Katri, a Swiss-Israeli banker in his late 60s, was arrested in late January on money-laundering
charges and held for four days before being released on bail, according to Mr. Katri's lawyer, Alec Reymond.
Mr. Reymond said in an interview that Mr. Katri, who remains under investigation, denies the laundering
charges but was cooperative during questioning by the Swiss investigating magistrate.
According to people familiar with the probe, Swiss investigators believe the banker holds the key to some of
the methods that they suspect Mr. Abacha used to process money he allegedly took from the Nigerian
Central Bank while he was in power between 1993 and 1998, when he died. They said Mr. Katri didn't
provide significant information during his January questioning. Mr. Katri, who in the mid-1990s was running
his own Swiss financial company called GK Finance &Commerce SA, declined a request to be interviewed for
this article.
The current Nigerian government has said it estimates at least $2 billion was stolen from the Nigerian Central
Bank during Mr. Abacha's time in power, in addition to an unquantifiable amount paid by foreign companies
in kickbacks on commercial contracts. Nigeria has been trying to recover that money from accounts in a
number of countries around the world, including Switzerland. The Swiss government in September agreed to
return $458 million to Nigeria, identified as money deposited in Swiss banks by the late dictator or his
representative.
Investigators hope Mr. Katri can help in a separate probe into whether a construction consortium, led by a
Halliburton Co. subsidiary, paid substantial bribes to Nigerian officials to win a multibillion dollar contract in
the 1990s to build a liquefied-natural-gas plant near Port Harcourt, Nigeria.
The U.S. Justice Department, in conjunction with authorities in France, Switzerland and Nigeria, is
investigating whether $132 million paid by the consortium to British lawyer Jeffrey Tesler was used for bribes.
Mr. Tesler, who has denied wrongdoing, has told investigators that Mr. Katri helped set up bank accounts in
Geneva where some of the money was deposited. A Halliburton spokeswoman said "we do not believe there
was any relationship between the company and Mr. Katri."
A key indication of Mr. Katri's possibly important role in handling illicit money for Mr. Abacha and other
Nigerians came from a 2004 investigation by the Central Bank of Kenya into international accounts held by
Kenya's Transnational Bank Ltd.
The Kenyan investigation had been requested by the current Nigerian government. According to a summary
of the 2004 Kenyan investigation's findings, reviewed by The Wall Street Journal, Transnational Bank
handled $198.6 million of Mr. Abacha's funds between 1996 and 1999. The summary said Transnational
Bank handled the funds by using its own accounts, known as "nostro accounts," held with a number of
international institutions, including Deutsche Bank AG and Citigroup Inc.'s Citibank. Spokesmen for these
banks declined to comment, and Transnational Bank didn't respond to requests for comment.
Nostro accounts are used by banks to conduct foreign exchange more efficiently by combining a large
number of small transactions. Investigators in the Kenyan probe alleged in their findings that Transnational
Bank's nostro accounts were used to obscure the provenance of Mr. Abacha's money by making it appear
that the funds came from a large pool of smaller customers rather than just one individual -- a technique
known as "layering."

--- Page 160 ---
Page 160 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Of the $198.6 million, the investigators identified $38 million that was transmitted through Transnational
Bank's nostro accounts to accounts held by Mr. Katri in Israel, Switzerland, the Cayman Islands and Nassau,
the Bahamas.
Corruption in oil-producing nations is a longstanding issue. In Africa, it has siphoned off much-needed
development funds and propped up brutal dictators such as Mr. Abacha. The Nigerian dictator is widely seen
as the worst in a long line of corrupt leaders in that country, looting it of much of its oil wealth during his years
in power.
---
Glenn Simpson in Brussels contributed to this article.

Dow Ends Flat As Fed's Signal Stumps Investors
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Ends Flat As Fed's Signal Stumps Investors
By Ian McDonald and Aaron Lucchetti
531 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE FEDERAL RESERVE has often sent markets spiraling or rallying. Yesterday it froze them.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average is still about 80 points from its record high, after finishing the day only
slightly above where it began. The benchmark idled because investors couldn't wring enough optimism out of
a statement from the Fed, which raised its short-term rate target to 5% as expected.
Many hoped the central bankers would signal a pause in its rate-raising campaign in its statement. But the
Fed failed to provide investors with a straight answer.
Further rate hikes "may yet be needed to address inflation risks" the Federal Open Market Committee said,
adding the word "yet" to its previous statements on rates. And doubts about that single word left stocks
treading water.
"The Fed was the story of the day and `yet' was the key word," said Ted Oberhaus, director of equity trading
at Lord Abbett Funds. The key question: Does the inclusion of "yet" indicate a pause is more likely -- or less?
The Dow edged up 2.88 points, or 0.02%, to 11642.65. It is up more than 8.6% this year and one good day
from its highest close, 11722.98, set on Jan. 14, 2000. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index finished down
2.29 points at 1322.85, while the tech-heavy Nasdaq Composite slid 17.51 points to 2320.74. Those indexes
are much further from their all-time highs also set in 2000.
Doreen Mogavero, a floor broker at the NYSE, predicts the Dow will hit a new record soon and then shoot
higher. "There will be a lot of woo-wooing and throwing paper," she said.
Others worry that psychologically important milestones, like the Dow's record, can distort investor sentiment,
leading to irrational buying.
"It's close enough that I have a hard time believing they, whoever they are, won't push this thing up to a
record soon," said Jeffrey Saut, chief investment strategist at Raymond James, who says stocks are
expensive at today's levels. "People will be excited, but I don't understand it and I don't trust it."
Treasury bonds, whose prices tend to weaken when short-term interest rates are on the rise, fell after the
Fed's statement but finished up a whisker on the day. The dollar continued its slide. After the Fed's move,
major U.S. banks raised their prime rates, the basis for many consumer loans, by one-quarter point to an
even 8%.
Fueling inflation concerns, oil futures rose $1.44 to $72.13 a barrel and gold jumped $4.30 to $703.70 an
ounce, its high since Sept. 25, 1980.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed; bonds climbed. The 10-year Treasury gained 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000
invested, to yield 5.128%. The 30-year bond added 6/32 to yield 5.193%.
The dollar declined, to 110.13 yen, down from 111.12; the euro rose to $1.2807 from $1.2752.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Peter McKay
401 words
11 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Gold Bugs
Investing pros have begun pondering the possibility that gold will hit a record over $800 a troy ounce soon.
This means that the gold bugs -- that patchwork of sometimes offbeat investors who love the shiny stuff --
are happier, and louder, than ever.
Gold futures for May delivery have risen this week 3.2%, or $21.50, to a 25-year high of $703.70 per troy
ounce. In the past two years, prices have soared 86%.
"We all said what was going to happen and why," says Bill Murphy, an ex-professional football player and
trader who heads up a group that goes by the name Gold Anti-Trust Action Committee, or GATA.
There are lots of reasons behind the move: For one, the dollar has weakened, and seems headed lower.
When people lose confidence in paper currency some turn to gold, which is still seen in some parts of the
world as an alternative store of value, despite many years in disfavor. This reputation as a store of financial
value means it is also seen as a hedge against inflation, which shows signs of picking up, and against
political turmoil, like say, turmoil in Venezuela, Iran and Iraq.
For some gold bugs, though, it's rarely that simple. Back in 1999, when gold was at multiyear lows around
$250, GATA argued the metal's price was being artificially suppressed by a cartel of large private banks
selling borrowed gold, much of it on loan from powerful central banks. The latest stage of the rally, Mr.
Murphy says, is because Russia has become a heavy buyer, helping to squeeze the alleged cartel.
"They're a little conspiratorial, for me even," says money manager Peter Schiff, an outspoken gold bug
himself. "I don't know if there was any real orchestrated event."
Whatever the case, the gold bugs aren't alone anymore. The investment audience for the precious metal is
broadening as hedge funds and others seek alternatives to stocks and bonds. Because the gold market is
relatively small, in terms of physical metal available and the number of investors who have traditionally
participated, even a small increase in mass appetite for the metal can result in more price increases.
Pick your reason. Gold seems headed higher still.
---

Dollar Falls Amid Speculation About Today's Fed Rate Decision
Currency Trading
Dollar Falls Amid Speculation About Today's Fed Rate Decision
By Isabelle Lindenmayer
494 words
10 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar was down against major counterparts after a day of choppy trading before the Federal Reserve's
interest-rate decision and the U.S. Treasury's report on foreign exchange today.
The dollar movements were largely speculative and highlighted the fragility of the U.S. currency, analysts
said.
Grant Wilson, senior foreign-exchange trader at Mellon Bank, said, "A lot of this is just conjecture; basically
we're back on the trend of pegging the dollar lower." Though the major currencies had started making moves
before this week, "people are trying to position themselves" before today's events.
Late in New York, the euro was trading at $1.2752 from $1.2706 late Monday. The dollar was at 111.12 yen
from 111.69 yen late Monday. The euro was around 141.70 yen from 141.92 yen, and the dollar was quoted
at 1.2212 Swiss francs from 1.2282. Sterling was trading at $1.8653 from $1.8573.
The market will carefully look at the statement that accompanies the Federal Open Market Committee's
interest-rate decision to be announced today around 2:15 p.m. EDT. The FOMC is expected to lift rates by a
quarter-point to 5%, marking its 16th consecutive rate increase.
Should the Fed leave the door open for further tightening, the dollar could rebound, though it is likely to be
only a short-term correction, analysts said.
Markets also await release late today of the Treasury's semiannual foreign-exchange report to Congress,
likely to echo the Group of Seven leading industrial nations' call for emerging Asian currency appreciation,
which would put downward pressure on the dollar.
The dollar started mixed against the majors, but fell throughout the morning, dipping below 111 yen to a
seven-month low. Then the dollar gained rapidly against the yen on talk of unusual trading activity by
Japanese entities.
The 110-yen level is important, as Japanese exporters are unhedged against the yen's strengthening further
than 110.
The dollar also fell against the euro, which came close to a one-year high in the North American morning
session, but then retreated a bit along with the yen.
Among the factors that stymied the dollar's ability to make short-term corrections was a report that Iran could
launch an oil-trading market denominated in euros rather than the dollar, analysts said.
Also putting downward pressure on the dollar, gold neared $700 an ounce, reaching a nearly 26-year high
and leading investors to sell dollar-denominated assets for gold.
The Brazilian real strengthened against the dollar after announcement of two large corporate deals that likely
will involve heavy dollar inflows, traders said. The Chilean peso also closed stronger against the U.S. dollar in
tracking gains of the Brazilian currency.
---
Azam Ahmed in New York and Alastair Stewart in Sao Paulo contributed to this article.

Gold Futures Surge on Weakness in Dollar, U.S. Response to Iran
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Surge on Weakness in Dollar, U.S. Response to Iran
By Alison Guerriere Ciaccio
509 words
10 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A confluence of bullish factors fueled a rally in precious metals yesterday on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange as the current-month gold contract closed just shy of $700 an ounce, at a 26-year
high.
May futures gained $21.60, to $699.40 an ounce. The most-active June contract did break $700, gaining
$21.60 an ounce to $701.50, just off the day's high of $702.20.
Gold still remains below a high of $850 on the cash market, a London fix price, set on Jan. 21, 1980. On that
date, futures hit an intraday high of $875 before settling at $825.50.
Adjusted for inflation, gold would need to trade to $2,200 to match those highs.
Yesterday's rally was sparked by the euro's gains against the dollar, and U.S. rejection of a letter sent by Iran
suggesting "new solutions" to the countries' differences set off renewed buying interest early in the session.
Crude oil's rally on the news also led the way for gold to rise, traders said.
"It's not surprising that the U.S. rejected the letter from Iran, but it has helped to renew interest [in gold] after
the cautious approach" on Monday following the release of the letter, said Stephen Platt, a commodities
analyst at Global Commodity Futures in Chicago.
Entangled in the Iran-U.S. news is a Federal Reserve meeting today that could give the market clues as to
whether there will be a pause in interest-rate increases.
"There is a possibility that the Fed will pause and stop raising rates in June but that won't be enough to choke
out inflation and therefore there will still be inflation fears and pressures," said Michael Cuggino, president
and portfolio manager at the Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds, noting his long-term bullish outlook for
gold.
While expectations are that Fed policy makers may increase the federal-funds target rate another quarter
point to 5%, many market participants forecast a pause in monetary tightening might occur soon.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rose almost $1 in a sharp recovery from Monday's steep losses. The U.S. rejection of
the much-touted letter from Iran's president reawakened concerns about the oil-rich country's nuclear
program. The June contract settled 92 cents higher at $70.69 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
COPPER: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex rose to record highs, supported by crude oil, precious
metals' strength and weakness in the dollar, along with tight supplies and strong demand. Mexican miner
Grupo Mexico said it will close its copper, silver and zinc mine in the north-central state of Zacatecas because
of a strike by miners there. Nearby May copper gained 9.05 cents to $3.6960 a pound and peaked at
$3.7000, a Comex high.

Stick It to Foreigners With Cheaper Dollars?
Letters to the Editor
Stick It to Foreigners With Cheaper Dollars?
427 words
10 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Martin Feldstein advocates dollar depreciation ("The Dollar at Home -- and Abroad," editorial page, April 28).
He states that "a dollar that is more competitive relative to [the Euro, yen and Canadian dollar] will cause
Americans to buy more U.S.-made products instead of imports from those countries." I can understand why,
say, the United Steel Workers union would favor this monetary form of trade protectionism, but how can an
economist seriously believe that it is good for Americans to have to pay higher prices for what they want?
American consumers wouldn't be the only ones to pay a price for Mr. Feldstein's policy prescription.
Foreigners holding dollars are in his crosshairs, too. "The longer we wait [to cheapen the dollar] . . . it will take
a larger fall for the dollar to repay the debt incurred by today's imports." Mr. Feldstein is saying that, sooner or
later, Americans should stick it to foreigners by paying off our debts to them in cheaper dollars. There
appears to be little honor or justice in monetary realpolitik.
Mark Hendrickson
New Wilmington, Pa.
---
With personal respect for Martin Feldstein, his rationales for manipulation of past and current values of the
dollar are typical of demand-side thinking during these past 35 years of chaos in international monetary
affairs.
He blames the crashes of economies in southeast Asia and Argentina during the late 1990s on the error of
producers in those countries who borrowed in dollars, rather than on the heavily deflated dollar that wound up
smashing U.S. financial markets as well.
Mr. Feldstein says: "The U.S. is a major exporter even with today's overstrong dollar." Has he not noticed that
the dollar's value has fallen off the table's edge, with the gold price rocketing from $380 in April 2004, to
above $650 an ounce today? This has occurred while the FOMC and its recently departed chairman raised
interest rates and yearned for the weaker dollar and improved current account balance Mr. Feldstein seeks.
Yet what has this policy of higher- priced imports and lower-priced exports yielded? The U.S. current account
deficit today is higher than ever, precisely because Americans must pay more for purchases and receive less
for sales.
A dollar with stable value tied to a price of gold would serve both Americans and global economies far better
than the morass of abstract theories that epitomize current U.S. monetary practices.
Wayne Jett
Managing Principal
Classical Capital LLC
Pasadena, Calif.

Dow Rises Again, Nears a Record; Gold Tests $700
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Rises Again, Nears a Record; Gold Tests $700
By Peter A. McKay
557 words
10 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average took another step toward a record, but other stock-market measures
were mixed. Gold prices rose again, nearing $700 an ounce.
Corporate news -- including a lowered profit forecast by computer maker Dell -- pushed major indexes
around, but many investors were sitting on the sidelines ahead of today's meeting of Federal Reserve
policymakers. A statement from the Fed, including a possible interest-rate increase, is expected around 2:15
p.m. Eastern time.
The Dow climbed 55.23 points, or 0.5%, to 11639.77, up 8.6% on the year and 83.21 points shy of the record
set in January 2000. General Motors, the Dow's most downtrodden component in recent months, rallied,
helped by the company's release of revised financial results for the first quarter and an upgrade by Deutsche
Bank analysts, who now view the auto maker's stock as a "hold" rather than a "sell."
The Fed is widely expected to raise its key target for short-term interests rate one-quarter percentage point to
5%. But Wall Street will look for signs that this hike will be the last in a series that has already lasted more
than two years, said Jim Bianco, president of Bianco Research, a Chicago research firm.
"People are so sensitive to the language the Fed uses," said Mr. Bianco. "Billions of dollars will change hands
because someone moved a comma."
Among other major market indicators, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.48 point, less than 0.1%,
to 1325.14, up 6.2% on the year. The Nasdaq Composite Index shed 0.3%, or 6.74 points, to 2338.25, up 6%
on the year, as Dell's woes spread elsewhere in the technology sector.
Besides the Fed's statement, investors today will respond early to the earnings of two bellwether companies.
Following yesterday's close of trading, Walt Disney and Cisco Systems announced results that beat analysts'
expectations. Cisco rose initially in after-hours trading but ended down 1.5%; Disney shares rose 1.4%.
Gold for May delivery jumped 3.2%, or $21.60, to a fresh 25-year high of $699.40 a troy ounce on Nymex's
Comex division, boosted by dollar weakness. Futures for June delivery, the most-active contract, finished
above the psychologically important level of $700 at $701.50, also up $21.60.
The rally in the yellow metal and other commodities is drawing interest from investors who haven't dabbled in
the sector in the past. "People are using gold to diversify," said Richard Radez, founder of the
precious-metals consulting firm Radez Investment Group Inc.
Crude oil ended at $70.69 per barrel, up 92 cents or 1.3% on Nymex, and up 16% on the year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose and bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note shed 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield up to 5.134%. The 30-year Treasury bond shed 7/32 to yield 5.206%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 111.12 yen, down from 111.69 yen, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2752 from $1.2706.
