Dow Industrials Hit 6-Year High As Crude Slumps --- Chain-Store Sales Lift Shares And Transports
Rise on Oil Drop; Bonds Slide on Inflation Worries
By E.S. Browning
494 words
5 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A REPORT OF ROBUST April chain-store sales, combined with a sudden slump in oil prices, helped push
the Dow Jones Industrial Average to another six-year high.
The blue-chip average edged up 38.58 points, or 0.34%, to 11438.86. It now is up 6.7% for the year, less
than 300 points from its record finish of 11722.98 on Jan. 14, 2000.
For the second day in a row, oil futures fell more than $2, declining $2.34 to $69.94 a barrel as worries about
summer gasoline shortages faded. The 6.3% two-day percentage loss was the largest in a year.
Stocks of transportation companies, which are heavy oil users, surged to a second consecutive record finish.
The Dow Jones Transportation Average jumped 3.34%, or 158.51 points, to 4908.24. It was the 20-stock
transportation average's largest percentage gain since last July.
"The big thing was crude losing more than $2 and going back below $70 a barrel," said Todd Clark of San
Francisco brokerage firm Nollenberger Capital Partners. Together with the strong retail-sales numbers, that
boosted confidence in the economy. "Everybody has been so afraid that these high gasoline prices are going
to crimp retail sales. The data suggested that that hasn't happened."
Reinforcing the optimism, a government report showed that first-quarter labor productivity, or output per hour
worked, was stronger than expected. That measure of economic efficiency bodes well for corporate profits.
Unit labor costs were higher than expected, which raises worries about future inflation, but that didn't
outweigh the optimism.
Fear that inflationary pressures could push interest rates higher helped send bond prices down, however,
nudging the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to another four-year high, 5.152%.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.87%, or 19.93 points, to 2323.90, up 5.4% in 2006.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.34%, or 4.40 points, to 1312.25, up 5.1% this year. As they
debate whether the Dow will break its record, investors are watching whether the S&P 500 can push past the
five-year high of 1313.21 hit Tuesday.
Gold futures jumped $8.30 to $674.20 a troy ounce, another 25-year high. The dollar hit another 11-month
low against the euro.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced, bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents for each $1,000
invested. The 30-year bond was down 3/32 to yield 5.238%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 113.64 yen, down from 113.69, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2708 from $1.2624.

English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WEAK PROFIT NUMBERS and worries about a too-strong economy combined to knock the Dow Jones
Industrial Average down from Tuesday's six-year high, but the decline was minor.
The Dow industrials gave up 16.17 points, or 0.14%, to finish at 11400.28, still up 6.4% this year and within
sight of its January 2000 record close of 11722.98.
A pullback in oil futures helped push the 20-stock Dow Jones Transportation Average to another record, up
20.93 points, or 0.44%, at 4749.73. The transports have risen 13% in 2006.
In the broad market, investors were reacting to disappointing quarterly sales at Dow-industrials
component-stock Procter &Gamble, which sent the household-products maker down more than 3%. Time
Warner's sales also missed Wall Street expectations.
In addition, the latest economic reports showed more strength than expected both in the nation's service
sector, which accounts for the great majority of the economy, and in factory orders.
A booming economy is good for business, but it raises worries that the Federal Reserve will push interest
rates higher than investors had hoped. Higher interest rates are bad for stocks because they boost
borrowing costs for companies and individuals alike.
"It was just a general pullback" after the market's robust recent gains, "rather than any kind of aggressive
selloff," said Mike Viracola, head of trading at Boston-based brokerage firm Canaccord Adams.
He said some investors are nervous about chain-store sales figures due out today.
The expectation of higher interest rates also makes existing bonds, with their lower rates, less attractive. So
the strong economic news knocked down bond prices, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up to
5.144%, another four-year high.
The day's big mover was oil futures, which fell $2.33 to $72.28 a barrel. That still isn't far from the April 21
close of $75.17, which was the highest level since crude futures began trading on the New York Mercantile
Exchange in 1983.
Gold futures hit yet another 25-year high, rising $1.20 to $665.90 a troy ounce.
The dollar fell to a fresh 11-month low against the euro.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index retreated 0.41%, or 5.36 points, to 1307.85, still up 4.8% in 2006. The
Nasdaq Composite Index declined 0.25%, or 5.87 points, to 2303.97, a gain of 4.5% on the year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms, though stocks in India set another record high. The Dow
Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, fell 0.54%, or 1.24 points, to 227.57.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.41 billion
shares, as 1,400 stocks rose and 1,880 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.19 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 5.144%. The 30-year bond was down 13/32 to yield 5.232%.
The dollar was mixed. In late New York trading, the currency traded at 113.69 yen, up from 113.26, while the
euro rose against the greenback to $1.2624 from $1.2621.

Stocks Rebound On Solid Profits, Fresh Optimism --- DJIA Hits Another Six-Year High As Continued
Economic Strength Overshadows Interest-Rate Fears
By E.S. Browning
486 words
3 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MONDAY'S INTEREST-RATE angst evaporated, as optimism about corporate profits and economic strength
helped to propel the Dow Jones Industrial Average to another six-year high.
Strong quarterly profit reports from Emerson and Archer-Daniels-Midland Co. helped to take investors' minds
off a Monday report on CNBC suggesting Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke may be ready to raise
interest rates more than investors had hoped. That report helped knock the Dow from a late-day gain of
about 49 points to a closing decline Monday of 23.85 points.
Yesterday, investors seemed to shrug off Monday's late-day surprise, sending the Dow industrials up 73.16
points, or 0.64%, to 11416.45. That was the highest finish since Jan. 19, 2000, a gain of 6.5% this year. The
industrial average now is a little more than 300 points below its record close of 11722.98, hit Jan. 14, 2000.
"Basically, it got us right back to where we were" before the television report, said stock trader Larry Peruzzi
at Boston Company Asset Management. Investors apparently decided the economy's strength will offset the
risk of slightly higher short-term interest rates.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index hit a five-year high, rising 0.61%, or 8.02 points, to 1313.21, up 5.2%
in 2006.
Bond prices also rebounded, recovering the ground they lost after the television report. But while they
returned to the general level at which they stood before the Bernanke surprise, they didn't recover their full
Monday decline, indicating bond investors remain concerned about the risk of higher interest rates. When
investors expect higher rates in the future, they bid down the price of existing bonds, which carry lower rates.
Investors weren't fazed by the continuing sharp rebound in oil futures, which rose 91 cents to $74.61 a barrel,
56 cents shy of the April 21 close of $75.17, the highest level since crude futures began trading on the New
York Mercantile Exchange in 1983.
Gold, which tends to be strong when investors fear inflation or international instability, rose $7.20 to another
25-year high, $664.70 a troy ounce.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.22%, or 5.05 points, to 2309.84, up 4.7% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 5.116%. The 30-year bond was up 12/32 to yield 5.204%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 113.26 yen, up from 113.25, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2621 from $1.2591.

Educating Ben
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Educating Ben
791 words
3 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
`I know you believe you understand what you think I said, but I am not sure you realize that what you heard is
not what I meant."
That quote did not originate with Alan Greenspan, but it is widely attributed to him, perhaps because it so
neatly ("eloquently" is not quite the right word) sums up his knack for obfuscation, especially before
Congress. By now, new Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke probably wishes he'd had that sentence
printed on a business card when CNBC anchor Maria Bartiromo asked him at a dinner over the weekend
whether he thought markets had properly understood him last week.
The Chairman's testimony last Thursday prompted a mild stock rally on the hopes that the Fed believes it can
soon afford to halt its string of interest-rate hikes. Investors always like to believe the end of rate hikes is nigh,
because rising rates create greater financial risks. But stocks went in reverse on mid-afternoon Monday when
Ms. Bartiromo reported that Mr. Bernanke had told her over the weekend that his testimony had been
misinterpreted. To be fair to the new Fed chief, there may be no other job in the world where a handful of
words uttered at a dinner party can count for so much.
However, the real issue is less Mr. Bernanke's dinner-party discretion, or Ms. Bartiromo's, than the Fed's
policy intentions. And one reason for market disquiet is uncertainty about how seriously the Fed is taking the
risk of incipient inflation. Any new Fed chief has first and foremost to establish his credibility in maintaining
the soundness of the currency, and the last few days suggest Mr. Bernanke has some work to do in that
department.
In the wake of his "dovish" remarks last week, the dollar continued its fall to a new recent low -- even against
the Canadian dollar. Gold and commodity prices kept rising, and oil staged another charge above $70 a
barrel. Even the TIPS spread on inflation-linked bonds widened. In other words, inflationary expectations
staged a rally of their own.
This is not the sort of rally Mr. Bernanke needs, as he himself sometimes seems to appreciate. "[I]nflation
expectations will remain low only so long as the Federal Reserve demonstrates its commitment to price
stability," the Fed chief also told Congress last week. The market reaction to his testimony focused on a
single line that seemed to suggest a pause in the Fed's rate-hiking campaign.
A careful reading of his testimony suggests, in fact, that Mr. Bernanke was trying to tell Congress that a
potential pause in rate hikes in June would not signal an end to the cycle, after an expected 25-basis-point
hike in May. But the markets focused on his apparent conviction that, although there were "risks" to the
inflation outlook, inflationary pressures remained "well-contained," as well as his remarks about a possible
softening of the economy in the second half of the year.
However, that "softening" has been predicted to be one or two quarters away practically since the Fed began
hiking rates in 2004. It still hasn't materialized. Last week the preliminary GDP-growth estimate for the first
quarter came in at 4.8% -- its best quarterly showing since 2003 -- and most inflation indicators have been
quietly climbing for several months now.
This evidence has prompted Bear Stearns' Chief Economist, David Malpass, and others to suggest that the
Fed has been "chasing neutral." The Fed's continuing accommodation, in other words, has meant that a
"neutral" level of interest rates has been slowly climbing, leaving the Fed running to stand still. Recall that
before Mr. Bernanke took office, the debate was whether he would raise rates once before pausing, just for
appearances' sake, or call off the rate hikes to keep the economy from tanking. Now the question is whether
the pause is coming after two hikes or three.
The danger here is that the longer the Fed waits to break inflationary expectations, the higher the Fed will
eventually have to take rates -- and the greater the potential financial damage. Economists at the Fed and on

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Wall Street have both vastly underestimated the strength of this expansion, in part because they missed the
incentive power of the 2003 tax cuts. They have in turn kept monetary policy too easy for too long, as real
price signals have been showing.
Dinner-party gaffes will happen, but Mr. Bernanke's bigger mistake would be to let the impression sink in that
he's an easy-money guy. The last Fed chief who allowed that to happen was run out of town on a rail in favor
of Paul Volcker.

India's Cement Makers Shine --- Building Spree, Rise in Demand and Share Price Likely Will Go On
World Stock Markets
India's Cement Makers Shine --- Building Spree, Rise in Demand and Share Price Likely Will Go On
By Binny Sabharwal
976 words
3 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
New Delhi -- A SPATE OF new roads, shopping malls and office buildings is transforming India's biggest
cities and generating huge demand for the basic building block of urban makeovers: cement.
With India's unprecedented construction spree likely to continue and cement supplies tight, analysts say
cement shares -- already up sharply this year -- might still have room to rise, as Mumbai's stock market
touches new highs in volatile trading. Yesterday, the Bombay Stock Exchange's Sensitive Index rose 176.22
points, or 1.5%, to a record 12218.78, as a ruling by the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia lifted
two of the country's largest generic-drug makers.
"Infrastructure growth is happening everywhere. The housing sector is on fire," said Bhavesh Shah, vice
president for research of Mumbai-based Asit C. Mehta Investment Intermediates. With manufacturers
straining to produce enough cement, prices and cement-company profits are climbing.
Some analysts caution that, if rising costs threaten to cause budget overruns, government construction
projects could be postponed, slowing cement demand. And an increase in interest rates could squeeze
housing sales.
Yesterday, the Indian government asked cement companies to find ways to reduce cement prices, in a bid to
soften the impact on the economy. "We think that the nearly 50% increase in cement prices since November
2005 is abnormal," Ajay Dua, secretary of the Department of Industrial Policy and Promotion, told reporters
after a meeting with cement manufacturers and the Builders' Association of India. Cement manufacturers said
significant increases in input costs justify higher prices.
Longer term, India's cement market, now the world's second-largest after China's, is bound to get bigger,
industry watchers say. In the first three months of 2006, Indian demand increased 11% from the year-earlier
period to 141.5 million metric tons. Infrastructure projects consume about 25% of India's cement output, while
property developers use about 60%.
Unlike China, India has relatively few manufacturers, so cement supplies are tight. India's four dozen or so
cement makers, now operating at an average capacity of 91%, can't meet rising demand.
Even as companies rush to add capacity, analysts expect a supply shortfall at least until 2008, when some
new plants begin production.
"The fact that the industry is operating at a near-full capacity has given good pricing power to cement
companies," said Rajan Kumar, research analyst at Networth Stock Broking in Mumbai.
The company best placed to cash in on the boom may be India's biggest producer, Grasim Industries, which
owns a 51% stake in listed UltraTech Cement. Plants of Grasim, which is one of the world's 10 top producers
by output, are located to exploit hotbeds of economic activity. The company's combined annual production
capacity is close to 32 million tons, including 17 million tons by UltraTech.
Shares of Grasim, which closed yesterday at 2,469.65 rupees ($55.15), have skyrocketed 78% so far this
year.
Grasim shares trade at 16.5 times projected earnings for the current fiscal year. Rivals Associated Cement,
or ACC, and Gujarat Ambuja Cements trade at about 20 times projected earnings.
ACC shares have risen 85% since the end of 2005. They closed at 989.40 rupees yesterday. Gujarat Ambuja
ended yesterday at 122 rupees -- 53% higher than at the start of 2006. Gujarat Ambuja doesn't have much
scope for expansion because it is operating at full capacity. ACC, the country's second-biggest producer,

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 211 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
might see growth slow by 2008 because its stronghold is in northern India, while most new demand is coming
from the country's southern regions.
---
Yesterday's Market Activity
European stocks jumped, propelled by a bounce in mining and oil shares as commodity prices rallied, while
upbeat corporate earnings also drove gains.
Asian stock markets ended mostly higher, with Japanese stocks gaining as the dollar strengthened to the 113
yen level. China shares listed in Hong Kong were up sharply as investors judged last week's selloff to be
overdone.
In TOKYO, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies gained 228.06 points, or 1.4%, to 17153.77,
lifted by buying in export and high-tech issues. Honda Motor added 3.5%, carrying momentum from its record
profit announcement last week and benefiting from the yen's pullback against the dollar. Toyota Motor added
1.6% after the Yomiuri Shimbuna newspaper reported the country's top auto maker was planning to raise its
dividend. The market will be closed the rest of the week for the Golden Week holiday.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 added 59.00 points, or 0.98%, to 6082.10. Miners Rio Tinto, Antofagasta and
BHP Billiton each rose more than 3% as copper and zinc prices traded up 3% on the London Metal Exchange
and gold hit a 25-year high. BP, Royal Dutch Shell and Cairn Energy each gained more than 1% as oil
headed back above $74 a barrel. BAE Systems rose on deal news.

Stocks Fall on Report Fed Chief Meant Rate Increases May Pause, Not End
Monday's Markets
Stocks Fall on Report Fed Chief Meant Rate Increases May Pause, Not End
By E.S. Browning
653 words
2 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER SPENDING most of the day in positive territory, stocks suddenly slumped after a late-day television
report that Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke isn't as dovish on interest rates as investors had
believed.
CNBC anchor Maria Bartiromo reported that she had seen Mr. Bernanke at the White House Correspondents
Dinner during the weekend, and that he said investors misinterpreted his congressional testimony Thursday
as a signal that the Fed is almost done raising rates. Ms. Bartiromo said Mr. Bernanke told her that the Fed is
trying to be "flexible" in its response to economic data, and that it might pause in its rate increases and then
raise rates again later. A Federal Reserve representative declined to comment.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which was showing a gain of about 49 points when Ms. Bartiromo spoke,
finished down 23.85 points, or 0.21%, at 11343.29. The Dow still is up 5.8% this year, and is less than 400
points below its January 2000 record finish of 11722.98.
Bond prices, already down, fell further on the report, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note to a
four-year high.
"If he really did say the market misinterpreted his comments last Thursday, you have to expect the Fed to be
more aggressive" in raising interest rates in the future, said Jay Suskind, director of trading at brokerage firm
Ryan Beck in Florham Park, N.J. Higher rates make life more expensive for businesses and consumers,
which can be bad for stocks.
Thursday, Mr. Bernanke told Congress the Fed could take a break from rate increases at some future
meeting, in order to analyze data. That was widely taken as a signal that, after one further increase in its
target for short-term rates at its next policy meeting, May 10, the Fed could stop its long-running rate-increase
campaign in June. Now investors fear Mr. Bernanke is sending the message that he could raise rates more.
They also worry that they were wrong in thinking that his comments would be easy to understand.
"His whole thing was that he was going to try to be more visible and transparent than Greenspan was, and
less opaque," Mr. Suskind said.
Earlier in the day, with interest-rate increases seemingly not a worry, investors had applauded Wal-Mart
Stores' report of robust April sales, as well as a series of strong economic reports on manufacturing growth,
construction spending and consumer spending. They had been shrugging off a surge in crude-oil futures,
which finished the day up $1.82 at $73.70 a barrel. Now they worry that a strong economy could provoke
more rate increases.
Gold finished the day at a 25-year high, up $5.70 at $657.50 a troy ounce.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.41%, or 5.42 points, to 1305.19, up 4.6% this year. The Nasdaq

Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.63%, or 1.43 points, to a record 226.73.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.41 billion

Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 18/32, or $5.625 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year
bond was down 28/32 to yield 5.230%.

Land of the Rising Dividends --- Quarterly Payouts Could Lure More Investors, Boost Share Prices
World Stock Markets
Land of the Rising Dividends --- Quarterly Payouts Could Lure More Investors, Boost Share Prices
By Yuka Hayashi
888 words
2 May 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tokyo -- SOME JAPANESE companies soon will be sharing their riches with investors more frequently, which
could make them more attractive to buyers and possibly boost their stock prices.
The cue is Japan's Corporate Law, which took effect yesterday and grants companies freedom to set the
timing and frequency of their dividend payments. There are signs that some companies will start paying
dividends every quarter, a practice common in the U.S. and Europe. Until now, Japanese companies had
been allowed to pay dividends twice a year.
The change could be good news for investors in two ways. First is the shorter interval between payouts.
Second, quarterly dividends could spread the appeal of share ownership to a wider group of individuals,
which would bring new money into the market, in turn supporting prices.
"Japan, for such a long time, had the lowest dividend yield and dividend-payout ratio" among major markets,
said Patrick Mohr, a strategist at Nikko Citigroup in Tokyo. "That is beginning to change."
Mr. Mohr estimates that Japan's publicly traded companies will make a record 5.5 trillion yen, or $48 billion, in
dividend payouts for the year that ended March 31. For the year ended March 2005, dividend payouts came
to 4.6 trillion yen, and payments totaled 3.7 trillion yen a year earlier.
Higher dividends are among a number of shareholder-friendly steps that Japanese companies have taken in
recent years. One reason is a change in Japan's shareholder base. During the country's long economic
slump, banks and other strategic corporate shareholders needing to raise cash sold off chunks of the
stockholdings they had in business partners. To appeal to new types of shareholders and fend off hostile
takeovers, companies also bought back more shares, beefed up disclosures and improved corporate
governance.
Still, the average dividend yield -- the amount of the dividend paid divided by the share price -- for companies
listed on the Tokyo Stock Exchange's First Section for large-cap stocks was 0.98% last year, below the
1.86% for the S&P 500-stock index.
Many Japanese investors are in, or nearing, retirement and have shown a penchant for financial assets that
pay frequent dividends. Mutual funds that invest in foreign bonds and pay monthly dividends have been a hit.
Many mutual funds targeting stocks that pay generous dividends also have been launched in the past year,
helping to bring many investors back into stocks.
Such new investors could help support Japanese stock prices if the central bank begins an expected series
of interest-rate increases later in the year. The Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies has gained 5.1%
since the beginning of 2006, on top of a 40% jump in 2005. Dividends and share buybacks could be "an
important factor in share-price performance as this current market rally matures, and, perhaps, stumbles,"
said Gregory Jones, head of Asia investing at Clay Finlay, a New York stock-investment firm.
Riso Kyoiku, which operates "cram schools" to prepare students for examinations, on April 3 announced a
plan to pay quarterly dividends. The next day, its stock jumped 4.3%, and between April 3 and April 28, it
gained 43%. Brokerage firm Nikko Cordial also saw its share price rise after announcing quarterly dividend
plans.
Among those that say they hope to move to quarterly dividends is optical-products maker Hoya. Shareholders
of electronic-parts maker Sumida in March approved an internal rule change that allows quarterly dividends
as soon as August.

Microsoft Woes Hurt Dow
Friday's Markets
Microsoft Woes Hurt Dow
By Peter A. McKay
491 words
29 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PLENTY OF WALL STREET pros have been sounding like someone's mother, warning investors to get ready
for a rainy day, even though lately it has been mostly sunny for stocks.
Generally strong growth in corporate earnings and the broader economy have allowed investors to look
beyond potentially portfolio-damaging factors like soaring prices for oil and other commodities. Indeed, Friday
the Commerce Department reported that first-quarter gross domestic product, the broadest measure of the
U.S. output of goods and services, jumped 4.8% on an annual basis, the biggest improvement in three years.
Stocks' performance Friday, however, keyed off a specific piece of corporate news: the news from Microsoft
Corp. that it is having to spend more to develop and maintain its products. Shares of Microsoft -- a
component of the Dow Jones Industrial Average and other stock indexes -- fell 11% -- its biggest one-day
loss in more than five years.
The Dow overall slipped 15.37 points, or 0.14%, to 11367.14, up slightly on the week and hovering near a
six-year high. For the month of April, the Dow gained 2.3%; it has risen four straight months for the first time
since 2003.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index nudged up 0.07%, or 0.89 point, to 1310.61, off 0.1% for the week.
The tech-heavy Nasdaq Composite Index slid 0.95%, or 22.38 points, to 2322.57, off 0.9% on the week.
Strategists and economists say the economy is due to slow down soon, if it isn't doing so already. The good
news for investors: Slower growth will discourage the Federal Reserve from continuing to raise interest rates,
which the central bank has been doing for nearly two years to ward off the risk of inflation.
"You have to take any GDP report with a grain of salt, because it's looking backward at things that have
already happened over the last three months," said Lewis Alexander, chief economist at Citigroup. "But,
looking ahead, the signs we see for slower growth are still in place."
Among other markets Friday, commodity prices continued to climb. Silver jumped 8.3%, or $1.04, to $13.51
an ounce on Comex, the largest percentage gain since March 1995. Gold rose to $651.80 an ounce, helped
by a weaker dollar. Crude-oil futures rose 91 cents, or 1.3%, to $71.88 a barrel on political fears, but were
down 4.4% on the week.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note gained 3/32, or 93.75 cents for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield down to 5.067%. The 30-year Treasury bond rose 3/32, yielding 5.168%.
The dollar weakened, to 113.86 yen, down from 114.12. The euro rose to $1.2616 from $1.2534.

The Week in Review
The Week in Review
697 words
29 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Pointing the Finger
Washington responded to public outrage over high energy costs even as reports of record profits continued to
roll out of Big Oil. Measures backed by President Bush and Senate Republicans would set higher standards
for passenger-car fuel economy, curb tax incentives for oil companies and even offer consumers a $100 tax
rebate. They also propose incentives for increasing refining capacity. Whether action results from these
public announcements -- Bush spoke during a visit to a Mississippi gas station -- isn't certain; some say it's
merely election-year posturing. But the timing can't be faulted. As the public chewed on the proposals, these
first-quarter profit reports came out: Exxon Mobil, $8.4 billion; Chevron, $4 billion; ConocoPhillips, $3.3 billion.
Tough Audience
Bush's approval rating fell to a record-low 36%, but he wasn't singled out. In fact, as WSJ/NBC pollster Peter
Hart said, "There's almost nothing that the public is satisfied with." Congress got a 22% rating, Democrats
33% and Republicans 35%. And 77% of respondents called themselves uneasy about the U.S. economy --
even as new-home sales, durable-goods orders and other indicators show growth. Many Americans feel they
are missing out on the good times, with gas prices, health-care costs and rising interest rates applying the
squeeze. And the Iraq war remains widely unpopular. The message? "What they're telling you," said Hart, "is
they want change on every front."
Let Freedom Rise
More than four years after 9/11, construction trucks began rolling at ground zero. A deal hashed out between
the developer and local officials calls for the five-tower project to be completed by 2012, joining a memorial
and transit hub scheduled for 2009. The government agency that owns the World Trade Center site will build
the 1,776-foot-high Freedom Tower, the costly centerpiece. Its design has changed for safety reasons, with
blast-resistant titanium replacing twisting glass, but critics maintain it could still be a terror target. Then there's
the office-space question: If they build it, will tenants fill it? Officials say nearly half the skyscraper could be
occupied with government leases.
A New Golden Age
The most precious metal continued to shine, with a variety of factors driving gold futures to 25-year highs.
Mideast tensions, a weak dollar and oil-driven inflation have highlighted its role as safe haven. Then there's
wedding season under way in India, the world's biggest consumer of gold jewelry. But an increasing appetite
for risk is also driving investors. AIM Investments, for example, reports weekly flows into its Gold &Precious
Metals fund are up 250% in 2006. It's looking like a smart bet now, but -- as veteran gold bugs know -- the
stuff is notoriously volatile: since 1993, the S&P gold index rose 83% in its best year and fell 34% in its worst.
No Escape From Reality
Microsoft, in its search for revenue in nontraditional media, wants to invite advertisers into your fantasy world.
The tech titan is in talks to acquire Massive Inc., a start-up that places ads in videogames. With clients
including Coca-Cola and Honda, Massive transmits ads over the Internet and inserts them on billboards, soda
cans and other on-screen elements of videogames. This allows advertisers to target and update ads, and
even show specific ones during selected times and sections of the country. So far, Massive mainly delivers
ads into games for PCs, but Microsoft is eyeing the much larger market for videogame consoles like its Xbox
360.

reported a sharp rise in first-quarter earnings, aided by labor efficiencies as well as higher sales of several
products.

Fed Testimony Helps Stocks, Hurts Dollar --- Treasury Bonds Also Rise Slightly On Possible Pause in Rate Hikes; Investors Had Anticipated...
Thursday's Markets
Fed Testimony Helps Stocks, Hurts Dollar --- Treasury Bonds Also Rise Slightly On Possible Pause in
Rate Hikes; Investors Had Anticipated News
By E.S. Browning
497 words
28 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE NEW FEDERAL RESERVE chief finally gave investors the news they have been waiting for, but aside
from another drop in the dollar, market reaction was mild.
Stocks and bonds posted moderate gains after Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke told a congressional committee
the Fed could take a break in its campaign to raise interest rates, even if inflation concerns aren't entirely
assuaged. The dollar fell to a seven-month low against the euro and a six-month low against the yen. Higher
rates make dollars more attractive, so an end to rate increases would hurt the dollar.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average edged up 28.02 points, or 0.25%, to 11382.51, another six-year high. It is
up 6.2% this year, less than 350 points from its record close of 11722.98 on Jan. 14, 2000.
"It seems from what Bernanke was saying that they want to pause" rate increases, and "that is what people
were hoping for," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at brokerage firm Cowen &Co.
Stocks didn't react more strongly, he said, because investors had bid them up for days in anticipation of such
news. Last week's release of the minutes of an earlier Fed meeting suggested the Fed wanted to stop raising
rates. And Mr. Bernanke made no promises. He indicated that any pause in the rate-increase campaign that
began in June 2004 would depend on whether data show signs of inflationary pressure. Markets still expect
the Fed to raise its target short-term rate another quarter-point to 5% at its May 10 meeting. So the first hope
for a pause would be at the June meeting. Mr. Bernanke also suggested the Fed could resume rate increases
later.
In addition, investors were nervous about China's announcement before U.S. markets opened that it would
boost interest rates. U.S. investors fear that China wants to slow its growth, which has helped drive world
economic expansion. Stocks of industrial-commodity producers declined. Copper maker Phelps Dodge and
aluminum maker Alcoa each fell more than 4%. Gold and oil futures declined as well.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.33%, or 4.31 points, to 1309.72, up 4.9% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.49%, or 11.32 points, to 2344.95, ahead 6.3% in 2006.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 7/32, or $2.19 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 5.079%. The 30-year bond was up 2/32 to yield 5.175%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 114.12 yen, down from 114.73, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2534 from $1.2454.

China Rate Rise Damps Markets On Fears of Decrease in Demand
Commodities Report
China Rate Rise Damps Markets On Fears of Decrease in Demand
By Tom Sellen and Peter A. McKay
373 words
28 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
China's surprise rate increase pushed commodity markets lower, as investors worried higher borrowing costs
in a country that has been a voracious consumer of raw materials could damp demand for copper and other
natural resources.
The drop in prices wasn't severe, particularly in light of the commodity markets' usual volatility. On the New
York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division, nearby May-delivery gold fell $5.70 an ounce to $633.30,
nearby May silver fell 34.5 cents an ounce to $12.47 an ounce and nearby May copper fell 13.50 cents to
$3.26 a pound.
Precious and base metals turned higher briefly as investors sought bargains, but the Chinese rate move and
news that the Securities and Exchange Commission approved a long-awaited silver exchange-traded fund,
Barclays Global Investors' iShares Silver Trust, ultimately pushed metals lower.
Analysts said the commodity selloff could be short-lived.
Byron Wien, hedge fund Pequot Capital's chief investment strategist, said one reason the commodities
markets didn't react more strongly to China's actions was it is still unclear whether the relatively small
interest-rate adjustment is a one-time move or the start of more. "If China is going to try to deal with its
currency valuation problem by raising interest rates, and this is the first of many steps, then I think it could
have significance for the commodities markets," he said. "But if this is just a gesture, but not a policy change .
. . I don't think it will have much impact."
China made a similar rate move in late 2004, also raising fears then of a negative impact on commodities.
"We have this previous precedent, and it didn't do much [then]," said Bill Nelson, associate vice president at
A.G. Edwards.
This time, China's rate rises are unlikely to be large enough to discourage construction of new big-ticket
projects already in the works, said Jeffrey Currie, head of commodities research at Goldman Sachs. Another
supporting factor for metals prices: the possibility that U.S. interest rates will soon level off.

Ann Davis contributed to this article.

Dow Reaches A Six-Year High But Bonds Fall
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Reaches A Six-Year High But Bonds Fall
By E.S. Browning
515 words
27 April 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS GOT another dose of the strong economic reports that ignited inflation fears and knocked
stocks down on Tuesday, but yesterday stocks advanced, sending the Dow Jones Industrial Average to a
six-year high.
The bond market, however, continued to suffer from the inflation concerns, and falling bond prices pushed
the yield of the 10-year Treasury note to another four-year high, 5.107%.
Stock investors seemed to focus more on a parade of strong quarterly profit reports from the likes of PepsiCo
and Colgate-Palmolive. The Dow got an extra boost from Merrill Lynch's upgrade of General Motors, a Dow
component, which rose almost 8%. The blue-chip average rose 71.24 points, or 0.63%, to 11354.49, its
highest finish since 2000. It is up 5.9%, within 369 points of its record close of 11722.98, on Jan. 14, 2000.
"The market is obviously very earnings-driven right now," said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San
Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners. But he added that rising bond yields reflect fears that the
Federal Reserve will try to cool the economy by raising interest rates more than had been expected, which
would be bad for stocks and bonds.
The day's economic reports showed durable-goods orders and new-home sales both surging in March,
reinforcing Tuesday's strong reports on consumer confidence and sales of previously occupied homes. Home
prices did show signs of moderating, however.
The Fed's "beige book" economic report cited solid economic growth and pointed to rising materials and labor
costs for businesses. But it also said companies were having trouble raising consumer prices.
Crude oil futures continued to decline after hitting $75.17 a barrel on Friday, their highest level since New
York's Nymex began trading oil futures in 1983. Crude fell 95 cents to $71.93.
Gold futures, which some analysts use as a gauge of inflation fears, rose to a 25-year high, up $7.80 to
$638.80 a troy ounce.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.28%, or 3.67 points, to 1305.41, up 4.6% this year but not back
to its recent highs. The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.14%, or 3.33 points, to 2333.63, up 5.8% in
2006.
Today, investors will be watching congressional testimony from Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke for his take on
inflation and interest rates.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.48 billion shares,
as 1,832 stocks rose and 1,471 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year
bond was down 5/32 to yield 5.178%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 114.73 yen, down from 114.82, while the euro
rose against the greenback to $1.2454 from $1.2432.
